Preface 
This master thesis, concerning a trade-off between indoor environmental quality and energy 
consumption in modern buildings, has been developed in order to fulfil the requirements to 
obtain a master degree in the field of Architectural Engineering at DTU Department of Civil 
Engineering. The master thesis is written at the Department of Civil Engineering at the 
Technical University of Denmark in the period 4th January to 4th June 2016 by Ana Tisov. The 
work load corresponds to 30 ECTS.

==PAGE:9==
V 
 
Abstract 
Energy efficiency in buildings should not be reached at the expense of indoor environmental 
quality (IEQ). This statement is often used in connection with the design and certification of 
sustainable buildings. However, when buildings are in the operation stage, this often seems to 
be forgotten. The fact is that modern buildings are not spared from IEQ related problems and 
this indicates that there is a need for joint assessments of energy and IEQ performance.  
The development of such key figures which would enable a simultaneous analysis of energy and 
IEQ performance for modern buildings was the main goal of the thesis. The development of this 
type of key figures was done according to the background studies. A literature review served as 
a basis for gathering information about the energy and IEQ performance indicators commonly 
used today, their evaluation techniques (requirements and recommendations) and tools for 
data analysis. No appropriate methodology or key figures, which would address joint 
assessment of the energy consumption and IEQ in buildings, were found. Hence, new key figures 
were developed according to the thesis objectives and were tested on data obtained from 
dynamic simulation models in order to produce usable key figures that enable such joint 
assessments.  
The practical usability of the developed key figures was demonstrated on building management 
system (BMS) data from existing commercial buildings where two different groups of buildings 
were used. The first group included analyses of five buildings where the aim was to test 
whether such key figures can be used for studying trends for a large number of buildings. The 
second group included a joint assessment analysis for a single commercial building where it was 
tested whether the key figures can be used as a tool for continuous commissioning during a 
building’s life cycle. 
The developed key figures showed the possibility of a joint assessment of energy consumption 
and IEQ where a correlation between heating, ventilation and air conditioning (HVAC) energy 
consumption and measured IEQ parameters (operative temperature and CO2 concentrations) 
was graphically presented on the same plot. Validation of the developed key figures on the BMS 
data showed which conditions need to be fulfilled before carrying out joint assessment analyses 
in order to conduct accurate analyses and lead to meaningful findings. A sufficient building 
information and data readings for the investigated buildings are required. It was discovered 
that providing information on a single key figure can contribute to a quick and efficient year-
round evaluation of a building’s energy performance as well as IEQ and can be used to study 
trends for a large number of buildings. Development and integration of the developed key 
figures with a building’s intelligent smart system could enhance the effectiveness and practical 
usability of such joint assessment analyses further on.

1 Introduction 
1 
1 Introduction 
Energy consumption in buildings has been significantly increased in the last half century 
throughout the whole world mainly due to economic developments and growing living 
standards [1]. This raises concern about the energy consumption in buildings, which are a 
substantial user of energy worldwide. Buildings themselves account for around 40 % of total 
energy use which makes the building sector the greatest energy consumer around the world [2]. 
Therefore, the design of energy efficient buildings and improvements in the energy 
performance of existing buildings are becoming of vital importance. Buildings are becoming 
more air-tight and with more advanced building service systems [3]. Where, during the 
building’s design and operation phase, energy efficiency is one of the top priorities, indoor 
environment evaluation is often done superficially. Many times a question arises whether these 
implemented design changes have lead to both an improved building’s energy performance and 
to better indoor environment for the occupants at the same time. Energy efficiency of such 
buildings sets a good example and satisfies the recommendations of the new Energy 
Performance of Buildings Directive (EPBD) Directive [3]. However, most often in first years of 
building’s operation, an aggravated indoor environment is reported by occupants.  
On the other hand, there are buildings where the well-being of occupants is a main priority of 
the employer and this leads to a constant supply of uniformly conditioned zones which leads to 
high energy consumptions. Many studies have proven that good indoor environmental quality 
(IEQ) can significantly increase employee productivity and health [4]. Companies aim to provide 
good thermal comfort and air quality for the occupants which result in the spread of heating, 
ventilation, and air conditioning (HVAC) installations and having sufficient ventilation rates. 
Nowadays, in developed countries and for commercial buildings, HVAC has become the largest 
energy end-use, accounting for about half the building’s energy consumption [2]. If the 
operation of these systems is not optimized this leads to vast energy-end use. 
In recent years, advanced information technology development has led to the development and 
increased application of building management systems (BMS) in buildings, which are expected 
to be installed in almost all buildings in coming years [5]. Currently, the recorded BMS data is 
most commonly used for energy performance evaluation [6]. The fact that energy performance 
affects indoor environment and indoor environment affects energy performance of buildings 
indicates that there is a need for joint assessment of energy and IEQ performance. Furthermore, 
BMS provide a valuable possibility of monitoring both indoor environment and energy 
consumption; and can be used for optimization of a building’s total performance.  
The primary goal of this thesis project was to develop key figures that enable such joint 
assessments of energy and IEQ performance indicators by detailed analysis of measured BMS 
data collected for existing commercial buildings. 
1.1 General thesis objectives 
There were two main objectives of this thesis. The first objective was to identify key figures that 
enable simultaneous analysis and therefore joint assessment of energy consumption and indoor 
environmental quality parameters. The second objective of the project was to validate the 
suggested key figures on data collected from existing buildings.  
 
==PAGE:16==
2 Background study 
2 
 
2 Background study 
The aim of the background study was to review relevant literature to form a background for 
development of key figures enabling the joint assessment of energy and IEQ. The review 
resulted in fifty cited publications. The main idea was to find out whether there exist any key 
figures where energy and indoor climate performance indicator are presented simultaneously 
on a graphical plot. The research showed that read articles could be fitted into four main areas 
according to their main topic: Energy Management, Commissioning, Operation Diagnostics and 
Indoor Environmental Quality studies. Reviewed case studies assisted in identifying the existing 
energy and IEQ parameters, most commonly used in practice. The chapter also provides a 
description on current building regulations regarding energy performance and indoor quality 
requirements. Furthermore, it was investigated which tools are commonly used in practice for 
big data analysis obtained from the BMS. The chapter is closed with a discussion which opened 
certain research questions. This served as a basis for brainstorming where ideas for new 
potential key figures for joint assessment of energy and IEQ performance indicators were found. 
This background study also served as a basis to set the thesis disposition which is presented 
after this chapter.  
The literature review was conducted using the Web of Science database accessed via license of 
the Technical University of Denmark. The following keywords were used for a primary search 
(the keywords were consequently used in different combinations, combinations used in the 
primary search are also listed): operation diagnostics, building operation optimization, energy 
performance, commissioning, commercial building*, energy management, thermal comfort 
[AND] field study, indoor environmental quality [AND] energy performance, data management 
[AND] BMS system* [AND] analysis, visualization tool*, energy use [AND] climate, degree-days 
method, EN 15251, simulation model*, performance metrics* [OR] indicator*, regression 
analysis. 
Furthermore, based on this literature review a paper was published which was presented at the 
12th REHVA World Congress: CLIMA 2016 (22nd–25th May 2016 in Aalborg, Denmark). The 
paper “Key figures for joint assessment of indoor environmental quality (IEQ) and energy 
consumption in modern buildings – a literature review” is presented in Appendix A.  
2.1 Specific areas 
In this chapter, an overview of the findings found for specific areas is presented. The aim of this 
paragraph is to provide the reader with a general overview of the area topics where the 
performance indicators for energy consumption and IEQ evaluation are mostly used.  
2.1.1 Energy management 
Current building regulations and energy initiatives mostly promote the importance of energy 
savings in buildings. The energy management and monitoring of a building’s energy 
performance is therefore an important part of building management. National building codes 
are implementing the EPBD [3] directive by introducing energy rating schemes for new and 
existing buildings. 
==PAGE:17==
2 Background study 
3 
 
The designed building’s energy consumption is estimated mostly by using simulation models [7, 
8, 9, 10]. Publications by Lam et al. [1], Zhao and Magoules [9] and Catalina et al. [11] present 
further methods for building energy prediction. These include a statistical (regression) method 
[1, 11], use of support vector machines, grey box modelling and the application of neural 
networks [9]. In the aforementioned publications the models were mostly used for research 
purposes. In everyday energy management, the well established degree-days (DD) method [12, 
13] and the bin method [14] are used for forecasting and comparison of energy consumption 
through different years. 
For existing buildings, building managers use different methods and tools depending on their 
expertise, knowledge and background. Input data of the analyses solely depend on the 
building’s systems reading: BMS [15] or energy information system (EIS) [7]. In several 
publications a comparison of measured and simulated energy consumption was conducted 
based on monthly values to identify reasons for aggravated performance indicated by 
measurements [11, 16–18]. IEA Annex 40 [19] presented several case studies from Europe 
using same method. 
As seen from the case studies published by Carling et al. and Baumann [20–22], systems in new 
buildings mostly meet the building's function and respond to the changes of zone environment 
(internal gains and weather dependant loads). Hence, the information on why such changes 
occurred is rarely available to the building analyst without deep and time consuming analysis. 
Such analyses often require export of logged data and their time consuming processing by 
means of an additional analytical tool. Therefore, the detailed accuracy of the interpreted data 
can vary from one building to another and makes it hard to do a reliable comparison [7]. 
As given as an example by O’Donnell [7], building managers compare a building’s annual gross 
energy consumption with the previous year’s performances or with the normalized data from 
similar buildings. As discussed in a report produced by the “Building EQ” project [3] it differs 
from country to country how to present a buildings energy use. In order to make a comparison 
of the annual energy use between the different buildings, the authors of the report expose 
several prerequisites that have to be fulfilled. Authors of the article address the issue of a 
building-to-building comparison by introducing standardised performance metrics. They 
emphasize that the analyst doing such comparisons and using the performance metrics has to 
be aware how the energy consumption is expressed and which area terms were used. 
2.1.2 Commissioning 
As the authors of the report IEA Annex 40 [19] argue, the commissioning process is often 
applied only when a building is taken into operation. Moreover, according to the authors, 
commissioning should be seen through the whole life cycle of a building by implementing the 
four types of commissioning: initial, retro-, re- and on-going. If the commissioning process is 
performed in such a structured manner, the gaps between the four different aspects can be 
bridged, like for example expectations of the owner and current performance of the building 
[19]. It is also important that building operators who are responsible for building and system 
operation are part of the commissioning process in order to maintain the operation of the 
building as intended during the design phase [19]. Furthermore, on-going commissioning 
==PAGE:18==
2 Background study 
4 
 
process is highly emphasized by ASHRAE and REHVA as an important step in order to achieve 
good operation of a building through its life cycle [14]. 
Dynamic simulation models have the potential to be used as a part of commissioning [7, 8, 16]. 
Deviations between the design simulation and the calibrated simulation of the current situation 
can be used to indicate the need for commissioning measures to restore the building to the 
design intent [16]. Differences of model output and measurement may be used as indicators for 
model or measurement errors, fault detection and diagnostics and therefore in support of 
commissioning [16, 20, 21]. As could be observed from case studies [20, 21], simulations helped 
to detect the faults in the HVAC system through the verification of the performance of a 
technical solution. 
2.1.3 Operation diagnostics 
Baumann [22] specifies operation diagnostics as a process of inspecting building and system 
operations in existing buildings in order to reduce energy consumption while still maintaining 
thermal comfort. The aim of operation diagnostics is to find the faults of a building’s poor 
operation through BMS data analysis and suggest the optimization measures. By data analysis 
and by their visualization the optimization potential is identified. In the end, measures on how 
to establish an optimized operation are proposed [22]. 
When analysing case studies by Baumann [22, 23] it was discovered that the measured HVAC 
data provides high-frequency data readings where building managers manually analyse these 
results. Baumann explains that the problem lies in a lack of methods used to assess the data and 
ways to handle these results [22]. As a solution Baumann proposes the automated fault 
detection and diagnostic (FDD) method for the HVAC system. Yu et al. [24] reviews FDD 
methodologies for ventilation systems while Bonvini et al. [25] deals with an algorithm for both 
whole-building and component-level FDD. Katipamula and Brambley [26] proposed an 
automated FDD method for the HVAC system. A single dominant fault in the system can be 
found automatically without manually analysing high amounts of data. A major limitation is that 
if multiple faults occur at the same time, the FDD method fails [22].  
2.1.4 Indoor environmental quality (IEQ) studies 
Indoor environmental quality (IEQ) studies reviewed in relation to building energy 
management would obviously provide valuable input regarding determination of common key 
figures. However, only IEQ studies dealing strictly with analyses of indoor environmental 
conditions were identified where energy consumption was not investigated [27, 28]. Such IEQ 
studies are commonly done when occupants of the building report a complaint to the facility or 
a building manager. If there is a complaint from larger number of occupants or there is an 
obvious sign of aggravated indoor environment (mould, draft, ventilation blowing hot stuffy air 
etc.), detailed IEQ studies are usually done. This normally includes close inspection and 
additional measurements. The outcome of such a survey is most often a recommendation for 
improvements. Therefore, IEQ studies provide valuable information on which IEQ parameters 
should be considered in the case of later joint assessment.  
==PAGE:19==
2 Background study 
5 
 
Research has shown that parameters like CO2, air particles, temperature, ozone, relative 
humidity, noise, illuminance and air velocity (draught) may affect human health and 
productiveness [8, 29-31]. Other important factors influencing IEQ parameters are building 
design, outdoor conditions, indoor contaminant sources, air exchange rates and ventilation 
characteristics [30]. In order to provide occupants with a healthy and stimulating work 
environment and to maintain acceptable indoor air quality (IAQ), indoor environment 
measurements have to be performed [4].  
Furthermore, Frontczak and Wargocki [4] discovered, during an extensive literature survey on 
effect of the indoor environment on human comfort, that thermal comfort is ranked of greater 
importance as compared with visual and acoustic comfort and indoor air quality among building 
occupants. As Wagner et al. [23] discuss, IEQ parameters are not part of everyday building 
manager tasks due to lack of the managers knowledge in human comfort and due to a need for 
extensive measurements. 
Kim and Haberl [32] conducted a comprehensive study of sixteen IEQ parameters based on 
ASHRAE/CIBSE/USGBC Performance Measurement Protocols [33]. High-frequency data were 
presented on a psychrometric chart, which provided effective data visualization. This study was 
only focused on addressing the IEQ of the building and does not deal with energy consumption 
analysis. Portable instruments have to be placed in a building when there is a need for deeper 
analysis, since BMS normally do not provide information on such extensive indoor IEQ data as 
air velocity or air particle concentration. An advanced psychrometric chart does not represent 
common visualization tool used in building management. Nevertheless, the method using the 
psychrometric chart for display of large amounts of measured data seems to be an interesting 
way of providing overview regarding thermal environment in the building. 
Dols et al. [34] indicated a need to implement IAQ commissioning as a part of a building's 
general commissioning process due to growing awareness of the importance of good indoor 
climate. As a part of indoor environmental measures, the authors propose detailed 
measurements of the HVAC system and building’s indoor climate after a month of use. The 
article does not address any IAQ commissioning during the later time of building operation if 
there are no direct indications of aggravated indoor environment (for example in the form of 
occupant complaints).  
When reviewing the IEQ related standards and guidelines, Abdul-Wahab [30] argues that 
through the last decade, the main focus of the guidelines is on ventilation systems in order to 
provide a good indoor environment. Many times the cause for IEQ problem lies in the 
ventilation system [29]. Having detailed information of the installed HVAC system and its 
operation schedule for the investigated building can help recognizing malfunctions at early 
stage and can lead to better indoor environment and also to energy saving [20]. 
2.2 Building performance indicators 
In this chapter the key indicators used in energy consumption analysis of building, indoor 
environmental quality studies and in evaluation of energy performance in connection to certain 
indoor environment parameters are presented. Those are the indicators identified in the 
articles during the literature review. 
==PAGE:20==
2 Background study 
6 
 
2.2.1 Energy performance indicators 
When doing the evaluation of energy performance of buildings, energy consumption has to be 
broken down to some extent in order to compare the performance to a reliable reference or to 
do a building-to-building comparison [35]. 
Energy consumption can be simply presented as heating and cooling energy consumption in a 
simple time-series plot where the consumption is plotted through the last several years and as 
such visualization already has high informative value [3]. The historical annual degree-days 
data allows comparison of energy consumption to a reference year. The degree-days method 
can be also used for a comparison of energy consumption of buildings in different climates. The 
energy consumption parameter is also commonly presented as a monthly heating/cooling 
primary consumption per area unit (kWh/m2) or per heated building volume (kWh/m3) [3]. 
Also, the energy consumption per number of occupants can be computed for office buildings [3]. 
In a report produced by the “Building EQ” project [3], they describe so called energy signature 
as good visualization technique where an average monthly (heating/cooling) consumption is 
plotted against the average monthly outdoor temperature [3, 19]. Different “carpet plots” 
showing the range of heating and cooling consumption through different time of the year or day 
can be shown for a better visualization [3, 20, 21]. Catalina et al. [11] uses energy consumption 
for different building age against an average building’s envelope U-value. Moreover, the study 
investigated the correlation between energy consumption and building morphology by 
introducing a so called building shape factor (a ratio between heated volume of a building and 
an area of all surfaces that are in contact with exterior, ground or non-heated spaces).  
Detailed analysis of data regarding HVAC systems as well as improving their operation by 
resolving malfunctions has been shown by several publications [19–21] to lead to notable 
energy savings. One concrete example can be analysis of energy consumption “per component” 
(chiller, fan etc.) in relation to occupancy schedule. However, many times building analysts do 
not have full understanding of the installed system in the building due to the complexity of the 
ventilation systems. Such detailed diagnostics are done only where there is expressed necessity 
for it.  
Degree-days method 
Degree-days method (DD) is commonly used for an energy consumption estimation [1] and also 
in building’s energy analysis if building use and HVAC equipment efficiency are constant [14]. 
Using the degree-day method also helps to understand the effect of changing weather 
conditions on energy use in buildings [12]. The method itself is popular due to its simplicity. It is 
accurate for rough estimations, but as described in ASHRAE’s Handbook – Fundamentals [14] it 
is necessary to know the method’s limitations when using it.  
Despite its popularity, many articles criticise its accuracy when doing precise building 
performance estimations. Catalina et al. [11] argues that evaluating heating demand by the 
heating degree-day method (HDD) leads in most cases to over-evaluating energy demand 
without taking into account a building’s morphology or its true thermal inertia. When outdoor 
temperature is around a balance point temperature, greater is the uncertainty [14]. According 
to the definition provided in ASHRAE’s Handbook – Fundamentals [14] the balance point 
==PAGE:21==
2 Background study 
7 
 
temperature is defined as a value of the outdoor temperature at which, for specified indoor 
temperature, the total heat loss is equal to the heat gain from sun, occupants, lights etc. In mild 
weather, occupants may open windows and therefore the benefit of heat gains is reduced. If 
they keep windows closed, indoor temperature will rise above set point. In either case, the true 
value of a balance point temperature becomes uncertain during mild weather. A bin method 
[14] is another application of the degree-day method that is used if there are different 
temperature intervals and time periods are evaluated separately. According to Cox et al. [10] the 
simplified degree-days method is a convenient way to study trends and a sufficient method 
when examining influence of different weather data on energy consumption.  
More problematic than the HDD method is the cooling degree-days method (CDD) since it 
neglects an influence of latent cooling loads on cooling energy as a consequence of being 
derived from the HDD method. Chapter 19 of ASHRAE’s Handbook – Fundamentals, presents the 
cooling energy consumption estimation and the causes for inaccuracy of the CDD method when 
used in practice. Analysis of real electric consumption data using the CDD method and an 
improved wet-bulb cooling degree method (CDDw) method, where sensible and latent loads are 
taken into account, is presented in an article by Krese et al. [13]. Results of the analysis showed 
that the improved CDDw outperformed the CDD method in the majority of described cases. 
Choosing an appropriate balance point temperature is important. Also when evaluating the DD 
value for a different climate, it is important to have a common balance point temperature or 
normalize data according to the given values. The number of HDD is increased with a higher 
heating balance point temperature. As for the cooling season, chosen higher cooling balance 
point temperature leads to a lower number of CDD.  
If the DD method is calculated from outdoor temperature data retrieved from simulation 
results, it is important to understand how the data of the weather file is built. It is important to 
understand which historical data is used. On the other hand, if a DD value is obtained from real-
time measurements of BMS, it is needed to understand the development of “normalized” year 
value from historical performance data. Also, according to Andre et al. [17], BMS measurements 
of outdoor temperature can overestimate outdoor temperature due to the effect of solar 
radiation. It is important to take into account the location of BMS sensors when doing analysis.  
2.2.2 Energy vs. IEQ performance indicators 
Comparison of energy consumption data in connection to indoor environment quality 
parameters gives rise to uncertainty in accuracy due to a room-to-building level comparison. 
When evaluating the indoor environmental quality parameters, they are normally obtained for 
certain zones (micro level) whereas for the energy consumption, data is recorded for the whole 
building (macro level). No case study was found where the building’s energy consumption was 
directly compared to certain room’s indoor climate performance indicators. 
One of the common parameters investigated during a building’s operation diagnostics is a room 
temperature parameter which is relatively easily available data since it is measured by a BMS. 
Case studies presented in the report IEA Annex 40 [19] and an article by Baumann [22] 
analysed operative temperature data by means of carpet plots. The analysis of data obtained 
from BMS showed several malfunctions in a case study presented by Baumann [22]. By using 
the carpet plot it is possible to observe for a certain time of the year indoor temperature, 
==PAGE:22==
2 Background study 
8 
 
thermal comfort behaviour and see when the operative temperature exceeds a certain 
acceptable range.  
2.2.3 IEQ performance indicators 
Measured BMS data is commonly analysed during normal operation diagnostics procedure 
whereas for IEQ parameters only room temperature data is normally investigated. Catalina et al. 
[11] observed how for different building types an indoor environment changes due to a 
building’s thermal mass through a certain time of day or time of year. The study proves how the 
benefits of the high building thermal mass are not only related to an energy reduction but also 
to the thermal comfort of the building. According to Abdul-Wahab it is important that the 
thermal comfort is kept inside a specified “comfort” range since exceeding this threshold values 
can affect occupant’s well being and the productivity [30].  
Costa et al. [8] suggested using predicted and measured CO2 values to evaluate a real occupancy 
pattern, which can be further used in energy-IEQ related analyses. Furthermore, according to 
Abdul-Wahab et al. [30], CO2 concentration levels can also be an indicator to evaluate the 
operation of the examined system and its efficiency. Furthermore, it can be evaluated whether 
the ventilation rates are sufficient for the occupancy density in the building [30].  
2.3 Standards and legislation 
The purpose of this investigation was to find out which requirements are currently valid for 
commercial buildings in the field of building energy performance and indoor environment in the 
European Union (EU). Furthermore, a general overview of the requirements mentioned in the 
articles investigated is presented. 
2.3.1 Energy performance requirements 
Energy Performance of Buildings Directive (EPBD) released in 2002 and later recast in 2010 has 
been launched by the European Parliament and Council on energy efficiency of buildings [36]. 
The Directive requires that each European Union Member country adapts the legislation and 
defines specific high energy-performance standards which would lead to all new buildings 
becoming “nearly zero energy buildings” by 2020, and in the public sector by 2018. It prescribes 
energy certificates for new and existing buildings. The directive also implies that all members 
have to increase the share of their energy requirements from renewable sources and promote 
energy-efficient renovation of old buildings [36]. 
In Denmark, this requirement is defined inside the Danish building code Building Regulations 
2010 (BR10) [37]. It contains rules on construction of buildings in Denmark with detailed 
requirements for specific building areas. Chapter 6 describes the indoor climate requirements 
and Chapter 7 the energy performance of buildings. The standard sets strict requirements for 
energy efficiency of current and future buildings. The key figures for a joint assessment of 
energy and IEQ performance indicators were primarily intended for commercial buildings, 
therefore requirements for non-residential buildings were reviewed and are presented in Table 
1.  
==PAGE:23==
2 Background study 
9 
 
Table 1: Energy performance framework for non-residential buildings according to BR10 [37].  
Energy performance 
Non-residential buildings (kWh/m2 per year) 
Current BR10 requirement

Class 2015 low energy 
41+1000/A 
Class 2020 low energy 
25 
A = the building’s heated floor space in m2. 
2.3.2 IEQ performance requirements  
There exist national and international standards, technical reports and guidelines that describe 
requirements (EN 15251 [38], EN ISO 7730 [39] and CR 1752 [40]) that specify thermal comfort 
and indoor air quality criteria. Mostly the requirement specifies that an IEQ performance 
indicator should be inside specified “acceptable range”. However, the same indoor conditions 
may lead to different subjective responses. Defining acceptable ranges for IEQ performance 
indicators is difficult, since it is connected to many different fields: biometerological, 
engineering, physiological, medical and climatological [27]. However, the IEQ requirements do 
not deal with these issues much in detail.  
As could be observed, current valid standards mostly describe thermal comfort 
recommendations (EN 15251 [38] and DS 474 [41]). According to EN 15251 standard [38], this 
European standard deals with IEQ parameters that have the biggest effect on energy 
performance of buildings. EN 15251 specifies methods for long-term evaluation of IEQ 
parameters in relation to energy performance parameters and is not recommended to be used 
for detailed indoor climate surveys. It was decided to use this standard for further development 
of the new key figures, since describing the methods for evaluation of IEQ parameters in 
relation to the energy performance.  
Standard EN 15251 defines four categories for indoor environment evaluation which are 
described in Table 2. According to these four categories, recommended values for IEQ 
parameters (Predicted Mean Vote (PMV), operative temperature and CO2 concentration level) 
were established. Additional performance requirements according to standard BR10 [37] and 
EN 15251 [38] are presented in Appendix B.  
Table 2: Explanation of the criteria of the categories defined in EN15251 [38]. 
Category 
Criteria 
I 
High level of expectation and is recommended for spaces occupied by very sensitive 
and fragile persons with special requirements like handicapped, sick, very young 
children and elderly persons. 
II 
Normal level of expectation and should be used for new buildings and renovations. 
III 
An acceptable, moderate level of expectation and may be used for existing buildings. 
IV 
Values outside the criteria for the above categories. This category should only be 
accepted for a limited part of the year. 
==PAGE:24==
2 Background study 
10 
 
2.3.3 Benchmarking and legislation in practice 
Relation of the EPBD to ongoing commissioning has been widely discussed in a report produced 
by the “Building EQ” project [3]. As authors of the report point out, nowadays, after the 
certification, there is usually no continuous evaluation of the building performance in order to 
reach or maintain an energy-efficient operation. This results in an aggravated performance. The 
authors conclude that in the case of existing buildings there is a lack of alignment between 
requirements prescribed by energy certification methodology applied for continuous 
commissioning.  
Similarly, several publications reviewed in the present paper indicate a lack of consistent 
methodology. The findings of Donnell et al. [7] show that in practice for commercial buildings 
there is a lack of standardized operation inspection method that would include all necessary 
information, guidelines and tools for the analyses. A study by Atta et al. [15] revealed that 
buildings analysts in general have different knowledge, background, training and education and 
rarely have the skills and training specifically required for the optimum building management. 
According to Andre et al. [35] there is a need for proper benchmarking to support building 
performance analysis, which would allow for comparison of actual key figures to a reliable 
reference. 
2.4 BMS and building performance optimization and visualization tools 
2.4.1 BMS 
Building automation is becoming a more and more essential component of buildings since it 
provides valuable technology which can lead to energy saving. According to Fan et al. [42], it is 
estimated that for buildings in Europe, energy savings of 22 % could be reached by 2028 by the 
adoption of advanced building automation technologies. Installed BMS are capable of storing 
large amount of building performance data from sensors and allow remote monitoring and 
control of building. As could be seen from the literature review, even though today there is a 
widespread application of BMS in commercial buildings, the large amount of data and absence 
of integrated analysis techniques makes a “data rich but information poor” situation [43]. This 
leads to a rising need for powerful tools that can extract the knowledge from these large sets of 
recorded measurements. 
As building managers doing analyses emphasize, naming sensors and signals in a consistent 
manner is important in order to understand the BMS data background and simplify data 
management during the evaluation process [44]. On the other hand, according to [14], existing 
BMS alone do not facilitate anything for the building managers during a building’s performance 
analysis to make their job easier. The main reason for this lies in a lack of capability displaying 
the detailed underlying data and ways of formal data transformation that would help in 
analyses and lead to accurate decision making [6, 14].  
Data mining techniques have been used in the past for fault detection analysis in order to 
improve a building’s performance and energy savings. Khan et al. [45] present a case study 
where different pattern recognition techniques are used to analyse the recorded BMS data of 
active electrical power of lighting and total active electrical power of eight adjacent building. 
==PAGE:25==
2 Background study 
11 
 
The methods used for data analysis are data mining techniques and ANN BEM (artificial neural 
networks and basic ensembling method) in connection to outlier detection. A tested method 
proved to be suitable for an automated fault detection and diagnosis (FDD) process by being 
able to detect outliers and anomalies. This way, energy consumption can be reduced by 
detecting unusual consumption extremes.  
Fan et al. [46] present a case study where a time series data mining technique is applied to 
analyse the BMS data obtained from a real commercial building. The study shows how the data 
mining process can help discover a large amount of knowledge by introducing clusters, 
univariate and multivariate motifs and temporal association rules. Furthermore, the authors 
emphasize the importance of developing sufficient tools where data analysis techniques could 
be integrated with BMS and lead to automatic analysis and to efficient and fast knowledge 
discoveries.  
2.4.2 Building performance optimization and visualization tools  
As discovered, most building analysts still emphasize a lack of interactive tools and simple 
visualization tools for analysis of the measured data. This could be solved by using the 
automated simulation based building performance optimization (BPO) search technique 
method which is described in an article by Attia et al. [15]. The article identifies two main 
groups of tools as efficient BPO tools. The first group includes simulation based optimization 
tools (BEopt and Opt-E-Plus) and the second stand-alone optimization packages (GenOpt, 
Matlab, Topgui and modeFRONTIER). 
A lot of self produced tools are based on software Matlab [15]. The results of an interview 
among 28 optimization experts in 2011 and presented by Attia et al. [15] show that most of the 
interviewees are forced to process and convert BMS data using existing processing tools 
(DView, Excell etc.) or write scripts in Matlab in order to create interpretable results. Each 
interviewee produced his own data analysis process and custom visualization techniques; time-
series plots, histograms, carpet plots, scatter plots, box plots or 3D plots. Mostly interviewees 
recommend using the GenOpt tool but the Matlab plots are most commonly used for large sets 
of quantitative data [23]. Case studies [20-23] use the multidimensional scatter plots in order to 
be able to present results in an efficient and quick way. Using such plots helps to identify 
reasons for faults due to good graphic visualization. Accurate optimization measures can be 
defined easily by just observing such plots.  
The visualization tool PIA, developed by KTH University and based on MATLAB was used in 
several of the reviewed commissioning analyses [20-23, 47]. In the case studies the tool showed 
promising results. However, like most of the tools, the PIA tool was not completely developed 
and according to [47] lacks several features before it can become a user-friendly tool. 
According to Baumann [22], visualization of data is essential in order to facilitate the extraction 
of relevant information regarding performance trade-offs and enable one to detect even a slight 
indication of optimization potential. Isakson and Eriksson [47] argue that data visualization can 
help achieve substantial improvements in energy management where all parties involved in 
building management can easily see faults through data visual analyses without having a deeper 
understanding of the investigated buildings.  
==PAGE:26==
2 Background study 
12 
 
2.5 Research questions 
The aim of the literature review was to investigate whether there are any indicators used in 
practice that show a building’s energy consumption in relation to IEQ parameters. It was 
discovered that the main focus of building’s performance analyses lies on the building’s energy 
performance analyses. In the reviewed articles there has not been a case where a building’s 
energy performance would be evaluated in connection to the analyses of several important 
indoor climate parameters. Current building regulations mainly promote the importance of 
energy savings in buildings. The main aim of the reviewed case studies was mostly identifying 
major factors that influence energy consumption or deal with operation diagnostics of the 
buildings and finding the optimization potential. Some articles present indoor environmental 
quality studies. However, these articles strictly deal with assessing the indoor climate of the 
analysed building.  
Looking at the case studies, only operative temperature or CO2 concentration levels were 
investigated when doing operation diagnostics of a building where the primary aim was a 
building’s energy performance analysis. It was discovered that thermal comfort is of greatest 
importance among the building users when evaluating indoor environment. Further 
investigation will be done where energy performance indicators can be compared to certain IEQ 
performance indicators to see if some important correlations between these indicators can be 
found. The emphasis is on finding such IEQ parameters that allow joint assessment of the 
energy and indoor climate performance. Also the idea is that the potential new key figures are 
applicable with the measured data from the BMS. Mostly such data includes operative 
temperature and CO2 concentrations.  
For example, the energy performance indicator (energy consumption per unit area) can be 
compared with a monthly degree hours criterion indicator (Method B, Annex F, EN 15251 [38]). 
By analysing such an indicator, the operation of the current building heating or cooling system 
can be examined. The analysis can show whether the system with its current schedule is capable 
of maintaining room temperatures inside a specified range during occupied hours.  
Since the room temperature data is a part of the usual BMS measurement data collection, 
heating consumption and room temperature data can be easily plotted against the certain time 
of year for the same time step [19]. Also, energy consumption in relation to an outdoor and 
indoor temperature can be observed. First, such a plot can show how the energy consumption 
rises with changing outdoor conditions. It can be seen whether the rising energy consumption 
for space heating or cooling is capable of maintaining the indoor environment inside an 
acceptable range. Second, for a different building age the energy consumption and the room 
temperature data can be analysed during the different outdoor conditions to see how sudden 
the changes of indoor temperatures are. Such analysis can also be used to study trends for a 
large number of buildings.  
Nevertheless, it was not discovered how accurate room-to-building level comparison can be. No 
case study was found where they would discuss which room type to take as representative 
when doing a study for a large number of buildings. Further investigation will be done to asses 
which room type and number of rooms should be included in the joint assessment analyses in 
order to reach with correct conclusions.  
==PAGE:27==
2 Background study 
13 
 
Another remaining issue is how much information is needed when doing a building-to-building 
comparison. It is the responsibility of a building analyst to decide how much information he 
needs in order to do an accurate analysis. Commonly the building manager obtains information 
about general characteristics of investigated buildings (building characteristics, systems, usage 
type, occupancy schedule, etc.) and climate.  
Over and above, another task was to investigate whether there exists an effective way to 
translate the high amount of collected data recorded by extensive BMS into useful and easily 
comprehensible information. Currently, analyses require manual data manipulation which not 
only leads to time consuming analyses but there seems to be general mistrust in the results 
obtained by using such optimization tools [15]. No effective and structured method was found 
that would provide the appropriate tools for analyses and lead to establishing a non-subjective 
and reliable building performance analysis mechanism. A need for a structured methodology 
and developed tools enabling such data analysis has been exposed in several articles.  
 
 
==PAGE:28==
3 Thesis disposition 
14 
 
3 Thesis disposition 
The relatively broad scope of the thesis makes it important to state what the thesis actually 
covers. The supplementary objectives and approach were defined once literature research 
showed what had been done until now in the area of interest. The literature review served as a 
basis for the identification of existing performance indicators used in practice and 
brainstorming for new key figures for the joint assessment of energy consumption and IEQ.  
In order to answer the two general objectives (chapter 1) and research questions (chapter 2.5), 
the thesis was divided into two parts with individual objectives, methodology, results and 
discussion. Part B is dependent on Part A, meaning that the developed methodology, key figures 
and findings from Part A were used for the analyses in Part B. Part A presents the identification 
of existing performance indicators and the development of new key figures that allowed for a 
common assessment of energy and IEQ performance indicators. Existing indicators and new 
developed key figures were tested on simulation data results. In Part B, the developed key 
figures were used for analyses of BMS data from existing buildings in order to assess the 
practical usability of the key figures. The supplementary objectives were:  
PART A:  
- 
Define most commonly used indicators for energy and IEQ performance analysis.  
- 
Identify the potential performance indicators that could be used for development of new 
key figures where both indicators are plotted on the same figure. 
- 
Develop an evaluation criterion for the new key figures for joint assessment analysis. 
- 
Develop a methodology (key figures) for the joint assessment of energy consumption 
and IEQ. Test new key figures for the joint assessment of energy consumption and IEQ 
on data obtained from dynamic simulation models. 
- 
Assess the practical usability of the proposed key figures by analysing the computed 
figures on simulation data and apply improvements where necessary.  
- 
Develop final key figures that can be used in analyses of existing buildings (Part B).  
- 
Validate the developed methodology and key figures for joint assessment analysis and 
discuss the conditions that need to be fulfilled in order to establish accurate analyses.  
PART B:  
- 
Validate accuracy of the collected BMS data from existing buildings.  
- 
Asses the practical usability of the key figures by analysing the BMS data for existing 
commercial buildings. This is divided into two groups: Group B.1 includes the analysis of 
the key figures for a large number of buildings and Group B.2 analysis of a single 
building with detailed building information. 
- 
Discuss the practical usability of the developed key figures on data from existing 
buildings; identify their usability to study trends for a large number of buildings or as a 
tool for continuous commissioning process of an existing building. 
The findings from both parts were then summarized in the conclusion where are also presented 
possible changes and recommendations for further work. 
 
==PAGE:29==
4 Part A: Development of new key figures using simulation data 
15 
 
4 Part A: Development of new key figures using simulation data  
4.1 Methodology 
In this chapter, the methodology that was followed during the identification of performance 
indicators and development of new key figures is presented. During the literature review no 
appropriate methodology was found for joint analysis of energy consumption and IEQ, therefore 
an individual methodology was developed in order to answer the general thesis objectives. 
Development of the new key figures was done while analysing simulation model results which 
were obtained using software IDA Indoor Climate and Energy (ICE). The literature review 
showed that Matlab software is most commonly used by building managers for writing scripts 
and analysis of large quantitative sets of data. This software was also used for the thesis 
purposes. First existing energy and IEQ performance indicators were reviewed and then further 
analyses were done to investigate the relationship between those two types of indicators. This 
results in the development of potential new key figures for joint assessment of energy 
consumption and IEQ. 
4.1.1 Simulation models 
Defined methodology and developed key figures were tested on energy and indoor climate data 
retrieved from the building simulation model made in the software IDA ICE.  
Simulation models are commonly used in energy management, commissioning and operation 
diagnostics as described during the background study [7, 8, 16, 20, 21]. Results allow 
assessment of dynamic interactions between the climate, a building’s energy and IEQ 
parameters once detailed input variables about building characteristics and its systems are 
defined [53]. For the analyses of existing performance indicators and development of new key 
figures the dynamic energy simulation method was used. Different simulation scenarios were 
created by changing a building’s system schedules and settings. Performance indicators and 
developed key figures were tested on data obtained from such simulations.  
Software IDA ICE 
IDA ICE is software developed by the Swedish company EQUA Simulation AB for the analysis of 
building’s energy performance and indoor climate [53]. It gives an opportunity to carry a whole-
year dynamic and detailed multi-zone simulation. The IDA ICE user interface is divided into 
three levels depending on the user’s needs and expertise. For the thesis project the standard 
level was mainly used due to its simplicity and efficient and fast implementation. The advanced 
level allows a more detailed design of the building and its operational schedules. For the 
purpose of the project, the advanced level was used for the design of a window opening 
schedule when a building scenario model with natural ventilation was investigated. 
IDA ICE simulations are capable of utilizing a building’s thermal mass and zone heat balance 
(including sun, occupants, equipment etc.). The simulations also allow calculation of the comfort 
indices PPD and PMV, air temperature, air humidity and CO2 concentrations. The weather data 
==PAGE:30==
4 Part A: Development of new key figures using simulation data 
16 
 
is supplied by yearly weather files or is manually defined for a given 24-hour period (design 
days) [10].  
Simulation model 
In order to test the developed key figure, an office building was used for the simulations. The 
building model was built following the office building example given in “Getting Started guide - 
IDA Indoor Climate and Energy” [53]. According to the guide, all needed input parameters for 
the basic model were inserted and are presented in Appendix C. Results obtained from the 
simulations were used for the development and tests of the new key figures. The simulation 
period was the year 2014: 01.01.2014–31.12.2014. 
The general concept was to design a building in a technically good condition. The aim of the 
building simulations was not to optimize the building’s design but to evaluate the developed key 
figures. According to the basic design model, certain modifications were done to the HVAC 
operation schedules for different scenarios.  
 
Figure 1: 3D simulation building model in IDA ICE. 
The basic office model presented a 2-story office building with 694 m2 gross floor area. The 
building’s geometry can be seen in Figure 1 where the building’s floor plan was same for both 
floors. The whole building was divided between twelve two-person office rooms orientated 
towards the south, four open-plan offices orientated towards the north (each facilitates 13 
employees) and into other facilities areas (toilets, storage, stairs).  
The occupancy schedule was defined from 6h–18h during weekdays and no holidays was 
assumed. It was assumed that each person uses technical equipment (laptop) with a power of 
75W. Electrical lighting (12W bulb) was turned on when daylight was not sufficient in providing 
the required 500 lux light intensity at the workplace during occupied hours. Since it was an 
office building, sedentary work which corresponds to 1.2 met was assumed. In heating season, 
when the temperature should be minimum 20 °C, clothing with a thermal insulation of 1.0 clo 
was assumed. In summer months, when temperature below 26 °C was desired, clothing with 
thermal insulation of 0.5 was assumed.  
The hourly data for outdoor temperature were obtained from hourly historic weather data 
measurements. The simulation model was provided with the outdoor conditions depending on 
the selected location and climate file. It was considered that the building was positioned in 
Copenhagen, Denmark, therefore, the climate and weather files (Værløse, Denmark) were 
==PAGE:31==
4 Part A: Development of new key figures using simulation data 
17 
 
chosen accordingly. The TRY weather file for Værløse (Copenhagen, Denmark) was used where 
a chosen year consists of measured data of representative months. Neither ISO 7730 [39] nor 
EN 15251 [38] do clarify the length of heating and cooling season or methodology to define it. It 
was estimated that the heating season in Denmark was considered from 1st October until 30th 
April and cooling season 1st June until 30th September. However, mechanical cooling was 
assumed to be activated whenever the operative temperature exceeded the maximum desired 
temperature of 26 °C (also outside summer months).  
A building with a highly insulated building envelope was designed where U-values for specific 
construction elements are presented in Appendix C. 
Ventilation system 
The designed offices were ventilated using a constant air volume system (CAV). The supply air 
temperature for the air handling unit (AHU) was left at the default of 16 °C. The air exchange 
rate was defined according to the formula given in Annex B (B.1) of standard EN 15251 [38] and 
is also presented in Appendix B. For the simulation models, an outdoor CO2 level of 400 ppm 
was used since it is defined by default as the ambient air CO2 level in the software settings. 
Heating energy  
Radiators were installed in the whole building, since they are the most commonly used heating 
appliances in commercial buildings. The heating energy was supplied via district heating. A heat 
exchanger with an efficiency of 85 % was chosen. The heat to the rooms was emitted by energy 
efficient low-temperature water based radiators where supply temperature was set to 55 °C 
and return 45 °C. The maximum supply heating water temperature was lowered to 55 °C in the 
plant. The control of the radiators was set to PI (proportional) which means it was regulated 
according to the operative temperature in the room. The heating set point was set to 20 °C to 
ensure pleasant room temperature in offices for the occupants. How water usage was set to 100 
l/m2 floor area and year during occupied hours.  
Cooling energy 
To ensure good indoor environment also in summer, mechanical cooling was assumed. As 
cooling consumption, electricity for fan operation was analysed. Cooling was generated with a 
chiller from AHU which had a supply water temperature of 5 °C. Offices were provided with 
cooling panels where zone supply temperature was set to 15 °C. The cooling set point was set to 
26 °C to provide comfortable indoor temperature and avoid a problem with overheating in 
summer. It was considered that the cooling energy was provided via district cooling. 
Building simulation scenarios  
 
 
 
Three different simulation scenarios were tested in order to establish three different building 
behaviours (difference in energy and IEQ performance). All three scenarios present same 
building described above where the only modifications were done to the building’s HVAC 
system control (settings) and window opening (possibility for natural ventilation). This is 
shortly presented in Table 3. 
==PAGE:32==
4 Part A: Development of new key figures using simulation data 
18 
 
Table 3: Settings of the HVAC system (heating, cooling, and ventilation) for three simulated scenarios.  
 
Scenario I  
Scenario II 
Scenario III  
Heating 
- Radiator heating 
- Radiator heating 
(reduced heating power) - Radiator heating 
Cooling 
- Mechanical cooling 
during occupied time 
- No natural ventilation 
- No mechanical cooling 
- No natural ventilation 
- Mechanical cooling 
during occupied time

natural ventilation 
Ventilation - Mechanical ventilation 
- No mechanical 
ventilation 
- Mechanical ventilation 
- Natural ventilation 
The first scenario (Scenario I) presents a basic building design with utilized HVAC system 
providing mechanical heating, cooling and ventilation of building. No natural ventilation was 
assumed.  
The second scenario (Scenario II) presents the same building where changes were made to the 
design power of the radiators which was reduced to half in order to present poorly designed 
building. For the second scenario a building without mechanical cooling was also considered. 
Still no natural ventilation was provided. This scenario was tested in an attempt to find out how 
the reduced heat distribution in winter and no mechanical cooling in summer affect the indoor 
climate for the tested office building. 
The third scenario (Scenario III) presents the same building scenario as presented in Scenario I 
where only the window opening schedule was changed. In the third scenario, the building was 
cooled also naturally by opening windows through the nights in order to support the designed 
mechanical cooling system. This can also help solving the problem with overheating in a more 
energy efficient way. Since finding an optimal design was not the aim of this case study, the 
night ventilation was designed for the whole year. If during the year when occupants leave the 
building (18h), the operative temperature still exceeded 24.5 °C, windows were automatically 
opened; only for 25 % to avoid the draft problems in winter (setting: 0.25 [18–6] weekdays). 
Simulation results analysis 
For the analyses done on simulation data, primary energy factors described in BR10 were 
applied [37]. According to BR10 [37], a primary energy factor of 0.8 was applied for district 
heating and cooling and primary energy factor of 2.5 for electricity usage (lighting, mechanical 
ventilation). The energy performance was evaluated for the whole building where energy use 
was expressed per unit of heated floor area (kWh/m2). General observation of the simulation 
results showed that for all the zones, CO2 concentrations and relative humidity were inside the 
recommended range for Category II of standard EN 15251 [38]. Recommended limit values for 
Category II of standard EN 15251 are presented in Appendix B (Table B.1).  
As a representative zone for the IEQ analysis, an office with the worst thermal comfort (problem 
with operative temperature) was considered. Results of indoor environment were extracted for 
a zone which had most occupied hours when the operative temperature exceeded 26 °C which 
indicated a problem with overheating of the space according to Category II, EN 15251 [38]. For 
all three simulated building scenarios, this zone represented an open-plan office which 
==PAGE:33==
4 Part A: Development of new key figures using simulation data 
19 
 
facilitated 13-employees and had a big glazing area oriented towards the northwest. The main 
focus of the indoor climate analyses was done for the office zones since those are the zones 
where building occupants spend most of their time. Where the aim of the building analyses is 
operation diagnostics, a more detailed investigation can be done including analyses for other 
zones (meeting rooms, canteen, toilets etc.).  
4.1.2 Development of performance indicators in Matlab  
As was discovered during the literature review, no developed tool was found that allows 
analysis of high-frequency BMS data and automatically enables visualization of the analysed 
results. For the purposes of this thesis project and the development of the new key figures, self-
produced scripts were developed using the software package Matlab. 
Software Matlab 
Matlab is a technical computing system for handling scientific and engineering calculations with 
numerical, graphical and programming capabilities and was developed by MathWorks [54]. This 
platform was used since the programming language is very flexible and has an easy user 
interface [55]. Also, the software offers a wider range of tools for development of self-produced 
scripts and allows advanced plotting of data. Furthermore, the built-in functions allow high-
frequency data analysis by using them in the script editor. 
Self-produced scripts in Matlab 
Two books [54, 55] on Matlab’s scientific computing approach were followed in order to 
produce the desired key figures by developing scripts in Matlab. The aim was to develop scripts 
that handle input for a large amount of high-frequency building data.  
For Part A, scripts were developed according to the simulation results. Later on (Part B), scripts 
needed to be revised and adjusted to the different type of input data in order to perform 
analyses on data from BMS installed in real buildings. All the Matlab scripts are presented in a 
ZIP file.  
4.1.3 Joint assessment of energy and IEQ performance indicators  
As discovered during the literature review, energy efficiency is one of the major focuses when 
doing the evaluation of building’s performance. Therefore, energy performance indicators were 
first investigated as a pre-processing step for the later development of new key figures.  
The background study served as a basis to investigate which types of IEQ parameters would be 
most suitable for a joint assessment of the energy and IEQ performance indicators. Based on 
these findings, IEQ parameters related to thermal comfort (operative temperature) and IAQ 
(CO2 concentrations) were analysed.  
The key figures were plotted where the two types of performance indicators, related to the 
building’s energy consumption and IEQ, were combined in the same figure. The developed 
method for the joint assessment of energy consumption and IEQ was primarily developed for 
the analyses of buildings with an installed HVAC system where the energy consumption of a 
==PAGE:34==
4 Part A: Development of new key figures using simulation data 
20 
 
certain system (heating, cooling, ventilation) can be compared to the measured IEQ data 
(thermal comfort and IAQ). Furthermore, these buildings should be equipped with an advanced 
BMS measuring a sufficient amount of data.  
The main section of Part A presents the development and testing of key figures (on simulation 
data) which showed how a building’s energy consumption was related to the indoor 
environment, such as thermal comfort and IAQ. The main aim was to observe on a single plot, 
the correlation between the provided energy for the building’s operation and the quality of the 
indoor environment.  
The idea was to establish an evaluation criterion for the developed key figures in respect to 
current energy performance requirements (building regulations BR10 [37]) and IEQ 
recommendations (standard EN 15251 [38]). It was believed, that this way it is possible to 
evaluate whether the building is energy efficient and at the same time provides good indoor 
environment for the occupants by comparing the results to the required or recommended 
boundary limits from a chosen standard. 
It was discovered that for the evaluation of indoor environment according to EN 15251, 
calculations of the IEQ performance indicators should be done in time intervals of an hour [49]. 
Hourly simulated or measured data should be used for the calculation of monthly values where 
less frequent recorded datasets (daily average values) of IEQ parameters do not allow a detailed 
enough analysis of the IEQ.  
Energy consumption is most commonly presented as a single value for the whole building on a 
yearly basis [3] whereas IEQ performance indicators are measured for a certain room and 
analysed for a shorter period of time (day, week, occupied/non-occupied hours etc) [27, 28, 32]. 
However, it was discovered that IEQ evaluation can be also done over a longer period of time 
(month, heating/cooling season, year etc.) [38]. A period of a month was recommended when 
doing joint assessments since the annual single value for the IEQ performance indicators does 
not necessarily present enough information. Furthermore, due to complex building dynamics 
and day-to-day variations in the building use, a shorter time period (day, week) does not seem 
useful for a general benchmarking and annual joint assessment of energy consumption and IEQ 
[3]. Monthly evaluations seem to still allow for a general observation of the building’s operation 
and can clearly show when there is something significantly wrong with the building’s operation. 
In order to show the building’s energy performance in relation to the particular IEQ 
performance indicator on a single plot, 2D plots were mostly used where an IEQ performance 
indicator and an energy performance indicator was presented on the x-axis and y-axis 
respectively. A 3D plot was used to show three different types of data where energy and IEQ 
performance indicators along with outdoor conditions were represented (outdoor 
temperature). 
Furthermore, it was investigated whether it is possible to present a “single number” indicator 
where a building’s energy consumption and IEQ could be represented with a single ratio value 
giving general information about the building’s overall performance.  
The methodology approach used for joint assessment analysis in this project is graphically 
presented by a flowchart shown in Figure 2.  
==PAGE:35==
4 Part A: Development of new key figures using simulation data 
21 
 
 
Figure 2: Flowchart showing the development approach used for the joint assessment of energy and IEQ 
performance indicators. Energy performance indicators (space heating; Qh, space cooling; Qc and mechanical 
ventilation; Qv consumption) were investigated in relation to IEQ performance indicators (showing thermal 
comfort in winter and summer (temperature; T) and IAQ (CO2 concentrations) through the whole year).  
The objective of the developed key figures was to graphically illustrate whether having a HVAC 
system maintains a comfortable indoor environment. That is, during the winter, space heating 
maintains the temperatures above the minimum desired temperature limit (for example 20 °C) 
and during the summer, cooling system prevents overheating of the rooms (temperature below 
26 °C). For most non-residential buildings, mechanical ventilation is also needed in order to 
provide good air quality for the occupants [26]. Therefore, evaluation of mechanical ventilation 
consumption against CO2 concentrations can indicate the quality of indoor air.  
Later on, when using expression key figure this refers to a key figure for a joint assessment of 
energy consumption and IEQ related performance indicators. 
Energy performance indicators  
As discussed during the background study, there exist many ways in how to present the energy 
data (like energy consumption per unit area, per volume or per person) [3]. In Europe, the 
building managers most commonly present annual energy consumption per unit area [3] and 
this energy performance indicator was also used for the thesis analyses. It should be noted that 
later on, where the expression energy consumption is used, it is referring to the energy 
consumption per square meter (kWh/m2). For simplicity’s sake, a shorter expression was used.  
 
==PAGE:36==
4 Part A: Development of new key figures using simulation data 
22 
 
General energy use 
The general energy use indicator was investigated since this indicator gives an overview of a 
building’s energy performance on a yearly basis for the whole building. Annual primary energy 
consumption was for each building scenario evaluated against current energy performance 
requirements and this was presented on a simple bar chart.  
For the developed indicators, the main idea was to present the energy usage indicator as 
monthly primary energy consumption for the subsystems such as heating (Qh), cooling (Qc) and 
ventilation (Qv). This way, the amount of energy spent for space heating can be directly related 
to the indoor environment during colder periods. Similarly, the cooling energy usage can be 
related to the indoor environment during warm periods. Mechanical ventilation consumption 
can be related to the IAQ where it is needed to know whether was the examined building 
ventilated also naturally (cooled). 
Current energy performance requirements were revised in order to investigate whether an 
evaluation criterion could be established which could be used in the joint assessment analyses. 
The aim was to find recommended boundary limits for the monthly evaluation of energy 
performance indicators. The developed indicators present monthly values, separated on 
whether the energy goes for space heating, cooling or ventilation. Energy performance 
requirements defined by BR10 [37] were revised according to current energy requirement 
2010 and for the Low energy class 2015. The energy performance requirements of BR10 are 
defined for a building’s supplied energy annually and this includes annual primary consumption 
for space heating, cooling, mechanical ventilation, domestic hot water and lighting for 
commercial buildings [37]. As seen, the energy performance requirements present annual data 
for the whole building, therefore finding monthly recommended limits according to current 
energy standards seems impossible.  
However, instead of using monthly energy requirement from the standard, as energy 
recommended limit for heating, cooling and ventilation predicted values from the building’s 
design phase can be used when doing evaluation of the existing buildings. 
Energy consumption in relation to monthly degree-days  
The DD method was primarily used to investigate the influence of outdoor conditions (outdoor 
temperature) on energy performance of a building (heating and cooling). Heating and cooling 
energy consumption were computed against HDD and CDD for heating and cooling season 
respectively. Numbers of degree-days (for heating and cooling season) were calculated 
according to the method provided by ASHRAE [14] using hourly values for the outdoor 
temperature. Calculation process for the heating season (HDD) is presented in Equation 1 and 
for the cooling season (CDD) in Equation 2, where tbal presents balance point temperature and 
tout hourly simulated or measured outdoor temperature [14].
