==PAGE:37==
4 Part A: Development of new key figures using simulation data 
23 
 
For simulation results, degree-days value for each month was calculated from the simulated 
outdoor dry-bulb temperature data. The balance point temperature (tbal) for HDD calculations 
was chosen at 17 °C (Equation 1) and for CDD at 25 °C (Equation 2), which is according to Cox et 
al. typically used in Denmark [10]. Cox et al. describe that it can be assumed that natural cooling 
of a building positioned in Denmark can be obtained when the outdoor temperature is between 
17 and 25 °C. 
Energy signature 
Energy signature was chosen as an energy performance indicator since it illustrates how a 
building’s heating and cooling energy consumption changes with changing outdoor conditions 
on a monthly basis [3]. According to the report by “Building EQ” [3], energy signature is a 
convenient and useful tool for regular internal benchmarking of the investigated building where 
monthly values are investigated. Heating and cooling energy consumption was plotted against 
monthly average outdoor temperatures for heating and cooling seasons respectively. Monthly 
average outdoor temperatures were calculated from the average daily outdoor temperatures. 
Average daily temperatures were for all simulation scenarios calculated from the hourly 
outdoor temperature data obtained from the simulation results.  
Regression analysis 
During the literature review, it was discovered that the regression analysis method is widely 
used in building energy performance analysis due to simplicity and accuracy when evaluating 
the relationship between two chosen variables [13, 50, 51]. For the thesis purposes, the 
regression analysis method was applied in order to investigate the relationship between a 
predictor variable (DD or monthly average outdoor temperature) and a response variable 
(energy consumption). The aim was to assess whether there exists a linear correlation between 
the chosen two quantities and how much can be the response variable (energy consumption) 
only seen as a function of predictor variable (DD or monthly average outdoor temperature).  
The linear regression method for data analysis in Matlab [52] was followed. During the 
regression analyses, the obtained residuals were studied to see how closely the dependent 
variable values are from the fitted linear model curve [52]. By using MATLAB Basic Fitting User 
Interface linear models having a linear predictor function (1st polynomial equation) were 
applied. The best fit was found for the given data and the corresponding coefficient of 
determination (R2). For the analyses, R2 and the residual plots were studied. R2 values show 
how well the established model describes the dependant variable on a scale from 0 (no fit) to 1 
(perfect fit). Furthermore, coefficients describing 1st polynomial equation (output=p1*input+p2) 
were studied where needed. In general, coefficient p1 describes the slope of the fitted linear line 
and p2 the intercept (value of the response variable (consumption) when the predictor (DD) is 
zero). It should be noted, that the best fit curve had to be interpreted correctly and therefore 
before the regression analyses were done, the raw input data was verified to see whether it 
included some outliers. The raw data was validated before doing the regression analyses in 
order not to interpret the correlation between the chosen variables due to a faulty input value.  
 
 
==PAGE:38==
4 Part A: Development of new key figures using simulation data 
24 
 
IEQ performance indicators  
One of the objectives for the developed key figures was to be applicable with BMS data therefore 
IEQ parameters measured with BMS were primarily investigated. On a continuous basis, 
installed BMS sensors can normally record IEQ parameters such as indoor air temperature and 
CO2 concentrations [49]. 
As for the IEQ performance indicators, operative temperature (thermal comfort) and CO2 
concentration levels (IAQ) were studied. Standard EN 15251 and its Category II 
recommendations were used for the evaluation of thermal comfort and IAQ. According to 
standard EN 15251 [38], Category II is used for a normal level of expectations (new buildings 
and renovations). Additional indoor environment quality requirements for the Category II (for 
new buildings and renovations) of the standard EN 15251 are presented in Appendix B. 
According to Annex G, indoor environment parameters in the rooms occupying 95 % of total 
used space should be inside the boundary limits of the specified category of EN 15251 for at 
least 95 % of the normal time of building use (occupied hours) [38]. Still acceptable yet 
tolerable violation of Category II boundary limits was considered when plotted IEQ 
performance indicators met this 95 % occupied hours limit criterion for Category II boundary 
limits. 
Later, when referring to Category II and Annex G recommendations, this refers to Category II 
and Annex G recommendations of standard EN 15251 [38]. 
Thermal comfort indicators 
“Long-term evaluation of the general thermal comfort conditions” method described in Annex F 
of standard EN 15251 [38] was investigated since this annex describes a technique allowing 
evaluation of thermal comfort conditions over time, based on hourly simulated or measured 
data. Since the aim of the IEQ performance indicators was to be applicable with the energy 
consumption, only the lower temperature limit of Category II (20 °C) for the heating season and 
the upper temperature limit of Category II (26 °C) for the cooling season was observed.  
To evaluate whether the heating system with its heating consumption was capable of 
maintaining the room temperatures above the desired lower limit, the evaluation of the 
temperatures below the lower temperature limit of Category II (20 °C) was investigated for the 
heating season. As specified in Annex A, EN 15251 [38], the lower limit corresponds to -0.5 PMV 
for heating season (office space, sedentary activity: 1.2 met, clothing: 1 clo). For the cooling 
season, only the upper temperature limit (26 °C) was observed in order to evaluate whether the 
building was cooled (naturally or mechanically) sufficiently and the temperatures were kept 
below the desired maximum temperature. According to Annex A, EN 15251 [38], the upper limit 
corresponds to 0.5 PMV for cooling season (office space, sedentary activity: 1.2 met, clothing: 
0.5 clo).  
The three methods described in Annex F “Long-term evaluation of the general thermal comfort 
conditions” of standard EN 15251 [38] and used for evaluation of IEQ in this project are: 
Method A, Method B and Method C. Later, when referring to Method A, Method B or Method C, 
this refers to the three methods of Annex F, EN 15251 [38]. 
==PAGE:39==
4 Part A: Development of new key figures using simulation data 
25 
 
Method A: Percentage outside the range (Annex F, EN 15251) 
This method defines a percentage of occupied hours outside the required comfort range; when 
PMV or the operative temperature is outside a specified range. For this project, the operative 
temperature boundaries of Category II were used (lower limit of 20 °C and upper limit 26 °C). It 
presents a simple method where a building manager’s decision can be made instantaneously if 
the allowable dead band (the boundary condition) is exceeded.  
When doing IEQ evaluation, results for Method A were shown on a yearly basis (annual 
occupied hours) since this is most commonly used IEQ performance indicator in operation 
diagnostics when doing annual building’s performance evaluation. Furthermore, monthly values 
for Method A were used for joint assessment analyses. 
Method B: Degree hours criterion (Annex F, EN 15251) 
Method B proposes a calculation of degree hours (DH) which represents a sum of occupied 
hours during which requirements are violated multiplied by weighting factor expressed as a 
difference (in °C) between actual and limit temperature. It was used to observe how many hours 
during winter months the operative temperature dropped below a lower limit of Category II (20 
°C). This is expressed as heating degree hours (HDH). For the summer months (or even for the 
whole year), it was observed how many hours during occupied hours the operative temperature 
exceeded a desired maximum temperature of Category II (26 °C). This is expressed as cooling 
degree hours (CDH). 
Monthly HDH value represents a monthly sum of the difference between the (simulated or 
measured) operative temperature and the recommended lower limit of Category II (20 °C) 
when the operative temperature fell below the recommended 20 °C temperature limit. Monthly 
CDH value represents a monthly sum of the difference between the operative temperature and 
the recommended upper limit of 26 °C (Category II) when the operative temperature exceeded 
the recommended upper temperature limit of 26 °C. 
Method C: PPD weighted criterion (Annex F, EN 15251) 
Method C presents a PPD weighted criterion where is time, when the actual PMV exceeds the 
comfort boundaries (for Category II: -0.5<PMV<0.5), weighted by a factor which is a function of 
the PPD. This method is similar to Method B but the comfort range is defined by means of a PMV 
index while the weighting factor is defined as a ratio between an actual PPD and a comfort limit 
PPD (for Category II: 10 %). The PPD (percentage of occupants dissatisfied) is a function of 
activity (metabolic rate), clothing insulation and four environmental parameters: air 
temperature, mean radiant temperature, air velocity and air humidity.  
Monthly PPD weighted value presents a sum of the product of the weighting factor and occupied 
hours during the observed month when PMV exceeds the comfort boundaries [38].  
 
 
==PAGE:40==
4 Part A: Development of new key figures using simulation data 
26 
 
Improvement method for Method A and B 
During the background study, when reviewing the three methods described in Annex F, EN 
15251, several limitations of these methods were exposed.  
As Carlucci and Pagliano [49] describe, there is a difficulty to clearly interpret values of Method 
A since these values only present the percentage of occurrence (frequency) when the operative 
temperature is outside the specified temperature range (Category II: 20–26 °C) and do not 
contain any information about the severity of the thermal discomfort. For the evaluation of 
results for Method A, Annex G can be applied which specifies the range for acceptable deviation 
(when temperatures are outside the specified temperature range) as maximum 5 % of the 
occupied hours. 
The limitation of Method B is that the same value of DH can be calculated in a case where there 
are only a few hours with high DH values (severe temperature difference) or in a situation 
where for larger number of occupied hours there is a low DH value (temperatures slightly 
below the lower or above the upper temperature limit). However, the recommendation 
according to Annex G cannot be easily applied for the evaluation of the results according to 
Method B. No limit degree hours value was found that would indicate a still acceptable violation 
of the thermal comfort according to Method B from standard EN 15251 [38]. Furthermore, the 
value can be varying from zero to theoretical infinity [49]. Understanding the value of thermal 
comfort indicator described by Method B is therefore difficult. 
The decision was made to further develop the current methodology describing Method A and 
Method B. Improvement method for Method A and B was defined in this section and later applied 
in the joint assessment analyses. The hypothesis of the author of the thesis was that more 
information and reliable result one can get, when evaluating value for the Method A together 
with value obtained from the Method B. It was investigated what would be the monthly degree 
hours limit value for the recommended 5 % exceedance of operative temperature during 
occupied hours (recommended limit of Annex G). The monthly degree hours limit value was 
calculated according to the average number of monthly occupied hours for the simulated 
building (opening hours during weekdays between 6h–18h). 
Table 4: Improvement method for Method A and B - Calculation method for recommended monthly DH limit 
value for 260 average monthly occupied hours.  
Average number of 
monthly occupied hours 
Recommended limit value of  
Annex G, EN 15251 [38] 
Monthly DH limit value 
260 
5 % 
260* 0.05 = 13 DH 
As seen from Table 4, the calculated value for recommended monthly DH limit was estimated at 
13 DH for the defined monthly occupied hours.  
The recommended value of 13 DH describes that in a case where for 5 % of monthly occupied 
hours the operative temperature exceeds the specified temperature range (Category II: 20–26 
°C), this temperature exceedance can be up to 1 °C for each exceeded hour. This wider 
temperature range (19–27 °C) presents Category III limits of standard EN 15251 [38]. 
According to the EN 15251 [38], the Category III can be still seen as acceptable criterion for the 
existing buildings. The developed method Improvement method for Method A and B describes 
==PAGE:41==
4 Part A: Development of new key figures using simulation data 
27 
 
that if the operative temperature for certain hour falls below 20 °C, this drop can be maximum 1 
°C (reaching 19 °C). For the summer season, this means that if the temperature exceeds 26 °C, 
this 1 DH results in temperature reaching up to 27 °C.  
This improvement method and using 13 DH as recommended value for monthly DH was applied 
as an evaluation criterion for the key figures for the joint assessment of energy consumption 
and IEQ in order to asses method’s practical usability.  
IAQ indicators 
According to EN 15251, occupants are the main pollution source during occupied hours for the 
office buildings and therefore IAQ in this building type can be evaluated based on CO2 
concentrations [38]. The indoor air quality was evaluated based on simulated and measured 
hourly indoor CO2 concentrations for the simulated building scenarios and the examined 
commercial buildings. 
According to Category II, indoor CO2 levels should not exceed 500 ppm more than the outdoor 
concentration level. Recommended limit value according to Category II is only an indicator of 
good indoor air climate in order to avoid “stuffy” air and such indoor concentrations do not yet 
present a health issue [29]. It should be noted that once Category II limits are exceeded, this 
indicates that ventilation rates are inadequate. However, if health issues are investigated than 
values around 5000 ppm should be considered as limits [30]. Besides showing whether air 
exchange rates are sufficient, such evaluation can also be used to indicate whether there are 
other pollutants and particles in the indoor air. Normally with higher CO2 levels, higher 
concentrations of other particles can also be inspected [29]. 
For simulation scenarios, outdoor concentration of 400 ppm was assumed which leads to 
recommended CO2 limit of 900 ppm. 
The CDF graph was used to illustrate annual percentage CO2 distribution during occupied hours. 
It can be quickly evaluated whether the recommended CO2 limit value is exceeded for more than 
5 % of occupied time (Annex G).  
An indicator showing monthly average CO2 concentrations was plotted in order to observe how 
CO2 levels change through the year for different building types (simulated scenarios). The main 
disadvantage was that it presents average monthly values. Each monthly value presents only a 
single value which does not provide much information about day-to-day IAQ. Despite this fact, 
the indicator was used to be later applied in the joint assessment process.  
Key figures for joint assessment of energy and IEQ performance indicators 
Developed key figures for joint assessment of energy and IEQ performance indicators can be 
sorted into two groups. The first part includes key figures where the evaluation of energy 
performance indicators (heating and cooling consumption) against thermal comfort indicators 
(operative temperature) was done. The second part presents the evaluation of the energy 
performance indicator (mainly ventilation consumption) against the IAQ indicator (CO2 
concentrations). 
==PAGE:42==
4 Part A: Development of new key figures using simulation data 
28 
 
Energy consumption against thermal comfort 
Key figures were developed by investigating a correlation between the energy performance 
indicators (energy per unit area) and thermal comfort indicators (Method A and Method B) that 
were described in previous sections. Heating and cooling periods were investigated separately 
where relevant.  
Firstly, a key figure (2D) was plotted where primary energy consumption indicator in 
connection with thermal comfort indicator for Method A was investigated. Monthly primary 
heating energy consumption (Qh) was plotted against the monthly percentage of occupied 
hours when the operative temperature fell below the recommended lower temperature limit of 
Category II (20 °C). For the cooling season, monthly primary cooling energy consumption (Qc) 
was plotted against the monthly percentage of occupied hours when the operative temperature 
exceeded the recommended upper limit of Category II (26 °C). As still acceptable violation of 
temperature boundary limits of Category II, 5 % recommended maximum exceedance limit was 
applied according to the recommendation described in Annex G. It was assumed that if the 
simulated temperatures were below the lower (20 °C) or above the upper temperature limit (26 
°C) of Category II for maximum 5 % of occupied hours, this still referred to an acceptable yet 
tolerable violation of thermal comfort boundaries. 
Secondly, a key figure (2D) was computed where monthly Qh and Qc against thermal comfort 
indicator for Method B was plotted. For the heating season, monthly Qh was plotted against 
monthly heating degree hours (HDH) for different scenarios. For the cooling season, monthly Qc 
was plotted against monthly cooling degree hours (CDH) for different simulation scenarios. As 
an acceptable violation for thermal comfort, 13 DH limit value was applied according to the 
developed Improvement Method for Method A and B (page 26, Table 4). The coloured text was 
used to indicate the monthly values where the values for Method A exceeded their 
recommended limit of 5 % (evaluation of results for Method A).  
Thirdly, a key figure (3D) was used to combine energy performance indicator with both 
indicators of thermal comfort; Method A and Method B. The developed key figure combined 
information about energy performance and indoor thermal comfort; frequency (Method A) and 
severity (Method B) of the aggravated thermal comfort. For heating and cooling season 
respectively was shown on x-axis monthly percentage of occupied hours when the operative 
temperature dropped below the lower (20 °C) or exceeded the upper temperature limit (26 °C), 
on y-axis monthly HDH and CDH value and on z-axis monthly Qh and Qc. The recommended 
limit values were used; for Method A according to Annex G (recommended limit value of 5 %) 
and for Method B according to the Improvement method for Method A and B (recommended 
monthly DH limit value of 13 DH). 
Whether there were exceeded recommended limit values for Method A or Method B, the 
monthly values were sorted into four quadrants. I. quadrant represents the monthly values 
where the recommended values for Method A and Method B were not exceeded. It shows 
months where the installed HVAC system with its monthly consumption value (Qh or Qc) 
provided good thermal comfort. II. quadrant presents months where the monthly results for 
Method A exceeded the recommended limit value of 5 % (Annex G). If a monthly value was 
found in III. quadrant, both recommended values for Method A and B were exceeded. It 
represents months where the installed HVAC system with its consumption value (Qh or Qc) was 
==PAGE:43==
4 Part A: Development of new key figures using simulation data 
29 
 
not capable of maintaining thermal comfort. Monthly values where both recommended limits of 
Method A and B were exceeded, were marked with coloured text (red or blue) indicating 
aggravated thermal comfort. If a monthly value was found in IV. quadrant then the 
recommended value for Method B was exceeded (recommended limit value of 13 DH). 
Finally, a key figure (3D) was developed that combined information about energy performance, 
indoor thermal comfort and outdoor conditions. It presents monthly Qh and Qc against monthly 
HDH and CDH (Method B) and against corresponding monthly heating degree-days (HDD) and 
monthly cooling degree-days (CDD) for the heating and cooling season respectively. Instead of 
presenting monthly degree-days indicator, monthly average outdoor temperature indicator can 
be used as an indicator presenting outdoor conditions. Furthermore, indicator for Method A can 
be used as thermal comfort indicator instead of presenting indicator described in Method B.  
Energy consumption against IAQ 
The development of key figures for joint assessment of energy performance and IAQ included 
four key figure proposals. As an energy performance indicator, primary ventilation 
consumption (Qv) showing electricity usage for fan operation was investigated for the 
simulated scenarios.  
Firstly, a key figure (2D) was developed where monthly Qv against an IAQ indicator showing 
monthly average CO2 concentrations was computed. The aim was to observe how IAQ changes 
with changing ventilation consumption. It can be used for the comparison of a large number of 
buildings or to give a general overview on how much a particular building spends on ventilation 
and how this affects a building’s IAQ in average.  
Secondly, a key figure (2D) was computed where monthly Qv against monthly percentage of 
occupied hours when CO2 concentrations exceeded the recommended CO2 limit of Category II 
(900 ppm for simulated scenarios) was plotted. According to Annex G, this exceedance should 
not be more than 5 % of the occupied time. It can be investigated whether the current 
ventilation system is efficient and capable of maintaining good IAQ. This method can be used to 
evaluate the frequency of the occurrence and indicates stuffiness of the air. Furthermore, this 
key figure can be used to observe whether changing the ventilation system from CAV to VAV 
results in energy savings whilst maintaining sufficient air quality.  
Similar to established DH limit value (developed in Improvement method for Method A and B) 
used for thermal comfort evaluation according to Method B, a method was established for IAQ 
evaluation. The developed method, called CO2 exceedance, presents the severity of exceedance 
above the recommended CO2 limit, defined by Category II. The calculation process is presented 
in Equation 3.  
                            
 
                         
   
) where CO2 measured > CO2 limit              (Eq. 3) 
The presented CO2 exceedance shows the difference between the exceeded and the 
recommended CO2 limit value once the (simulated or measured) CO2 concentrations exceed the 
recommended CO2 limit value of the defined category of standard EN 15251 (Equation 3). Like 
in Improvement method for Method A and B for thermal comfort evaluation, a recommended 
maximum CO2 exceedance limit was defined for the average number of monthly occupied hours. 
==PAGE:44==
4 Part A: Development of new key figures using simulation data 
30 
 
The recommended monthly CO2 exceedance limit value was calculated for the maximum 
recommended 5 % exceedance during occupied hours (Annex G recommendation). It was 
assumed that when the recommended CO2 limit is exceeded, the hourly (simulated or 
measured) CO2 exceedance should not be larger than 100 ppm (tolerable violation).  
Average monthly occupied hours value was calculated for the occupied hours of the simulated 
building (opening hours during weekdays between 6h–18h). For a month with 260 occupied 
hours 5 % of occupied hours violation leads to 1300 ppm as seen from Table 5. As a maximum 
recommended monthly CO2 exceedance limit value was therefore defined value of 1300 ppm.  
Table 5: CO2 exceedance - Calculation method for recommended monthly CO2 exceedance limit value for 260 
average monthly occupied hours.

As a third key figure (2D), monthly Qv was computed against monthly CO2 exceedance above the 
recommended CO2 limit for Category II (900 ppm for simulated scenarios). The recommended 
monthly CO2 exceedance limit of 1300 ppm was used as defined in Table 5. 
The fourth key figure (3D) presents a plot where the monthly Qv was plotted against two 
indicators for IAQ: monthly percentage of occupied hours when (simulated or measured) CO2 
concentrations exceeded the recommended CO2 limit of Category II and against the value of 
monthly CO2 exceedance. In the case of building’s design such key figure can help making a 
decision when optimizing the ventilation system. It might be that current design of a ventilation 
system (CAV type) does not present an optimized energy efficient solution. A new building 
design scenario with changed ventilation system to VAV type with CO2 control could show 
whether this presents a more energy efficient solution while providing good air quality (CO2 
below the recommended CO2 limit) by providing higher air exchange rates only when needed.  
The recommended limit values were used; for monthly percentage of occupied hours when CO2 
exceeded the recommended CO2 limit according to Annex G (5 % recommended limit value) and 
for monthly CO2 exceedance according to the developed CO2 exceedance method (1300 ppm 
recommended monthly CO2 limit value). Whether there were exceeded recommended limit 
values for percentage value (5 %) or for monthly CO2 exceedance value (1300 ppm), the 
monthly values were sorted into four quadrants. I. quadrant represents the monthly values 
where none of the recommended limit values was exceeded and therefore shows months where 
good IAQ was provided. II. quadrant presents months where the recommended monthly 
exceedance limit value of 1300 ppm was exceeded. If a monthly value was in III. quadrant, both 
recommended values were exceeded and this indicated insufficient ventilation rates to provide 
fresh indoor air. If a monthly value was in IV. quadrant, the monthly CO2 concentrations 
exceeded the recommended CO2 limit of Category II for more than 5 % of occupied hours.

4.2 Results 
The results section of Part A presents the computation of existing performance indicators and 
developed key figures on simulation data. 
4.2.1 Energy performance indicators 
General energy use 
In Figure 3 and Table 6, results of primary energy consumption for Scenarios I, II and III are 
presented with the annual primary energy consumption of each service system. The primary 
energy was broken down into different parts. Annual heating consumption (Qh) presents 
annual district heating usage for space heating, annual cooling consumption (Qc) shows annual 
district cooling consumption used for space cooling and annual ventilation consumption (Qv) 
annual electricity consumption that was used for fan operation. Lighting presents annual 
electricity usage for lighting and DHW presents annual domestic hot water usage. It should be 
noted that primary energy takes into account primary energy factors described in chapter 4.1.1.  
In Figure 3 and Table 6 are also presented the energy performance requirements according to 
BR10 [37] for the commercial (office) buildings according to the current 2010 requirement and 
the 2015 Low energy class requirement.

4.3 Discussion 
The discussion part includes the validation of the plotted performance indicators and developed 
key figures on simulation data presented in previous chapter.  
4.3.1 Energy performance indicators 
General energy use 
Figure 3 showed the division of annual primary energy consumption for all three scenarios on 
simple bar chart where was quickly noted the difference in consumption values. These values 
were numerically presented in Table 6. As seen from Figure 3, all three design scenarios 
satisfied the current BR10 [37] 2010 requirement. The annual total primary energy 
consumption was higher than the requirement for Low energy class 2015 [37] for the simulated 
scenarios. It was observed that the annual heating consumption (Qh) was higher than the 
annual cooling consumption (Qc) which is a typical case for heating-dominated buildings; 
buildings in a cold climate as is Denmark.  
Figure 4 illustrated monthly primary heating consumption (Qh) for all three simulated 
scenarios. As seen, Qh was rising with the winter months. Furthermore, Scenarios I and III had 
same monthly Qh values whereas consumption for Scenario II was slightly lower for the winter 
months of 2014 due to the reduced heating power of the radiators.  
Figure 5 presented monthly primary cooling consumption (Qc). For all three simulated 
scenarios, the highest consumption was for the summer of 2014 (June, July and August) 
whereas for the spring and autumn of 2014 (April, May, September and October) the cooling 
need was gradually decreased. There was still significant cooling needed in spring and autumn 
of 2014 even though cooling season in Denmark was assumed 1st June–30th September. Still, 
cooling was provided whenever the operative temperature exceeded 26 °C (cooling set point). 
For Scenario III, Qc was lower than for Scenario I. Applying natural cooling of building through 
the summer nights in Scenario III reduced the demand for mechanical cooling of the building. 
There were zero values for Qc for Scenario II since no mechanical cooling was considered. 
The inspection of Figure 6 showed that the monthly primary ventilation consumption (Qv) were 
similar for all three simulated scenario through the year 2014 and varied in the range between 
1.6–1.9 kWh/m2. Al three simulated scenarios had same mechanical ventilation system (CAV, 
same ventilation rates and schedule settings). 
Energy consumption in relation to monthly degree-days  
During the literature review, it was observed that in energy management heating degree-days 
(HDD) method is commonly used for evaluation of building’s heating energy consumption and 
this was also done for the simulated Qh. As seen from Figure 7, increased number of HDD led to 
an increase in Qh in order to maintain the building's needs.  
When doing analyses for the cooling season presented in Figure 8, no strong dependency 
between cooling degree-days (CDD) and monthly Qc was observed. The calculated CDD values 
==PAGE:62==
4 Part A: Development of new key figures using simulation data 
48 
 
for the summer months (June–August 2014) were not higher than 1.5 CDD which implied that 
almost no mechanical cooling was needed for the summer of 2014. However, looking at the Qc, 
it was seen that there was a certain cooling need for these summer months for Scenarios I and 
III. Even for a Scenario III, where natural ventilation was implemented as a supplementary to a 
mechanical cooling system, a mechanical cooling was still needed. Scenario II had zero values 
for Qc since no mechanical cooling was considered. As described in ASHRAE’s Handbook [14], 
there is no linear relationship between the cooling energy consumption of the building and CDD 
value. During the literature review, no case study was found where degree-days method would 
be used solely for cooling season evaluation and energy prediction for the summer months. 
The main limitation on using CDD method for thesis purposes was that the indicators were 
developed for the modern commercial buildings where occupants, equipment and appliances 
play an important role. This increases humidity level and therefore influences latent cooling 
load [14]. As mentioned in background study, latent cooling loads are neglected in the DD 
method. Another reason for CDD method limitation is that it does not take into account solar 
gains. Nowadays, buildings are becoming more thermally insulated and therefore the indoor 
temperature does not rapidly changes with changing outdoor conditions. Furthermore, modern 
building design more emphasises implementing big glazing surfaces. Therefore, the indoor 
environment can be more influenced by the solar gains through the glazing area than solely by 
the outdoor temperatures. This was also proved when analysing simulation model results for 
the cooling season. There was needed cooling power for the spring (April, May) and autumn 
months of 2014 (September, October), however, there were zero CDD values for these months. 
This shows that in spring and autumn there can still be quite low temperatures outside leading 
to zero CDD values. However, due to high solar gains through the glazing areas and high internal 
gains, indoor temperatures exceed the cooling set point of 26 °C and there rises a demand for 
cooling. 
Regression analysis 
To study the influence of outdoor conditions (expressed as DD value) on energy consumption, 
regression analysis was used. The relationship between the Qh and HDD was quantified for the 
heating season where the residuals for a linear model (1st polynomial equation) were studied. 
For the cooling season, relationship between Qc and CDD was studied. In Table 8 are presented 
calculated values for the coefficient of determination; R2, showing goodness of the fit [52].  
Table 8: Linear regression analysis of the relationship between the monthly energy consumption and the 
monthly degree-days values for the heating and cooling season 2014 for each building scenario. For the 
heating season, this presents evaluation of the linear correlation between heating consumption (Qh) and 
monthly heating degree-days (HDD). For the cooling season, this presents the evaluation of linear correlation 
between cooling consumption (Qc) and cooling degree-days (CDD).

The fitted linear line and study of the residual plots showed that the raw data followed the given 
polynomial function better for the heating than for the cooling season for all three scenarios. R2 
==PAGE:63==
4 Part A: Development of new key figures using simulation data 
49 
 
value close to one shown in Table 8, indicated there was a good fit between the established 
linear model and the dependant variable (Qh) for the heating season. For the cooling season, 
still a moderate linear correlation between fitted model and the dependant variable (Qc) was 
found. It should be noted that R2 measures how well the fitted polynomial equation predicts the 
dependent variable. Therefore it was needed to understand the data and the relationship 
between the observed variables first in order to correctly evaluate whether the established 
linear model is appropriate and relevant for the study. It was assessed that the linear model, 
where cooling consumption is only described as function of outdoor conditions, is not 
appropriate due to the limitations of the CDD method described earlier despite the moderate 
value for R2 for the cooling season. 
Energy signature 
Figure 9 revealed that Qh was for all three simulated scenarios decreased with increased 
monthly average outdoor temperature. Analysis showed that January 2014 had higher Qh than 
February 2014 even though the monthly average outdoor temperature was lower for the month 
of February. However, it should be noted that the difference was only 0.5 °C. It could be 
observed that monthly average outdoor temperature value did not tell much about the 
frequency of certain temperature occurrence but just gave general information on how cold the 
month was.  
For the cooling season (Figure 10), it was observed that Qc was rising with higher monthly 
average outdoor temperature. As seen, even though the monthly average outdoor temperature 
for months of April and October 2014 was below 10 °C, there was still needed a certain amount 
of cooling. With the months becoming warmer (May and September 2014) also the demand for 
mechanical cooling of building arose. As could be observed from the plot in Figure 10, with 
highest average monthly outdoor temperatures for months of June, July and August 2014 also 
Qc was the highest.  
Regression analysis 
For each simulated scenario, regression analysis was performed to study the correlation 
between the Qh and Qc and the monthly average outdoor temperature for heating and cooling 
season respectively. 1st polynomial regression model was fitted. Table 9 presents the results for 
R2 values for all three simulated scenarios for the heating and cooling season.

presents the evaluation of linear correlation between cooling consumption (Qc) and monthly average 
outdoor temperature (Tout).

It could be seen that good fit between the dependant variable (Qh) and predicted model was 
obtained for all three scenarios for the winter season 2014. There could still be seen a moderate 
linear correlation between Qc and the fitted linear model for the summer of 2014 for Scenarios I 
and III even though it gave higher residual values than for the heating season. There were zero 
values for Qc for Scenario II therefore no regression analysis was performed for this case.  
Comparison of the regression analyses done for the relationship between energy consumption 
and degree-days method and energy consumption and monthly average outdoor temperature 
showed that the good fit between the predicted linear models and the dependant variables 
(consumption values) was found for the heating season (Qh). The inspection of residual plots 
and determination of R2 for the cooling season showed for the three simulated scenarios how 
cooling energy consumption cannot only be seen as a function of the outdoor conditions but also 
as a result of solar and internal gains. However, the relationship between the cooling 
consumption and the fitted model still showed a moderate linear correlation.  
4.3.2 IEQ performance indicators 
Thermal comfort indicators  
Method A (Annex F, EN 15251) 
When analysing the CDF graph of the operative temperature during occupied hours in Figure 12 
it was seen that there was only a small temperature spike above 26 °C for Scenario I (maximum 
value found at 26.2 °C). However, the analysis of the results for Method A (Table 7) for Scenario 
I showed that for 31.3 % of annual occupied hours the operative temperature exceeded 26 °C 
that was defined as the upper recommended temperature limit according to the Category II. The 
percentage of occupied hours when the operative temperature fell below the lower 
recommended temperature limit (20 °C) was 0.3 % as seen from Table 7. 
For Scenario II where simulation scenario without mechanical cooling was considered, the 
percentage of occupied hours when the operative temperature exceeded 26 °C was calculated at 
40.8 %. The analyses showed that the maximum operative temperature for Scenario II was 
reached at 33.7 °C. Results for Scenario II presented in Table 7 also revealed a problem with 
maintaining the operative temperature above 20 °C. 4.8 % of annual occupied hours had the 
operative temperature below 20 °C.  
Investigation of the operative temperature profile during occupied hours for Scenario III in 
Figure 12 showed that there were less occupied hours when the operative temperature 
exceeded 26 °C than in comparison with the occupied hours for Scenarios I and II. The operative 
temperature was maintained for most of the occupied hours inside the desired temperature 
range (20–26 °C). Temperatures exceeded 26 °C for 10.9 % of the annual occupied hours and 
temperatures were below 20 °C for 1.3 % of the annual occupied hours. As could be inspected, 
there was a higher percentage of the occupied time when the temperatures were below 20 °C 
for the Scenario III than in Scenario I. This was the result of a night-time natural ventilation 
application which led to over-cooling of the rooms during the nights. 
==PAGE:65==
4 Part A: Development of new key figures using simulation data 
51 
 
Evaluation of the results for Method A (Table 7) according to the Annex G recommendation was 
done. As described earlier, Annex G describes that thermal comfort for a specified category is 
met if the calculated percentage of occupied hours, when the temperature is outside the 
specified temperature range (Category II: 20–26 °C), is not higher than 5 %. This recommended 
limit was violated for all three simulated scenarios.  
For Scenario I, there was around 30 % of occupied hours when the operative temperature 
exceeded the boundary limit of 26 °C whereas for Scenario II this percentage value was around 
40 %. From results for Method A it could be concluded that the overheating problem for both 
scenarios was serious.  
 
Assessment: As already discussed, the indicator defined by Method A presents frequency of 
thermal discomfort. However, the indicator itself lacks presenting information about the 
severity of the overheating problem which would reveal more information about the actual 
thermal discomfort of the occupants. This issue will be further addressed in next section; 
Analysis of the indicators for Long-term evaluation of the general thermal comfort conditions. 
Method B (Annex F, EN 15251) 
Analysis of the results for Method B showing monthly HDH (Figure 13) showed that Scenario I 
had lowest HDH values during the winter months of 2014 (January, February and December) in 
comparison with the other two simulated scenarios. Lowering the design heating power of the 
radiators in Scenario II led to a significant operative temperature drop and therefore an 
increase in the number of HDH. January’s HDH value for Scenario II was increased from 0.5 
(Scenario I) to 37.6. For Scenario II, using natural ventilation through the whole year resulted in 
a higher value of monthly HDH for the coldest months. The reason lies in the natural ventilation 
schedule. During the winter months, operative temperature exceeded 24.5 °C during occupied 
hours due to internal and solar gains. Whenever the temperature stayed above this temperature 
limit at 18h, this activated window opening. Windows were opened until the occupants came 
back to work in the morning. Temperatures fell below the desired minimum temperature of 20 
°C (even in March 2014) and these resulted in high HDH values for Scenario III. If the aim of the 
study was optimal building design, further investigation of the window opening schedule should 
be done in order to solve this problem.  
For the cooling season, monthly CDH values were observed when the operative temperature 
exceeded the upper limit of Category II (26 °C). Figure 14 showed that for Scenario II, thermal 
comfort of the occupants was aggravated during the summer months when no mechanical or 
natural cooling of the building was provided. Figure 15 showed that the lowest CDH values were 
found for Scenario III where natural ventilation was provided for a specific window opening 
schedule. This led to a lower number of CDH in summer in comparison to the CDH for Scenario I.  
Assessment: Having no recommended upper boundary limit for monthly DH from standard EN 
15251 made it hard to evaluate whether the calculated monthly DH values represent a minor or 
significant overheating problem. This will be further discussed in next section; Analysis of the 
indicators for Long-term evaluation of the general thermal comfort conditions. 
 
==PAGE:66==
4 Part A: Development of new key figures using simulation data 
52 
 
Method C (Annex F, EN 15251) 
Evaluation of the results presented for Method C in Figure 16 showed that for Scenario II (no 
mechanical cooling), the weighted PPD value for June 2014 was more than 10 times higher than 
for same month in Scenario I. For Scenario II, higher PPD values were also obtained for winter 
months due to the reduced heating power of the radiators compared to Scenario I. Due to the 
natural cooling of the building for Scenario III through the summer nights, weighted PPD values 
were lower for the summer months but higher during the winter months than compared to 
Scenario I. For Scenario III occupants felt cold in winter, most likely due to over-cooling of the 
building by natural ventilation.  
Assessment: Evaluation of the diagrams in Figure 16 did not reveal information on whether 
occupants were feeling hot or cold. Weighted PPD values have to be analysed in relation to PMV 
values in order to investigate whether occupants are too warm or too cold. One could assume 
that a PPD value in summer represents occupants feeling too hot and in winter too cold. 
However, as seen from results for Method A and Method B there can still be overcooling during 
summer and overheating during winter.  
Analysis of the indicators for Long-term evaluation of general thermal comfort 
conditions (Annex F, EN 15251) 
When making a decision on what type of IEQ performance indicator should be used for the joint 
assessment of energy consumption and indoor environment and to study trends over a long 
period of time, methods described in Annex F of standard EN 15251 seemed suitable. However, 
one must be aware of the method’s limitations. Once indicators were tested on simulation data, 
several limitations discovered during literature review were proved.  
PMV thermal model 
There are several criticisms of Fanger’s PMV model used in the applied methods of Annex F, EN 
15251 due to different building types, geographical location and model input parameters [49, 
56, 57]. Yang et al. [56] argue that the PMV model works quite well for air-conditioned buildings 
(HVAC system), but not for naturally ventilated buildings because it only partly accounts for 
thermal adaptation to the indoor environment. An applied PMV thermal model is a function of 
certain metabolic rate and occupant’s clothing [56]. People adapt to indoor conditions and may 
sense the warmth as less severe by changing their clothing or by acclimatization to conditions 
[48]. Exceeding the strict boundary conditions of thermal discomfort according to the standard 
does therefore not necessary lead to occupant thermal dissatisfaction.  
Wagner et al. [28] did a case study where they argue that having a fixed limit (boundary 
condition) for the acceptable indoor temperature for naturally ventilated or passively cooled 
buildings does not correspond to the subjective votes of building occupants but rather with a 
temperature range which depends on the outdoor temperature. Therefore, a comparison of 
thermal comfort for these two types of buildings should be done with caution. In 2010, the 
standard ASHRAE 55 [58] was revised to include an adaptive model for naturally ventilated 
buildings. By implementing an adaptive approach, it is assumed occupants are more 
comfortable in a wider range of conditions than the conditions prescribed by applying the PMV 
==PAGE:67==
4 Part A: Development of new key figures using simulation data 
53 
 
index. This approach is also presented in Annex A.2 “Acceptable indoor temperatures for design 
of buildings without mechanical cooling system” of standard EN 15251 [38]. 
In recent years, all commercial buildings have been equipped with HVAC systems and the 
developed key figures were mainly intended for the joint assessment analyses of this building 
type. Furthermore, none of the simulated building scenarios presented a building that was 
solely naturally ventilated therefore PMV model was used while being aware of the limitations. 
Recommendation for the future work includes proposal for integration of the developed key 
figures for naturally ventilated buildings where opening of windows is possible by occupants. 
Joint assessment analysis of Method A and Method B 
As Annex G of standard EN 15251 describes building meets the criterion of the selected 
category if inside the limits for at least 95 % of the normal time of building use (occupied 
hours). For the evaluation of results for Method A this referred to an acceptable yet tolerable 
violation of the operative temperature outside the specified range for Category II (20–26 °C). 
However, the recommendation according to Annex G cannot be easily applied for the evaluation 
of the results according to Method B. No limit degree hours value was found that would indicate 
a still acceptable violation of the thermal comfort according to Method B from the standard EN 
15251 [38]. It was either concluded that local discomfort exists (non-zero DH values) or not 
(zero DH values) depending whether the temperature boundary limits defined for Category II 
(20–26 °C) were exceeded or not. No tolerable violation limit value was identified for results for 
Method B.  
In this paragraph, annual evaluation was done for results for Method A and Method B in order 
to investigate the severity of the thermal discomfort for the simulated scenarios. The annual 
occupied period when the operative temperature was outside the specified temperature range 
for Category II (20–26 °C) was investigated. The observation of results for Method A showed 
that the percentage of occupied hours when the temperature was outside the specified range 
was quite similar in the case of Scenarios I and II. This revealed the frequency of the situation 
when the temperature was outside the boundaries of the Category II. There were approximately 
10 % more occupied hours of thermal discomfort for Scenario II.  
In order to evaluate the severity of the thermal discomfort, the Ratio for Method B method was 
developed (Equation 4). The annual degree hours value (annual results for Method B) was 
normalized according to the annual occupied hours for each building. For the simulated 
scenarios, there was 3119 annual occupied hours.  
                                                                          
                   
                                                                      (Eq. 4) 
A recommended limit value for the Ratio for Method B was derived from the recommended limit 
according to Annex G. When using 5 % value as a percentage limit value, this leads to a ratio 
limit value of 0.05. Table 10 presents the annual percentage of occupied hours when the 
operative temperature was outside the specified temperature range for Category II (results for 
Method A), annual degree hours (results for Method B), calculated Ratio for Method B (Equation 
4) and recommended ratio limit value (0.05) for Ratio for Method B for each building scenario 
for 2014.

The evaluation of the values for Ratio for Method B presented in Table 10 showed that the 
severity of the occurrence, when the operative temperature was outside the specified range of 
Category II, was much larger for Scenario II (ratio value 1.09) than in comparison with 
Scenarios I and III (ratio value 0.0024–0.0026). As seen, the recommended limit of 0.05 was 
exceeded for Scenario II only, implying a severe problem with the temperatures outside the 
range. Scenario II had a reduced heating capacity and no mechanical cooling which led to an 
aggravated thermal comfort (maximum temperature found at 33.7 °C). 
For Scenario I, operative temperature was outside the specified range of Category II 31.6 % of 
occupied hours and in Scenario III only 12.2 %. For Scenario I, there were more occupied hours 
(Method A) when the operative temperature was outside the specified range, however, these 
occupied hours had low degree hours values (Method B) leading to low Ratio for Method B value 
(maximum temperature found at 26.2 °C). 
As seen, the Ratio for Method B was similar for Scenarios I and III. Once the operative 
temperature exceeded the specified temperature limits of Category II (20–26 °C) for Scenario I, 
the temperatures stayed slightly below the lower or above the upper temperature limit and did 
not reach any extreme temperature peaks. As discovered, the cooling set point for Scenario I 
was 26 °C which is also the upper temperature limit of Category II. The cooling system was 
activated once the operative temperature reached 26 °C. Due to a certain time lag between 
sensors measuring the operative temperature reaching 26 °C and the activation of the cooling, 
operative temperature slightly exceeded the set point of 26 °C. When doing the evaluation of 
thermal comfort according to Category II, even slight exceedance above 26 °C is recognized as 
thermal discomfort (strict boundary conditions for evaluation) resulting in high values for the 
results of Method A (frequency of the occurrence).  
During the development of Improvement method for Method A and B, it was discussed the 
importance to evaluate the severity of the thermal discomfort situation. The analysis in this 
section showed that it is needed to evaluate thermal comfort according to both methods; 
Method A and Method B. It was proven that the results for Method A give information about the 
frequency of thermal discomfort situation when the temperatures are outside the desired 
temperature range. However, only by analysing the results for Method B one gets insight in the 
severity of the thermal discomfort situation. For the joint assessment of energy consumption 
and IEQ was therefore decided to use both methods; Method A and Method B with the 
recommended limit value of 13 DH as defined in Improvement method for Method A and B. 
==PAGE:69==
4 Part A: Development of new key figures using simulation data 
55 
 
Limitations of Method C 
The usability of this method was tested on results from simulation models. Hence, since not all 
of the needed environmental parameters are commonly measured through the BMS (air 
temperature, mean radiant temperature, air velocity and air humidity), this method could not 
be used for the analyses of the BMS data from existing buildings. Additional measurements 
would be needed if the aim was to use Method C for a real building’s performance evaluation. 
Furthermore, in practice it is hard to estimate an occupant’s activity and their clothing which is 
necessary information when using the PMV model.  
The graphical visualization of the results for this indicator did not show potential for the 
development of new key figures where joint assessment of energy and IEQ performance 
indicators was done on a single plot. It was decided to not use this method for further 
investigation. Evaluation of thermal comfort according to Method A and Method B showed 
better potential for assessment and interpretation of the thermal discomfort and also possible 
applicability with an energy performance indicator on a single graphical plot with a fixed scale.  
IAQ indicators 
Figure 17 revealed that the CO2 concentrations were below the recommended CO2 limit of 
Category II (900 ppm) for more than 95 % of the occupied time. 
Analysis of the plot showing monthly average CO2 concentrations in Figure 18 showed that for 
Scenarios I and II the monthly average concentrations varied from 720 ppm to 810 ppm. For 
Scenario III, lower concentrations were achieved during the summer of 2014 due to the natural 
ventilation application. Natural ventilation of the office spaces not only solved the problem with 
overheating but also improved the IAQ by providing air exchange with the outdoor air.

indicators

Energy consumption against indicator for Method A 
For the heating season of 2014, Figure 19 revealed that for Scenario I the simulated monthly Qh 
values led to a low monthly percentage of occupied hours when the operative temperature fell 
below the lower temperature limit of Category II (20 °C). For the winter months of 2014 
(January, February and December) these percentage values were around 2 % with monthly Qh 
between 2–3 kWh/m2. 
As seen, reducing the heating power of the radiators to half for the winter months for Scenario II 
led to a slight decrease of Qh but at the same time also to a higher percentage of occupied hours 
when the temperatures fell below the lower limit of Category II (20 °C). For Scenario II, the 
recommended limit of Annex G (5 %) was exceeded for most of the observed months. For 
Scenario II, the percentage value for January 2014 was increased to 13.9 % of occupied hours 
instead of 1.5 % (Scenario I) with January’s energy saving of 0.2 kWh/m2. Similar was for the 
==PAGE:70==
4 Part A: Development of new key figures using simulation data 
56 
 
months of December and February 2014. Reduction of the power of the radiators led to a poor 
building’s design where no significant energy saving was obtained; however, thermal comfort 
was aggravated.  
Comparison between Scenarios I and III showed that the same monthly Qh values were 
obtained for the winter months where the thermal comfort was slightly worse in the case of 
Scenario III. The percentage of occupied hours below 20 °C was for the month of January for 
Scenario III increased to 5.5 % instead of 1.5 % (Scenario I). This increase in percentage value 
was most likely caused by the natural ventilation application for Scenario III which only affected 
thermal comfort and not energy consumption.  
General observation of Figure 20 showed that for all three simulated scenarios, the current 
cooling system (with its Qc) did not satisfy the thermal comfort recommended limit of 5 % 
established for Method A. Further analyses of Figure 20 for the cooling season showed that the 
Scenario I had highest monthly Qc values for the summer months of June, July and August of 
2014 (1.4–1.9 kWh/m2) in comparison with the other two simulated scenarios. However, this 
did not lead to the lowest monthly percentage of occupied hours when the operative 
temperature exceeded 26 °C. It could be observed that for Scenario I the operative temperature 
exceeded the maximum desired temperature of 26 °C for more than 50 % of the summer 
occupied hours (July and August 2014). 
For Scenario II it was observed that implementing no natural or mechanical cooling of the 
building (zero values for Qc) led to a significant increase in the percentage of occupied hours 
when the operative temperature exceeded 26 °C. It was observed that for the months of June 
and July 2014 the operative temperature was above 26 °C during all the occupied hours (100 % 
value). 
For Scenario III, a natural ventilation application led to a decrease in energy consumption spent 
on mechanical cooling in comparison with Scenario I (for July 0.8 kWh/m2 Qc saving obtained). 
Moreover, this also improved the thermal comfort while decreasing the percentage of occupied 
hours when the operative temperature exceeded the desired maximum temperature of 26 °C 
(for July instead 52.9 % only 29.5 %).  
Energy consumption against indicator for Method B  
Figure 21 revealed that Qh values were similar for all three scenarios where Scenario I had the 
lowest monthly HDH values. Comparison of results for Scenarios I and II showed that Qh was 
slightly decreased for the coldest months for Scenario II (January, February and December 
2014). This resulted in much larger HDH values for these months where the recommended limit 
value of 13 DH was exceeded. The decrease of the heating power of radiators did not provide 
significant energy savings but mostly aggravated the thermal comfort. The red coloured text for 
Scenario II showed that for most of the winter months of 2014 (January–March and November–
December) the percentage value exceeded the recommended 5 % value (results for Method A). 
However, the recommended DH value was only exceeded for the months of January, February 
and December 2014. 
Comparison of results for Scenarios I and III showed that the same Qh was obtained for the 
months of December and January 2014. However, Scenario III had higher monthly HDH values. 
==PAGE:71==
4 Part A: Development of new key figures using simulation data 
57 
 
This was the result of overcooling of the building due to the application of natural ventilation. 
For all the investigated months, monthly HDH values did not exceed the recommended DH limit 
value of 13 DH whereas the recommended percentage value of 5 % was exceeded for the month 
of January (red coloured text). This implied that when the operative temperature dropped 
below 20 °C, the temperatures stayed closely to the boundary temperature of 20 °C (mainly 19.8 
°C). 
The inspection of Figure 22 revealed that the highest Qc was obtained for Scenario I while 
Scenario III had the lowest monthly CDH. The blue coloured text showed that for all the 
scenarios the recommended percentage of occupied hours when the temperature exceeded 26 
°C exceeded the recommended limit of 5 % (Annex G). Only for October of 2014 for Scenario III 
this value was not exceeded. However, the DH evaluation showed that the recommended DH 
limit of 13 DH was only exceeded for Scenario II. It was established that more attention should 
be brought to the months for which the recommended DH limit of 13 DH was exceeded since 
this indicates the severity of the overheating problem. 
For Scenario I, no natural ventilation was assumed and cooling of the building was provided 
only by the installed mechanical cooling system. The highest Qc values were obtained for the 
summer months while having a low number of CDH. The blue text indicated that for all the 
investigated months the percentage value for Method A exceeded the recommended value of 5 
%. However, monthly CDH values did not exceeded the recommended limit value of 13 DH for 
any of the investigated months. It indicated that operative temperature often exceeded the 
upper temperature limit of Category II (26 °C) for Scenario I but these temperatures stayed 
mostly just above the upper limit of Category II, with a maximum temperature found at 26.2 °C. 
It was assessed that despite the high percentage value calculated for Method A, the overheating 
problem was not severe since monthly DH values did not exceed the recommended limit value 
of 13 DH.  
Analysis of the cooling season for Scenario II showed that going without any mechanical cooling 
(Qc had zero values) resulted in high values for monthly CDH. It was seen that the 
recommended value of 13 DH was exceeded for all the summer months for Scenario II.  
With opening windows during the summer nights (Scenario III), the demand for mechanical 
cooling was lowered and thermal comfort was improved. This led to less CDH during the 
summer months of 2014 for Scenario III than in comparison with Scenario I. By opening 
windows through nights during the hottest months (June, July and August) Qc was reduced 
significantly (for July 60 %); a smaller reduction was also observed for warm months (May and 
September). Furthermore, the recommended DH value was not exceeded for any of these 
months indicating good thermal comfort.  
Energy consumption against indicator for Method A and against indicator for 
Method B  
Analyses of the 3D plot presented in Figure 23 showed that Qh was pretty similar for all three 
simulation scenarios through the winter months of 2014 (1.8–2.8 kWh/m2). It was seen that for 
Scenario I neither the recommended 5 % limit value (Method A evaluation) nor the 
recommended DH limit value of 13 DH (Method B evaluation) were exceeded and the monthly 
values were therefore put in the I. quadrant. This proved that the designed heating system with 
==PAGE:72==
4 Part A: Development of new key figures using simulation data 
58 
 
its heating consumption in Scenario I was capable of providing good thermal comfort for the 
occupants (maintaining desired minimum indoor temperature). 
For Scenario II, lower Qh values were obtained than for Scenario I where the thermal comfort 
was aggravated for the months of January, February and December 2014. Both recommended 
limit values for Method A and Method B were exceeded and therefore values were categorized 
in III. quadrant implying aggravated thermal comfort. For March and November 2014, the 
percentage value for Method A exceeded the recommended 5 % but not the recommended DH 
limit of 13 DH and the values were put in II. quadrant. 
Same monthly Qh was obtained for Scenarios I and III. However, for Scenario III, the percentage 
of occupied hours when the operative temperature fell below the lower limit of 20 °C was 
higher than for Scenario I. Still, for almost all the observed months the values were categorized 
in I. quadrant which indicated good thermal comfort. Only for the month of January the 
percentage value according to Method A exceeded the recommended limit value of 5 % but for 
Method B the recommended DH limit value was not exceeded (II. quadrant). 
From Figure 24 it was observed that for Scenario I, a certain Qc was needed in order to maintain 
the operative temperature below 26 °C. Despite the mechanical cooling, the percentage of 
occupied hours when the operative temperature was above 26 °C exceeded the recommended 
limit value of 5 % for all the summer months (II. quadrant). However, the recommended value 
of DH was not exceeded, for any of the investigated months.  
For Scenario II, where no mechanical cooling was considered (zero consumption values), 
thermal comfort was aggravated for almost all the occupied hours through the summer months 
of June, July and August 2014. Analyses of the monthly results showed that for June and July the 
percentage of hours when the operative temperature exceeded the upper temperature limit of 
Category II (26 °C) was 100 % which also resulted in high a CDH value (around 1000 CDH). It 
was seen that for all the observed months, the recommended limit values for Method A (5 %) 
and Method B (13 DH) were exceeded and monthly values were found in the III. quadrant. This 
implied that certain mechanical (or natural) cooling was needed in order to provide good 
thermal comfort of the occupants in the summer of 2014.  
Lower Qc was needed to provide better thermal comfort for the occupants in summer for 
Scenario III than in comparison with Scenario I and II. This was due to the natural ventilation 
application through summer nights besides utilizing the mechanical cooling of the building. 
Even though the recommended value of CDH was not exceeded for any of the summer months 
for Scenario III, the percentage of occupied hours for Method A still exceeded the recommended 
value for most of the months and values were found in II. quadrant. This showed that the 
percentage of occupied hours when the operative temperature was outside the specified range 
should be investigated along with the DH values and using the DH limit as proposed in the 
developed Improvement method for Method A and B (page 26, Table 4).  
Energy consumption against thermal comfort ( Method B) and against outdoor 
condition indicator (DD method) 
Observation of the plot presented in Figure 25 revealed that with colder months in 2014 
(increased HDD value for January, February and December), a demand for heating was 
==PAGE:73==
