59 
 
proportionally increased and therefore primary Qh. Depending on the capabilities of the 
designed heating system, the operative temperature was differently maintained above the lower 
temperature limit of Category II (20 °C). It was observed that with higher HDD values, HDH 
values were also increased; slightly even for Scenario I. This implied that despite designing a 
building that was thermally insulated, the installed windows had good thermal properties and 
the heating system was designed accordingly to the building’s needs, the indoor environment 
was to some degree still influenced by the outdoor conditions 
Comparison done for Scenarios I and II in Figure 26 for the summer months of June, July and 
August 2014 showed how was with certain monthly mechanical cooling (non-zero consumption 
values for Scenario I) the number of CDH decreased for a constant CDD value. Once natural 
cooling was applied, the mechanical cooling consumption and cooling degree hours were 
decreased (Scenario III). It was observed that even though August 2014 was the hottest month 
(highest CDD value) this did not lead to the highest CDH value. It was seen that June 2014 had a 
CDD value of almost zero; however, the CDH value was high. This could be caused by solar and 
internal gains and was not solely influenced by the outdoor temperatures. As already discussed, 
a cooling need does not only depend on the outdoor conditions but is also affected by internal 
gains: occupants, equipment; especially for well insulated commercial buildings. This could also 
be seen from the analysis of 3D plot presented in Figure 26. 
Energy consumption against IAQ 
Figure 27 showed that Qv was the same for all three simulated scenarios; however, the 
scenarios did not have the same monthly average CO2 concentrations. The natural ventilation 
application in Scenario III resulted in lower monthly average CO2 concentrations. Even though 
the open plan office served as working space for 13 occupants, it seemed that the mechanical 
ventilation system with its ventilation rates was able to maintain good IAQ for all three 
simulated scenarios on average. 
Figure 28 showed that for most of the months for all three scenarios such ventilation rates 
(resulting in certain Qv) were efficient and the CO2 concentrations were below the 
recommended CO2 limit of Category II for more than 95 % of the occupied time. In order to 
provide sufficient air exchange, design ventilation rates for the occupied space were calculated 
according to standard EN 15251 (equation in Appendix B) for the number of occupants in the 
room. Detailed evaluation of the key figure presented in Figure 28 showed that the 
recommended 5 % limit value was only exceeded for months of July and October for Scenarios I 
and II. It was seen that once natural ventilation was applied in Scenario III, Qv (and the 
ventilation rates) stayed the same but the percentage of occupied hours above 900 ppm was 
decreased. That indicated that better air quality was provided when utilizing natural ventilation 
as part of a building’s cooling system.  
Figure 29 allowed inspection of the severity of CO2 exceedance above the CO2 limit of Category II 
(900 ppm). It was seen that the recommended CO2 exceedance limit value of 1300 ppm was only 
exceeded for the months of July and October 2014 for Scenarios I and II. Still, these months had 
among highest Qv values. The green coloured text also showed that for these months the 
percentage of hours when CO2 exceeded 900 ppm, was higher than 5 %. It was seen that once 
==PAGE:74==
4 Part A: Development of new key figures using simulation data 
60 
 
the natural ventilation of building was applied (Scenario III) there were zero values for the CO2 
exceedance for all the months while still having the same consumption values. 
The 3D plot presented in Figure 30 revealed that poor IAQ was inspected for Scenarios I and II 
for the months of July and October 2014 since these monthly values were categorized in III. 
quadrant even though they had highest Qv values. For all the simulated scenarios, most of the 
monthly values were sorted into I. quadrant. It could be assessed that for these months, good 
IAQ was provided for the given monthly Qv (1.6–1.9 kWh/m2). 
4.3.4 Assessment of the developed methodology and key figures on 
simulation data 
Quality and usability of the developed key figures 
Fairly efficient and easy analyses of the plotted key figures on simulation data were established. 
It was assessed that evaluation of a building’s yearly performance can be done by presenting 
monthly energy and IEQ related performance indicators on a single plot; developed key figures. 
Established evaluation criteria (5 %, 13 DH and 1300 ppm) allowed evaluation of the indoor 
environment, implying whether a building provides good IEQ with its energy consumption.  
As discovered, due to a wealth of information that the figure holds (2 or 3 types of data types, 
computed indicator values, recommended limits, division of the values into different quadrants 
and coloured text) the readability of the developed key figures can be occasionally difficult. The 
quality of the graphical visualization features can be further improved where the developed 3D 
plots can be presented in a more interactive way. 
It was assessed, that when doing a joint assessment of energy performance and thermal comfort 
over a longer period of time (month, heating and cooling season or year), thermal comfort 
should be evaluated according to the methods of Annex F, EN 15251 [38]: Method A and Method 
B. Results for Method A reveal the frequency of occurrences when the temperatures are outside 
the specified temperature range for a chosen category. However, results for Method B hold 
information on the severity of the thermal discomfort; whether are temperatures significantly 
far or close to the boundary temperature limits (for Category II: 20–26 °C). Since no upper 
recommended limit for the degree hours method (Method B) is introduced by standard EN 
15251, the Improvement method for Method A and B (page 26, Table 4) was developed with its 
upper recommended monthly DH limit. Using Improvement method for Method A and B proved 
to be useful and beneficial in order to perform evaluation of IEQ related problems (severity). It 
was decided to use this method in Part B where the developed key figures were tested on data 
from real buildings.  
Similarly, for the simultaneous evaluation of energy performance and IAQ, ventilation 
consumption should be investigated along with the evaluation of occurrence and severity of CO2 
exceedance above the recommended CO2 limit. Such IAQ evaluation method includes the 
percentage of occupied hours when CO2 exceeds the recommended CO2 limit of a specific 
category and the monthly CO2 exceedance value (in ppm) above this limit. This way, it can be 
assessed good IAQ for the occupants maintained with the provided HVAC system.  
==PAGE:75==
4 Part A: Development of new key figures using simulation data 
61 
 
The developed 3D plots (Figure 23, Figure 24 and Figure 30) showed whether a building had a 
problem with IEQ for a certain month by sorting the monthly values into four quadrants. As 
described, monthly values found in III. quadrant deserve closer inspection since this implies an 
aggravated IEQ. As seen from analyses done on simulation data, it can be assessed whether the 
installed HVAC (with its monthly energy consumption) is efficient and provides good IEQ. A 
monthly value in the III. quadrant demonstrates poorly utilized energy consumption due to 
aggravated inspected IEQ. It can be further investigated what the reason for aggravated indoor 
environment is despite the high corresponding energy consumption for the investigated month. 
Furthermore different category of standard EN 15251 can be applied, for the evaluation of IEQ 
related problems if reasonable (Category I: for high level of expectation or Category III: 
moderate level of expectation).  
Using a 3D plot where energy consumption, IEQ and outdoor conditions are presented on a 
same plot seems to show a potential and should be further investigated. Including the outdoor 
condition indicator into the joint assessment analyses allows studies of a building’s energy and 
IEQ performance in connection with the changing outdoor conditions. For the developed key 
figures, outdoor conditions were represented with a monthly DD or monthly average outdoor 
temperature values. The limitations of using DD method were discussed earlier (chapter 4.3.1). 
Furthermore, the monthly outdoor condition indicator does not reflect day-to-day temperature 
variations and leads to a certain information loss (daily temperature spikes) when presenting a 
single monthly (average) value as an indicator of outdoor conditions. However, using monthly 
average outdoor temperature as an outdoor condition indicator still gave a certain level of 
information. It revealed how the heating and cooling consumption were changed with the 
changing outdoor conditions and how this affected the indoor environment. Further 
investigation can be done in order to study which type of indicator is most appropriate for joint 
assessment analyses showing outdoor conditions on a monthly basis.  
Over and above that, when doing a joint assessment of energy consumption and IEQ one should 
be aware that IEQ boundaries (Category II: temperature range; 20–26 °C, CO2 limit; 900 ppm) 
serve as a recommendation. In general, these limits present rigid and strict boundary 
conditions. Special attention should be brought when the established recommended limit values 
(5 %, 13 DH and 1300 ppm) are violated since this implies aggravated indoor climate. 
Room-to-building level comparison 
As discussed during the background study, caution is needed when doing a room-to-building 
level comparison. When analysing the simulation results, the office room with the worst thermal 
comfort was used as a representative model for the designed building model (Part A). When 
designing a building, it seems accurate to evaluate the room which represents a room having the 
“worst” indoor environment. Either this is done depending on the room temperature, CO2, 
relative humidity or on other particles content depends on the room type and building 
characteristics (kitchen: CO2, office with big glazing area: indoor temperature, laboratory: 
certain chemicals etc.). If after the evaluation, this room meets the requirements of chosen 
standard, it can be concluded that also rooms having better IEQ would satisfy the listed 
requirements.  
==PAGE:76==
4 Part A: Development of new key figures using simulation data 
62 
 
For a joint assessment of energy performance and indoor environment for existing buildings 
(Part B), more attention was given when deciding which office room to analyse as a 
representative. It was decided to use the measured IEQ parameters (operative temperature, 
CO2) for the different rooms that are most commonly occupied (and equipped by BMS sensors 
measuring relevant IEQ data). Calculating representative IEQ performance indicator as a mean 
of the calculated IEQ performance indicators for the most commonly occupied rooms 
individually leads to obtaining a “building’s representative IEQ” performance indicator.  
The developed key figures can be plotted to compare the building’s energy performance and 
representative mean IEQ. Such representative mean IEQ performance indicator values 
represent IEQ for a larger percentage of the overall building space (heated floor area) and 
therefore cover more of the building’s space than if using data for a single room only.  
For the existing office buildings, measured IEQ parameters for a large number of office spaces 
need to be obtained first. The “building’s representative IEQ” performance indicator is 
calculated as a mean IEQ performance indicator from the available IEQ parameters for a large 
number of office spaces. However, when the measured IEQ parameters are available only for a 
single room or zone, they can be used for further analyses while being cautious and aware that 
this represents only a small portion of total heated building floor area. In general, it is important 
to clearly state the number of occupied spaces included in IEQ evaluation, their orientation, 
room types and position of the BMS sensors in order to correctly interpret the IEQ data and 
arrive at accurate conclusions when comparing different buildings.  
In a case where an office room with significantly aggravated IEQ would be found, such room 
should furthermore deserve a careful inspection. If building diagnostics for a single building is 
the aim of the investigation, several other room types (canteen, reception etc.) can be inspected 
in order to get a more detailed evaluation. For an office building also meeting room could be 
evaluated if used often.  
Annual, seasonal and monthly evaluation time step 
As explained in chapter 4.1.3, in general, the monthly values should be presented during joint 
assessment analyses of energy and IEQ performance indicators. In this paragraph, the analysis 
was done to investigate the deviation in results when doing joint assessment analyses over 
different time periods (yearly, seasonally or monthly). 
Presented IEQ performance indicators (Method A, Method B and CO2 exceedance) showed only 
single values. For example, the annual percentage of occupied hours when the operative 
temperature was outside the specified temperature range for each simulated scenario 
presented a single number while evaluating occupied hours for the whole year. When doing 
such long-term evaluation it seemed that the longer was the observed time period, the poorer 
information such single IEQ performance indicator value held. During the analyses of results 
presented in chapter 4.2, it was observed that annual energy consumption values (Table 6) 
described energy performance behaviour better than did annual IEQ values (Table 7) the indoor 
environment.  
Table 11 and Table 12 present results of analysis done on a yearly and monthly basis. It 
presents the results for the annual primary energy consumption (Qtotal; including heating, 
==PAGE:77==
4 Part A: Development of new key figures using simulation data 
63 
 
cooling, ventilation, lighting and domestic hot water consumption) and annual percentage of 
occupied hours when the operative temperature was outside the specified range of Category II 
(20–26 °C) for the year 2014. Furthermore, tables below also include the monthly total 
consumption (Qm; including monthly heating, cooling, ventilation, lighting and domestic hot 
water consumption) and monthly percentage of occupied hours when the operative 
temperature was outside the specified range of Category II for the winter month; January 2014 
(Table 11), and the summer month; July 2014 (Table 12).

The evaluation of results for Method A (Table 11) reported thermal comfort as less aggravated 
for January 2014 than did annual results described thermal discomfort for the whole year 2014. 
The annual values implied an aggravated thermal comfort through the whole year 2014, and 
therefore implying that the operative temperature also dropped below the lower limit of 
Category II (20 °C) in winter of 2014. Actual monthly analysis showed that the occurrence when 
the operative temperature was outside the specified range (mostly below 20 °C) was less 
frequent for January 2014 than through the whole year. 
Monthly results for Method A (Table 12) showed a more frequent occurrence of the operative 
temperature outside the specified temperature range for July 2014 than compared to the annual 
results describing thermal discomfort occurrence for the year 2014.  
==PAGE:78==
4 Part A: Development of new key figures using simulation data 
64 
 
As seen, information about months facing extreme thermal discomfort (summer, winter) is lost 
when reporting annual values. Annual evaluation of results for Method A for Scenario II showed 
good thermal comfort for half of the occupied time in 2014. As discovered, thermal comfort in 
the summer months of 2014 was much worse than compared to the rest of the months. 
However, this information was available only when doing a monthly evaluation. Tremendously 
aggravated thermal comfort was inspected for July 2014 (Table 12) where there was no 
occupied hour when the operative temperature was below 26 °C for Scenario II. In general, 
monthly evaluation allows better and more detailed evaluation of IEQ. Reporting only a single 
annual value omits information about months with thermal discomfort.  
Furthermore, the comparison of monthly primary energy consumption for January and July 
2014 showed that lower consumption values were obtained in January than in July for all three 
simulated scenarios. It seemed that more energy was spent for the winter than for the summer 
months resulting in better thermal comfort. For July 2014, approximately 10 % of the annual 
energy consumption was spent whereas 12.5 % for January 2014 (for Scenario I).  
Appendix D presents similar analyses and findings as presented in Table 11 and 12 where the 
results for Method A are presented on an annually, seasonally and monthly basis. Energy used 
for space heating and cooling is presented individually. Occupied hours below the lower limit 
and above the upper temperature limit of Category II were analysed separately instead of 
presenting occupied hours when the operative temperature was outside the specified range of 
Category II.  
 “Single number” indicator 
The aim of the following analysis was to assess whether a “single number” indicator can be 
derived which combines both types of performance indicators (energy and IEQ) in a single 
value. Analysis was done where monthly and annual values were investigated in relation to 
current requirements. As a “single number” index, an overall performance ratio was introduced 
where the IEQ performance indicator (percentage of occupied hours when the operative 
temperature was inside the range of Category II (20–26 °C)) was divided with the energy 
performance indicator (building’s total energy consumption per unit area), having units: % per 
kWh/m2. Energy use included primary energy consumption for heating, cooling, ventilation, 
lighting and domestic hot water in order to coincide with the same energy uses as in the energy 
requirement. 
The investigated time periods were: January 2014, July 2014 and all year 2014. Overall 
performance ratio values were computed for these three periods for each simulated scenario. 
The overall performance ratio values were calculated by dividing the calculated percentage (%) 
of occupied hours when the operative temperature (Toper) was inside the range of Category II 
(20–26 °C) with the corresponding simulated primary energy consumption for each simulated 
scenario on monthly and yearly basis respectively. The calculation method is presented in 
Equation 5.  
                           
                                          
                                                
                                
  
 
 
                                (Eq. 5) 
==PAGE:79==
4 Part A: Development of new key figures using simulation data 
65 
 
Regarding IEQ requirements, the recommendation of Annex G [38], describing that for 95 % of 
occupied hours the operative temperature should be inside the specified range of Category II 
(20–26 °C), was used.  
Regarding energy performance requirements, current 2010 requirement of BR10 [37] was 
applied and is presented in Equation 6. 
                                    
    
      
  
 
                                                           (Eq. 6)         
It was established, that the recommended ratio value can be used for the general evaluation of 
any commercial building and therefore the building floor area was assumed as not known. To be 
on the safe side, 2010 energy requirement (Equation 6) for commercial buildings was assumed 
at 71.3 kWh/m2, despite recalculating it to the specific building’s floor area.  
In order to establish a recommended minimum ratio value for annual and monthly evaluation, 
two different recommended limit values were introduced respectively. For the annual 
evaluation, the recommended minimum annual limit ratio value of 1.3 % per kWh/m2 was used. 
This value was obtained through the calculation process presented in Equation 7. 
                   
                               
                                      
                                  
    
               
 
                    (Eq. 7)   
The annual energy requirement (71.3 kWh/m2) [37] was divided between twelve months in 
order to obtain a maximum recommended average monthly energy use. The recommended 
minimum monthly limit ratio value was computed using the approach presented in Equation 8.  
                    
                                  
                                      
                                 
    
               
 
             (Eq. 8)            
The division of annual energy framework into monthly energy framework do not represent an 
accurate interpretation of defined energy performance requirements. Hence, this was done to 
estimate a boundary limit value, used for the evaluation of the overall monthly performance 
ratio values (Equation 5).  
Figure 31 shows the overall performance ratio values for three simulated scenarios obtained by 
using Equation 5 for the whole year 2014 and for two individual months: January 2014 and July 
2014. The red and blue bars (Figure 31; top left and top right) show monthly percentage of 
occupied hours when the operative temperature was inside the specified range per monthly 
energy consumption for each simulated scenario for January and July 2014 respectively. The 
green bars on the bottom of Figure 31 present the annual percentage of occupied hours when 
the operative temperature was inside the specified range per annual energy consumption for 
each simulated scenario for the year 2014. 
All three graphs also include the recommended minimum (monthly or annual) ratio value 
according to the established limit ratio values (Equation 7 and 8). It was assumed that if the 
calculated monthly or annual overall performance ratio value was higher than the minimum 
monthly (16.1 % per kWh/m2) or minimum annual limit ratio value (1.3 % per kWh/m2), the 
investigated building had good overall performance in general for the observed period.  
==PAGE:80==
4 Part A: Development of new key figures using simulation data 
66 
 
 
Figure 31: Overall performance ratio - Ratio values showing percentage (%) of occupied hours when the 
operative temperature was inside the specified range of Category II (20–26 °C) per unit of primary energy 
consumption for the whole year 2014 (green bar chart) and specific month; January (red bar chart) and July 
2014 (blue bar chart). The horizontal dashed lines present boundary limits for the building’s overall 
performance evaluation. If the plotted bar exceeded the dashed line, it was assumed that simulated scenario 
had an overall good performance for the observed time period.  
The evaluation done for the whole year (Figure 31; bottom, green bars) showed good overall 
performance for Scenarios I and III. Furthermore, annual overall performance was better for 
Scenario III than compared to Scenario I (bar reaching further from the limit line). It was 
observed that Scenario II did not meet the minimum recommended ratio value for the overall 
building performance.  
The analysis of results for January 2014 (Figure 31; top left, red bars) showed that a good 
overall performance was assessed for Scenario III. As observed, Scenario I barely met the 
recommended criterion, yet was categorized as having good overall performance.  
For the summer month of July 2014 (Figure 31; top right, blue bars), poor overall performance 
was inspected for Scenarios I and II since the recommended minimum limit ratio value was not 
met. Either there was higher energy use than specified by the recommended monthly energy 
framework (divisor value in Equation 7) or monthly percentage of the occupied time when the 
==PAGE:81==
4 Part A: Development of new key figures using simulation data 
67 
 
operative temperature was inside the specified range of Category II was lower than the 
recommended 95 % for Scenarios I and II. As seen, the recommended criterion was met for 
Scenario III only. 
This analysis showed the possibility to evaluate the energy and IEQ performance by a “single 
number” indicator where a building’s overall performance is assessed. The main limitation of 
using the developed “single number” indicator is a loss of individual information on energy 
consumption performance and IEQ performance. Presenting only a single value for the 
building’s performance evaluation seems too broad to hold sufficient detail of information about 
the building’s actual operation. In order to conclude whether the building is operating in an 
energy efficient way, the energy performance indicator has to be investigated as an individual 
performance indicator. Similarly for the evaluation of IEQ (thermal comfort or IAQ), evaluation 
has to be done for an individual IEQ performance indicator. As shown from results for July 2014 
(Figure 31; top right, blue bars), poor overall performance was assessed for Scenarios I and II. 
However, without any additional information (or previous analyses), it is not known whether 
such performance was due to a high value for building’s annual energy consumption or due to a 
low percentage of occupied hours when the operative temperature was inside the specified 
temperature range.  
For the assessment of overall performance for the existing buildings, the predicted values 
obtained during the building’s design phase can be used. Calculation of the recommended 
minimum annual or monthly ratio limit value (Equation 7 and 8) can be done based on designed 
(simulated) values instead of using requirements (limitations due to applied monthly energy 
evaluation criterion). 
Furthermore, using developed 2D key figures (where on x-axis an IEQ and on y-axis an energy 
performance indicator is graphically presented) seem to show a more suitable approach for the 
joint assessment analyses of energy and IEQ performance indicators than using “single number” 
indicators due to the limitations discussed above.  
Energy performance in respect to IEQ performance – Integration in practice 
Nowadays, certification schemes mostly focus on energy consumption and therefore the 
existing figures mostly present energy performance indicators. As discussed during the 
literature review, IEQ evaluation is most often forgotten or done superficially in practice. The 
aim of the presented analysis was to obtain a 2D plot, where a building’s energy efficiency in 
respect to thermal comfort (or IAQ) can be evaluated on a yearly basis. 
This section presents the development of a new key figure. The objective of the proposed key 
figure is to allow for the evaluation of the building’s energy consumption according to energy 
performance requirements specified in the national building code (describing evaluation of a 
whole building on a yearly basis) whilst providing a certain amount of information about the 
building’s IEQ. It was believed, that no detailed or time consuming building operation 
diagnostics is needed in order to produce such type of key figure. The only inputs needed are 
the measured BMS energy and temperature datasets. 
==PAGE:82==
4 Part A: Development of new key figures using simulation data 
68 
 
The developed key figure (2D plot) shown in Figure 32 presents on x-axis percentage of annual 
occupied hours when the operative temperature was outside the specified range (Category II: 
20–26 °C) and on y-axis a percentage of the utilized annual energy framework. 
The percentage of annual occupied hours when the operative temperature was outside the 
range (20–26 °C) presents results for Method A for each simulated scenario for the year 2014 
(Table 7, page 37).  
The percentage of utilized annual energy framework was calculated using Equation 9 for each 
simulated scenario. The percentage of utilized annual energy framework (Equation 9) presents 
a division of the annual primary energy consumption for each simulated scenario for the year 
2014 (Table 6, page 32) with the 2010 energy requirement. The 2010 energy requirement 
according to BR10 (using Equation 6, page 65) was calculated at 73.7 kWh/m2 for the simulated 
building scenarios (A = 694 m2). 
                              
                 
 
                           
                                                                        (Eq. 9) 
The horizontal dashed line in Figure 32 shows a situation where a simulated building’s energy 
consumption exceeds 2010 energy requirement of BR10 [37]. 
 
Figure 32: Percentage of utilized annual energy framework against Percentage of annual occupied hours 
when the operative temperature was outside the range (Category II, 20–26 °C) for three simulated scenarios 
for the year 2014. The horizontal dashed line indicates a situation where the building’s (simulated or 
measured) annual energy consumption exceeded the 2010 energy requirement according to BR10 [37]. 
The evaluation of results presented in Figure 32 showed that 2010 requirement was met for all 
three simulated scenarios. Furthermore, the percentage of utilized annual energy framework 
was around 60–70 %. The percentage of utilized annual energy framework (60 %) was lowest 
for Scenario II yet this scenario had the highest percentage of annual occupied hours when the 
operative temperature was outside the range (46 %). Scenario I had higher percentage of the 
utilized annual energy framework (69 %), yet a lower percentage of annual occupied hours 
when the operative temperature was outside the specified range (32 %). Best thermal comfort 
(12 % of occupied hours when the operative temperature was outside the specified range) was 
found for Scenario III which utilized 66 % of the annual energy requirement.  
==PAGE:83==
4 Part A: Development of new key figures using simulation data 
69 
 
The plotted key figure seems to show a potential for the evaluation of a building’s energy 
efficiency in respect to IEQ. Since an annual evaluation is done, the building’s energy 
consumption (including primary energy for heating, cooling, ventilation, domestic hot water 
and lighting) can be compared to the energy performance requirement described in the national 
standard. Further investigation can be done in order to test whether the developed key figure 
presented in Figure 32 can be used in benchmarking to rank a pool of buildings. It can be 
investigated whether it can be incorporated in national certification schemes and building 
certification methods such as LEED (Leadership in Energy and Environmental Design), BREEAM 
(Building Research Establishment Environmental Assessment Method) or similar. Joint 
assessment analyses seem to allow a building’s performance assessment where building is not 
only evaluated based on its energy efficiency and use of the renewable sources but also by its 
capability of providing good indoor climate for the occupants.  
Further development of the key figure presented in this paragraph can be done where instead of 
results for Method A, results for Method B can be applied in order to study the severity of the 
thermal discomfort situation. A percentage value for the results of Method B can be calculated 
using the approach presented in the method Ratio for Method B (Equation 4, page 53) and 
multiplying the calculated ratio value by 100 %. Furthermore, a 3D plot can be developed by 
combining the developed 2D key figure (percentage of utilized energy framework and indicator 
for Method A) with an indicator showing a percentage value for Method B.  
As a recommended limit for the results for Method A and percentage value for Method B, Annex 
G recommendations can be applied (5 %). Furthermore, instead of presenting thermal comfort 
indicators, indicators for IAQ can be used (percentage of occupied hours when CO2 
concentrations are above the recommended CO2 limit and CO2 exceedance above the 
recommended CO2 limit).  
 
 
==PAGE:84==
5 Part B: Validation of the developed key figures on data from existing buildings 
70 
 
5 Part B: Validation of the developed key figures on data from existing 
buildings 
Part B presents the validation and assessment of the practical usability of the developed key 
figures on data from existing commercial buildings. These analyses were sorted into two 
groups. Group B.1 presents analyses of five commercial buildings in the Czech Republic where 
the aim was to assess the practical usability of the key figures for a large number of buildings. 
Main issues regarding the use of such simultaneous analyses for studying trends for a large 
number of buildings were exposed. Group B.2 includes the analyses of the developed key figures 
for a specific building where detailed building information was available and BMS data was 
recorded over a longer period of time (yearly datasets). The practical usability of the developed 
key figures for a continuous commissioning of a single building was assessed and their potential 
for future use in building management was assessed.  
5.1 Methodology 
The methodology chapter in Part B includes a description of the commercial buildings used in 
the analyses, characteristics of installed BMS in the investigated buildings and description of the 
analysis approach for the high-frequency BMS datasets analysis; including pre-processing of 
data. It should be mentioned that the energy consumption and IEQ analyses independently were 
not the main aim of this project but it was important to check the reliability and plausibility of 
the input data before doing the joint assessment of energy and IEQ performance indicators in 
order to correctly interpret the input data and prepare both types of data (energy and IEQ) for 
further joint analyses.  
In the end, the methodology section presents adjustments done to the key figures developed in 
Part A in order to be applicable with measured BMS datasets for each group of buildings. In 
general, the same methodology process and evaluation criteria as developed in Part A were 
followed. BMS data for both building groups were analysed by adjusting previously developed 
Matlab scripts for Part A and then graphically presenting them for the data from existing 
buildings. 
5.1.1 Group B.1: Large number of buildings 
Five buildings (named A, B, C, D and E) were investigated in order to see whether the key 
figures can be used to study trends for a large number of buildings. Practical usability of the 
developed key figures was tested on data obtained from Energocentrum Plus, s.r.o. [59]. 
Building description 
Due to the disclosure agreement between the Energocentrum Plus, s.r.o. and its clients; the 
gathered building information was limited. For the five examined buildings, information about 
the building’s floor area, the number of employees and occupied hours were obtained (Table 13 
and Table 15). Furthermore, Table 13 also includes sample intervals and measurement periods 
for which the datasets were downloaded. The months with complete datasets were used for 
further analyses.

The energy source for the HVAC systems operation was electricity. All buildings were 
mechanically ventilated and there was no possibility to manually open the windows due to 
safety reasons. For the investigated buildings, the heating season was assumed from 1st October 
to 30th April with a heating set point at 20 °C. According to the building manager in 
Energocentrum Plus, s.r.o. [59], mechanical cooling was in general mainly needed for the 
summer months June–August 2015. However, for the examined buildings, cooling was provided 
through the year whenever the temperature exceeded the cooling set point of 26 °C.  
BMS characteristics 
The installed BMS was manufactured by Domat Control System s.r.o. [60]. However, the 
information on what type of sensors were installed in the investigated buildings and their 
position in the building was not available. Also, the information about sensors calibration was 
missing. The analyses were conducted assuming the sensors and meters were correctly 
calibrated and the collected readings presented accurate data.  
Type of collected data 
The type of data used for the joint assessment of energy consumption and IEQ was sorted into 
two groups: energy related and IEQ related data. Investigated variables also included time and 
date relevant variables. The measured BMS electricity consumption included net electricity for 
heating, cooling, ventilation, lighting, equipment etc. where no primary factors were included. 
The energy related data type also included monthly energy readings (electricity) for the period 
2012–2015 for buildings B–D and for the years 2014 and 2015 for building A. No primary 
energy factors were included. For the five investigated buildings, IEQ related data was available 
for a single room equipped with BMS sensors (mainly a main open plan space). 
For investigated buildings in the Czech Republic, measured BMS data included the data types 
presented in Table 14. 
 
==PAGE:86==

Data cleaning 
In order to enhance data quality, missing and inaccurate data for each building dataset needed 
to be correctly handled before starting with the data analyses. Preparation of the data for the 
analyses was done according to Matlab’s guideline “Matlab Data Analysis” [52]. The guideline 
defines an outlier as a value that is more than three standard deviations away from the mean 
value. Such outlier values were removed from the datasets. Data was deleted from the set where 
there were gaps with missing data values for more than two hours. If data was missing for less 
than two hours, a missing value was represented by the average value for the time period of one 
hour before or after the missing dataset.  
Occupancy period 
Period estimation was done in order to separate the data for occupied and non-occupied hours. 
Where 1 or 5 min timestamp data was recorded, it was transformed into hourly average values. 
According to Fan et al. [42], averaging dataset values into hourly values does not lead to the loss 
of any meaningful information captured by sensor data. It only helps to reduce the size and 
computation time of data. 
Since the holiday period was not known for the examined buildings, the occupied time was 
assumed to be during the opening hours of the buildings listed in Table 15 through the whole 
year. The opening hours were not given for the building E. For the analyses, same opening hours 
schedule was assumed for building E as for building B.

Key figures for joint assessment of energy and IEQ performance indicators for 
Group B.1 
Energy performance indicators 
General energy use 
Two different energy performance readings were obtained for the investigated building: high-
frequency BMS data and monthly electricity readings. 
The BMS data was recorded for buildings A–E for the different time periods, listed in Table 15. 
The monthly electricity readings were collected for the period 2012–2015 for buildings B–D and 
for the building A for the years 2014 and 2015. As explained in the previous section (Data 
cleaning), the BMS data values and monthly electricity readings were evaluated for the removal 
of outliers. For buildings B and D, along with detailed BMS data monthly energy readings were 
available for the same time periods (May–December 2015). Therefore, a comparison of these 
two energy usage data types was done. This was done in order to assess data reliability and 
correctly interpret the input data for a further joint assessment of energy and IEQ performance 
indicators. The hypothesis was that the values of the monthly readings and processed BMS 
monthly values were the same. 
Energy consumption in relation to monthly degree-days 
For the investigated buildings in the Czech Republic, HDD and CDD values were obtained from 
the free online meteorological information source degreedays.net [61] for the city of Brno in the 
region of Moravia in the Czech Republic as recommended by the building manager in 
Energocentrum Plus, s.r.o. [59]. According to the company’s building manager, the heating 
balance point temperature was set to 13 °C. For the cooling season, the same cooling balance 
point temperature value of 25 °C was assumed as for Denmark (Part A). 
Since the heating energy consumption was not available, monthly total electricity consumption 
obtained from BMS was evaluated against the monthly HDD values for the heating months. 
Similar for the cooling season, monthly total electricity consumption was evaluated against the 
monthly CDD values. 
Energy signature 
Since it was not possible to divide the electricity consumption into various subsystems (heat, 
cooling, ventilation, lighting, equipment etc.), the monthly electricity consumption was 
computed against the monthly average outdoor temperature. No division was done for the 
heating and cooling season respectively. For the existing buildings in the Czech Republic 
outdoor temperature data information was not provided from the measured BMS datasets. 
Therefore, the outdoor temperature data was obtained from online free meteorological 
information source weatherunderground.com [62] for the weather station in Brno, Czech 
Republic. 
 
==PAGE:88==
5 Part B: Validation of the developed key figures on data from existing buildings 
74 
 
IEQ performance indicators 
The investigated buildings were put in the Category II since assumed the buildings present 
recently built commercial buildings. The Category II recommendations were used as boundary 
limits for the evaluation of thermal comfort and IAQ. 
Thermal comfort indicators 
It was assumed that the existing buildings are in good condition, have low air velocities and the 
mean radiant and air temperatures are similar. Therefore, the measured indoor air 
temperatures were considered to be operative temperatures in order to simplify the thermal 
comfort evaluation process.  
The results for Method A and Method B were presented for occupied hours when the operative 
temperature was below the lower (20 °C) or above the upper temperature limit (26 °C) or 
outside the specified range (20–26 °C) of Category II. The recommended limit of Annex G was 
applied. It was assumed that violation of thermal comfort boundaries was acceptable yet 
tolerable if the measured temperatures were outside the specified range (for Category II: 20–26 
°C) for maximum 5 % of occupied hours.  
The Improvement method for Method A and B was used for further thermal comfort evaluation 
with its recommended DH monthly limit value of 13 DH (page 26, Table 4). For simplicity sake, 
value of 13 DH was used as a boundary condition for evaluation of Method B for all the 
investigated buildings. Later on, DH limit value was recalculated according to the building’s 
actual monthly occupied hours in order to identify the error obtained while using a constant DH 
limit value.  
IAQ indicators 
CO2 concentration measurements were only available for building E for the period November 
2015–February 2016. Outdoor concentrations of 400 ppm were assumed. For the evaluation of 
IAQ according to Category II, indoor CO2 limit of 900 ppm was used. Furthermore, 
recommended limit given by Annex G was applied here. It was decided that if the CO2 
concentrations were below the recommended CO2 limit of Category II for at least 95 % of the 
occupied time, the specified IAQ limits of Category II were fulfilled.  
Joint assessment of energy and IEQ performance indicators 
 
Key figures for the joint assessment of energy and IEQ performance indicators developed in Part 
A were plotted for the available datasets for buildings A–E.  
Processed BMS data was used for the joint assessment analyses since the measured BMS data of 
energy performance and indoor climate enabled simultaneous analyses during the same 
measured time period. 
 
 
==PAGE:89==
5 Part B: Validation of the developed key figures on data from existing buildings 
75 
 
Energy consumption against thermal comfort 
For the joint evaluation of energy consumption and thermal comfort no distinction was made 
between the heating and cooling season since only total electricity consumption was presented 
as an energy performance indicator. If individual readings for space heating and cooling 
consumption would be available, the heating and cooling seasons would be investigated 
separately. For joint assessment analyses, thermal comfort indicators were presented for 
occupied hours when the temperatures were outside the specified range for Category II. 
Three key figures developed and tested on simulation data in Part A were used for the joint 
evaluation of energy consumption and thermal comfort indicators for buildings A–E. For the 
available datasets, the monthly total electricity consumption was plotted against the monthly 
percentage of occupied hours when the temperatures were outside the specified range of 
Category II (Method A). The recommended limit of Annex G was applied. Furthermore, total 
monthly electricity consumption was plotted against monthly degree hours (Method B). 
Afterwards, the two previous plots were combined and the same performance indicators were 
plotted on a 3D plot where the energy consumption indicator was plotted against the indicator 
for Method A and against the indicator for Method B. For the evaluation of the results presented 
on the 3D plot the recommended limit values were used; for Method A according to Annex G (5 
% recommended limit value) and for Method B according to the Improvement Method for 
Method A and B (recommended monthly DH limit value of 13 DH). 
Energy consumption against IAQ 
 
Since the CO2 concentrations were only recorded for the building E, energy consumption was 
evaluated in relation to CO2 concentrations only for this building. The 3D plot developed in Part 
A was presented for the building E where the monthly total electricity consumption was plotted 
against the monthly percentage of occupied hours when the measured hourly CO2 
concentrations exceeded the recommended CO2 limit of Category II (900 ppm) and against the 
monthly CO2 exceedance above the recommended CO2 limit.  
According to Annex G, as a recommended limit value for monthly percentage of occupied hours 
when the measured hourly CO2 concentrations exceeded the recommended CO2 limit of 
Category II a 5 % limit value was applied. It refers to an acceptable yet tolerable violation of the 
CO2 concentrations above the recommended CO2 limit of Category II. As a recommended 
monthly CO2 exceedance limit a value of 1300 ppm was used (page 30, Table 5).  
5.1.2 Group B.2: Single building with detailed building information 
Since only limited building information was known for the set of buildings in the Czech Republic 
(Group B.1) with the incomplete yearly datasets, the decision was made to also test the 
indicators on a specific building where detailed building information and complete BMS 
datasets were available. Viborg Town Hall was chosen for the evaluation since this building was 
equipped with an advanced intelligent BMS where data was measured for a longer period of 
time, the energy consumption was recorded for different subsystems (heating, cooling and 
mechanical ventilation) and the available building information allowed a detailed study of the 
building’s dynamics. 
==PAGE:90==
5 Part B: Validation of the developed key figures on data from existing buildings 
76 
 
Building description 
Several investigations of Viborg Town Hall and two master thesis projects were reviewed in 
order to get the needed information about building characteristics [63, 64]. The building 
presents a low energy sustainable building located in Viborg, Denmark [63]. The building 
consists of six floors with a gross area of 19343 m2 and 885 occupants [63].  
 
Figure 33: Cross section of Viborg Town Hall [64]. 
Heating was provided by district heating (heat exchanger) and ground water (absorption heat 
pumps). The distribution of heat into the space was through low temperature thermo active 
decks (TAD) installed in the floors and traditional radiators mounted below the windows. The 
TAD zone covered an area of 8232 m2. The installed TAD also served as a cooling source where 
groundwater was used. Natural ventilation was utilized to ventilate the open plan offices where 
the stack effect from the atrium was used. The natural ventilation was controlled by the 
automated windows which were placed above the suspended lamella ceiling.  
Mechanical ventilation (VAV with heat recovery) was used for the meeting rooms (ground 
floor), canteen and kitchen (5th floor). The VAV was regulated based on a room temperature or 
CO2 concentration level schedule. Moreover, CO2 concentrations above 500 ppm led to the 
activation of the ventilation system in kitchen and canteen in order to provide good IAQ [63]. 
The temperature set points were 22 °C in winter and 24 °C in summer. Mechanical ventilation 
helped to maintain the thermal comfort inside the desired temperature range (by heating and 
cooling air when needed). As pointed out by Ingason and Jensen [64] the mechanical cooling of 
meeting rooms was working only when occupants informed the building manager about the 
meeting and therefore, that ventilation was needed. 
In general, the heating season was considered to be between 1st October and 1st May whereas 
the cooling season was from 30th April to 30th September. A more detailed description of the 
building and its HVAC installation is available in the MSc thesis project of Hansen [63] and 
Ingason and Jensen [64]. 
 
==PAGE:91==
5 Part B: Validation of the developed key figures on data from existing buildings 
77 
 
BMS characteristics 
Viborg Town Hall was a part of the European Union’s BEST Energy Project. One of the project’s 
objectives was to install an advanced BMS in order to control and evaluate energy consumption 
for Viborg Town Hall [64]. Installed energy meters allowed studies of the energy used for 
heating, cooling, ventilation, lighting etc. Moreover, the secondary energy meters measured the 
net energy for TAD, radiators, and the mechanical ventilation system separately. The accuracy 
of these energy meters was ± 5 % [65].  
For further analyses, the measured BMS datasets including room temperature and outdoor 
conditions were used. The sensors measuring indoor temperatures were positioned on the 
ceilings [63] of two open plan offices on the 2nd and 4th floor, the mayor’s office on the 4th floor, 
the canteen on the 5th floor and the reception on the ground floor. The location of the different 
rooms can be seen in Figure 34. 
 
Figure 34: 3D layout of Viborg Town Hall with the shown locations for the reception, the open plan offices 
and the canteen [63]. 
Type of collected data 
The installed BMS recorded a large number of measurements for different data variables. Beside 
the energy consumption and basic IEQ parameters, different system flow rates, pressure, 
control signals etc. were recorded as well as various weather data types. For the scope of this 
project, the measured BMS data types presented in Table 16 were used.  
==PAGE:92==
5 Part B: Validation of the developed key figures on data from existing buildings 
78 
 
Table 16: Energy consumption and IEQ parameters measured for Viborg Town Hall.  
Type of measured 
parameter

Energy consumption

- TAD energy consumption (kWh) for heating and cooling

The net energy was analysed where no primary energy factors were taken into account. Energy 
per square meter was calculated according to the total heated and cooled floor area (TAD zone 
area; 8232 m2) as proposed by Hansen [63]. The indoor temperature was measured for the 
reception on the ground floor, open plan offices on the 2nd and 4th floor, the mayor’s office on the 
4th floor and the canteen on the 5th floor. According to the reviewed documentation from COWI 
[66], BMS sensors installed in the building should measure CO2 concentrations. However, it was 
discovered that this data was not available by the BMS. CO2 data for the examined building was 
available from the individually positioned HOBO loggers coupled with a Vaisala CO2 
measurement device. These long-term measurements were used for the analyses of the IAQ for 
the investigated year. The placements of the measuring devices are illustrated in Figure 35. 
 
Figure 35: Long-term measurement locations of the HOBO loggers in Viborg Town Hall [63].  
CO2 concentrations were measured by a Vaisala CO2 measurement device and stored in the 
HOBO logger. The accuracy of such measurements is ± 100 ppm (accuracy ± 5 % plus 
calibration uncertainty) given an air temperature interval -5–45 °C, a humidity range of 0–85 % 
and a flow velocity of 0–10 m/s [63]. According to Hansen [63], the devices were calibrated by 
technicians at the International Centre for Indoor Environment and Energy (ICIEE).  
More about the placement of the HOBO loggers measuring stations, the logging interval period 
and the calibration process is available in Hansen’s MSc thesis [63].  
==PAGE:93==
5 Part B: Validation of the developed key figures on data from existing buildings 
79 
 
Data cleaning 
For Viborg Town Hall, complete datasets for the whole year of 2013 were available from Hansen 
[63], which allowed yearly analyses from 01.01.2013–31.12.2013. For the year 2013, data was 
downloaded for four periods as described in Table 17. Furthermore, the data was later 
downloaded for a part of the year 2016 in order to compare building’s performance for different 
years. It was discovered that the BMS automatically compresses the data in eight hour averages 
when more than 170 days are in the history. Therefore, only the last five months in history 
could be used for the comparison (December 2015–April 2016). 
Table 17: Measurement period for the collected BMS datasets for Viborg Town Hall for the whole year of 
2013 and the winter period in 2016.

As seen from Table 17, measured datasets were missing for the period from 14th May until 19th 
June 2013. In accordance with the followed data analysis approach [52], months of May and 
June 2013 were excluded from the analyses. Other missing values and outliers were treated 
following the same approach described for the five buildings in the Czech Republic (Group B.1, 
chapter 5.1.1).  
The HOBO loggers coupled together with the Vaisala measuring device recorded CO2 
concentrations every 10 minutes [63]. The CO2 data was transformed into hourly average values 
following the approach described for Group B.1. Due to limitations in the storage capacity of the 
HOBO loggers, data were downloaded several times during the measuring period [63]. For the 
thesis project, the CO2 measurement period from 01.01.2013–31.12.2013 was investigated. No 
CO2 measurements were done in the year 2016. 
Occupancy period 
The working hours were set between 7h–17h during weekdays. Official holidays for Viborg 
Town Hall were only during the Christmas holidays (20th Dec–1st Jan). This period was excluded 
from the analyses since it was considered as non-occupied hours. During the summer, 
employees took holidays at different times (mostly July) [63] and the building was operating in 
normal operational mode for this period as through the rest of the year [63].  
 
==PAGE:94==
5 Part B: Validation of the developed key figures on data from existing buildings 
80 
 
Key figures for joint assessment of energy and IEQ performance indicators for 
Group B.2 
The evaluation of the energy and IEQ performance indicators was for the whole year of 2013. 
These two types of indicators were later combined in joint assessment analyses. Later on, 
referring to the analyses done for the year 2013, refers to analyses done for the year 2013 
where the months of May and June were excluded due to the missing data. For the joint 
evaluation of the energy performance and thermal comfort data recorded for the period 
January–April 2016 was also used for Viborg Town Hall in order to establish a comparison of 
building’s operation during the period January–April 2013 and January–April 2016.  
Energy performance indicators 
General energy use 
As already explained, energy consumption for different subsystems was available for Viborg 
Town Hall. The building’s energy consumption was investigated for heating, cooling and 
ventilation separately. 
Monthly heating consumption included the building’s energy consumption for TAD (heating), 
radiators and consumption used for pre-heating of the coils for the mechanical ventilation. The 
heating consumption for hot water usage was not taken into account since it does not have a 
direct correlation with the thermal comfort or IAQ. Monthly cooling consumption included 
monthly cooling consumption for TAD’s energy consumption used for cooling and energy 
consumption used for pre-cooling of the coils for the mechanical ventilation.  
Since monthly heating and cooling consumption values already included the energy 
consumption for the mechanical ventilation system, this system’s consumption was shown 
independently as ventilation consumption on a single plot where it was marked whether the 
energy was used for the heating or cooling of air.  
Energy consumption in relation to monthly degree-days 
Monthly degree-days values for Viborg Town Hall were calculated from the measured hourly 
BMS outdoor temperature datasets for the year 2013 following calculation approach presented 
in Part A (page 22, Equation 1 and 2). The same balance point temperatures were used for the 
monthly HDD and CDD calculation as used in Part A (17 °C as the heating and 25 °C as the 
cooling balance point temperature) since the building was positioned in Denmark. 
Energy signature 
For Viborg Town Hall, the hourly recorded outdoor temperature data was measured by the BMS 
sensors. These measured outdoor temperatures were used for the calculation of monthly 
average outdoor temperatures.  
 
==PAGE:95==
5 Part B: Validation of the developed key figures on data from existing buildings 
81 
 
Measured outdoor temperature by BMS sensors and from weather station 
As discussed by Andre et al. [17], BMS sensors can overestimate outdoor temperatures due to 
the direct sun exposure and therefore lead to lower HDD values in winter and to higher CDD 
values in summer months. As authors emphasize, it is important to know where the BMS 
sensors are positioned. According to Andre et al. [17], there can be a great deviation between 
the BMS outdoor temperature readings and data from meteorological information source which 
normally includes data from extensive weather stations.  
Therefore, the measured BMS outdoor temperature data for 2013 was compared to the outdoor 
temperature data from the online site weatherunderground.com [62] for the location of the town 
Viborg, Denmark. This was done in order to verify the measured BMS temperatures and analyse 
the effect of a solar radiation on the collected BMS readings. 
IEQ performance indicators 
Category II was chosen for the IEQ evaluation since Viborg Town Hall presented a recently built 
building categorized as a low energy sustainable building.  
Thermal comfort indicators 
The same assumption was done as for the buildings in Group B.1; measured air temperatures 
indoors were considered to be operative temperatures.  
CDF graph was used to show the percentage distribution of the operative temperature during 
occupied hours for the rooms with positioned BMS sensors. Evaluation of thermal comfort was 
done according to the Method A (percentage of occupied hours when the operative temperature 
was outside the specified temperature range of Category II (20–26 °C)) and according to the 
Method B (monthly degree hours when the temperatures were outside the specified range of 
the Category II (20–26 °C)). The annual results for Method A were presented in a table. 
Numerical presentation of results for Method A in Part A showed a better and more efficient 
way than presenting results on a CDF graph. As for Group B.1, the recommended limit values for 
Method A (5 % limit value) and Method B 1 (13 DH limit value) were applied. 
IAQ indicators 
Annual distribution of CO2 concentrations was computed for the different measurement 
locations of HOBO loggers (Figure 35). Only complete CO2 datasets for the locations of L6 
(northwest open plan office on the 3rd floor), L7 (southwest open plan office on the 4th floor), L8 
(mayor’s office on the 4th floor) and L9 (canteen on the 5th floor) were found for the year 2013.  
According to Ingason and Jensen [64], the CO2 decay stagnated at around 350 ppm which 
showed a background concentration during the non-occupied hours [64]. Therefore, as the 
outdoor concentration 350 ppm was assumed. This led to recommended CO2 concentration 
limit of 850 ppm according to Category II. 
 
==PAGE:96==
5 Part B: Validation of the developed key figures on data from existing buildings 
82 
 
Joint assessment of energy and IEQ performance indicators 
When doing a joint assessment of energy performance and IEQ as a representative room, the 
occupied space where occupants spend most of the time was primarily evaluated. In the case of 
Viborg Town Hall, this was the employees open plan offices.  
The representative mean values were calculated in order to get a general overview of the IEQ of 
the spaces where occupants spent most of their time. As explained in chapter 4.3.4, by using 
representative mean IEQ values larger percentage of total used building space is included in the 
joint assessment evaluation than by only including the measured IEQ data for a single open plan 
office.  
Energy consumption against thermal comfort 
Joint assessment analyses were done where primarily concern was on most commonly occupied 
open plan office spaces. The representative thermal comfort indicators were obtained as mean 
values of the thermal comfort indicators (results for Method A and Method B) calculated from 
the measured BMS room temperature data available for the open plan offices on the 2nd and 4th 
floor. Depending on the results for the IEQ performance indicators, also other room type could 
be inspected if a tremendously aggravated thermal comfort would be reported. 
The same key figures were used for the joint assessment of energy consumption and thermal 
comfort indicators for Viborg Town Hall as for buildings in Group B.1. Furthermore, the same 
recommended limit values for the thermal comfort indicators were applied as for buildings in 
Group B.1. The distinction of heating and cooling consumption allowed evaluation done for the 
heating and cooling season separately.  
Heating and cooling energy consumption was computed against the monthly percentage of 
occupied hours when the operative temperature was below the lower (20 °C) or above the 
upper temperature limit (26 °C) of Category II for the heating and cooling period respectively. 
Similarly, monthly heating and cooling consumption was plotted against monthly heating and 
cooling degree hours. Third plot included the 3D plot developed in Part A where was energy 
consumption evaluated against both indicators for thermal comfort evaluation: Method A and 
Method B. 
The 3D plot developed in Part A, where energy consumption, thermal comfort and outdoor 
conditions were represented on a same plot, was computed for the heating and cooling season 
respectively. Energy consumption was plotted against the indicator for Method A and against 
the monthly average outdoor temperature. 
Furthermore, same type of key figure (3D plot) was used for evaluation of building’s 
performance during different years. The aim was to assess, whether the proposed key figures 
can be used as a tool for building’s continuous commissioning where it can be assessed whether 
the building deteriorates with time (higher energy consumption and worse IEQ). For Viborg 
Town Hall, BMS readings were downloaded for the latest period which allowed the study of the 
energy performance and IEQ data on hourly basis. The periods January–April 2013 and 
January–April 2016 were the only ones usable for the comparison between the year 2013 and 
2016. For these two periods, energy consumption was plotted against the indicator for Method 
==PAGE:97==
5 Part B: Validation of the developed key figures on data from existing buildings 
83 
 
A and against the monthly average outdoor temperatures for the representative open plan 
office.  
Energy consumption against IAQ 
Evaluation of building’s ventilation energy consumption and IAQ parameters was done for the 
representative open plan office and the canteen. Despite the fact that open plan offices were 
solely naturally ventilated, this plot was used to investigate whether the applied natural 
ventilation system was capable of maintaining good IAQ.  
Representative mean IAQ indicator for the open plan offices represented a mean value for the 
measured CO2 concentrations for the open plan office on the 3rd (L6) and the 4th floor (L7) since 
HOBO loggers were positioned for these locations. Furthermore, canteen (L9) was investigated 
since this room type was mechanically ventilated and the joint evaluation of ventilation 
consumption and IAQ could show whether the mechanical ventilation system provided 
sufficient ventilation rates to maintain good IAQ. The locations with marked positions of HOBO 
loggers were presented in Figure 35.  
Firstly, monthly ventilation consumption was computed against the average monthly CO2 
concentrations. Secondly, a 3D plot was computed presenting mechanical ventilation 
consumption against the two indicators of IAQ developed in Part A: monthly percentage of 
occupied hours when the measured CO2 concentrations exceeded the recommended CO2 limit of 
Category II and against the monthly CO2 exceedance above the recommended CO2 limit of 
Category II (900 ppm).  
The recommended limit value for monthly percentage of occupied hours when the measured 
hourly CO2 concentrations exceeded the recommended CO2 limit of Category II was 5 % as 
described in Annex G. It refers to an acceptable yet tolerable violation of the CO2 concentrations 
above the recommended CO2 limit of Category II (900 ppm). As a recommended monthly CO2 
exceedance limit a value of 1300 ppm was used as defined in Part A (page 30, Table 5).

5.3 Discussion 
The discussion section of Part B includes the validation of the proposed key figures for a joint 
assessment of energy and IEQ performance indicators on BMS data from existing buildings and 
the assessment of the practical usability of these key figures.  
5.3.1 Group B.1: Large number of buildings 
5.3.1.1 Energy performance indicators 
General energy use 
Figure 36 presented comparison of the processed monthly BMS electricity consumption values 
(Qe) for buildings A–E. The observation of the mean values showed that building C had the 
highest mean energy consumption (mean monthly consumption 10.7 kWh/m2) in comparison 
with the other buildings. Buildings A and B had similar monthly average consumption (8–9 
kWh/m2) whereas lowest average energy consumption was obtained for the buildings D and E 
(5.5–6.5 kWh/m2).  
There was a suspicion of faulty readings for certain monthly values after the analyses of the 
monthly BMS values (most suspicious value for the building B for October 2015). According to 
the Matlab’s outlier method, none of the processed monthly BMS values was identified as an 
outlier [52]. It should be noted, that one should be cautious when using these monthly values. If 
building information was not limited, further investigation should be done to find how the data 
was measured and what was included in the total electricity consumption value of the BMS 
value. Still, the processed BMS values were used for further joint analyses since not shown that 
the values were outliers. 
Processed monthly BMS values and measured monthly energy readings  
Figure 37 revealed that monthly energy readings showed significantly different consumption 
values than were the processed monthly BMS values during the same measurement period; 
May–December 2015 for buildings B and D.  
In general, reported monthly energy readings were higher than the processed monthly BMS 
values (up to 45 %) for buildings B and D during the observed period in 2015. Still, certain 
monthly energy readings were notably lower than the processed monthly BMS monthly values 
(10 or 50 % of the BMS value). Such a deviation in monthly energy readings implied faulty 
values for certain monthly energy readings even if not identified as such by Matlab’s outlier 
method [52] (analysis presented in Appendix E). The collected BMS datasets seemed to show 
more accurate consumption values and were used for further joint assessment analyses.  
Energy consumption in relation to monthly degree-days 
Figure 38 showed there was no significant increase in monthly Qe with rising monthly HDD 
values for the heating season 2015. As explained, Qe presented total electricity consumption 
values and did not represent the portion used for space heating. The deviation of the electricity 
==PAGE:121==
5 Part B: Validation of the developed key figures on data from existing buildings 
107 
 
consumption did not solely depend on the outdoor conditions but also on the occupancy 
behaviour, electrical appliances, lighting, internal gains etc. Furthermore, Qe seemed to 
decrease with increasing HDD values for buildings A and B.  
Figure 39 showed for the summer months of 2015 an increase in monthly Qe with increased 
monthly CDD values. Increase in electricity consumption of 0.5–1.2 kWh/m2 was observed for 
buildings A, B and D for the summer (July and August 2015) compared to the spring and autumn 
months (May and September 2015). This can be interpreted as the consumption used for the 
space cooling in the summer of 2015.  
Table 20 presents the results for R2 indicating a goodness of the linear fit between the observed 
variables. 
Table 20: Linear regression analysis and goodness of fit (R2) between the monthly electricity consumption 
(Qe) and corresponding degree-days values for buildings A–E. For the heating season, this presents 
evaluation of the linear correlation between Qe and monthly heating degree-days (HDD) during the period 
October 2015–February 2016. For the cooling season, this presents evaluation of the linear correlation 
between Qe and monthly cooling degree-days (CDD) during May 2015–September 2015.

When doing the linear regression model analysis for the heating season (October 2015–
February 2016), a fairly good linear fit was found for buildings A and B. The study of equation 
coefficients p1 and p2 (for building B; p1 = -0.008 and intercept value p2 = 12) showed that Qe 
was decreasing with increasing HDD for the fitted model (negative value for p1) for buildings A 
and B. As described in chapter 4.1.3, R2 only shows how the linear model predicts the dependant 
variable but not if the linear model is actually appropriate for the observed data. It was 
discovered that despite the good fit obtained for the linear model, the fitted model was not 
appropriate for describing the relationship between measured Qe and HDD values.  
For the cooling season, high values for R2 (Table 20) were observed for buildings A, B and D (R2 
between 0.95–0.98) and implied a good linear fit between the two observed variables. For 
building C, only time period of two months was available (2 evaluation points). For datasets 
with only two evaluation points, a perfect linear fit would always be obtained therefore 
regression analysis was excluded for this building. For building E, no data for the summer 
months was available.  
Energy signature 
Figure 40 showed for most of the buildings slightly higher monthly energy consumption (Qe) 
for the summer months of 2015 (higher monthly average outdoor temperature) than compared 
to the winter months of 2015 and 2016. Table 21 presents the results of regression analysis 
showing goodness of the linear fit (R2) for buildings A–E.
