According to the established linear regression model, good linear correlation between the fitted 
model and electricity consumption was observed for building A (7 evaluation points). The 
regression analysis showed poor fit and low values of R2 for buildings B (9 evaluation points), D 
(10 evaluation points) and E (4 evaluation points). The monthly electricity consumption values 
varied significantly away from the fitted linear model which was based on the monthly average 
outdoor temperatures. As proven, total electricity consumption can be hardly seen only as a 
function of a single predictor such as is the measured monthly average outdoor temperature.  
5.3.1.2 IEQ performance indicators 
Thermal comfort indicators 
The analyses of the operative temperature distribution for buildings A–E (Figure 41) showed 
that the maximum temperature during occupied hours was for building A reached at 27.4 °C, for 
building B at 28.4 °C, for building C at 25.7 °C, for building D at 29.8 °C whereas for building E at 
24.5 °C for the observed time periods.  
Evaluation of the results for Method A in Table 18 showed that the operative temperature was 
for more than 95 % of the occupied time inside the specified range (20–26 °C) of Category II for 
buildings A and E and therefore fulfilled the 5 % criterion for thermal comfort evaluation 
according to Method A (Annex G). It seemed that the two buildings were capable of providing 
good thermal comfort for the occupants.  
As seen from Table 18, buildings B and D had the most significant problem with space 
overheating where the operative temperature exceeded the upper temperature limit of 
Category II (26 °C) 15.7 % and 30.8 % of the observed occupied hours respectively. On the other 
hand, building C had a problem with the temperatures dropping below the lower recommended 
limit of Category II (20 °C) 9.6 % of the observed occupied time.  
Certain monthly HDH values were calculated for buildings A, C and E as reported in Figure 42 
(results for Method B). The recommended limit of 13 DH was exceeded only in December 2015 
for building C. For building A, the problem with the temperatures below the lower 
recommended limit of Category II (20 °C) was inspected for the spring and autumn months of 
2015 (May, June, September and October). With the later months (November and December 
2015) there was no over-cooling problem. As described, the heating of buildings A–E started in 
October or November and lasted until April. For building C, it was observed that the problem 
with the indoor temperatures being below the lower limit of 20 °C was for the whole 
investigated period (December 2015 and January 2016). For the building E, only 0.2 HDH were 
inspected for the month of November 2015.  
==PAGE:123==
5 Part B: Validation of the developed key figures on data from existing buildings 
109 
 
CDH values (results for Method B, Figure 43) indicated the severity of the overheating problem 
since presenting the situation when the operative temperature exceeded the upper temperature 
limit of Category II (26 °C). Figure 43 showed that buildings B and D were facing an overheating 
problem in summer months of 2015 (July and August) yet still in spring and autumn of 2015 
(June and September). As seen, both buildings had a similar number of cooling degree hours for 
July and August 2015 (building B 125 CDH and building D 103 CDH in August 2015). As Carlucci 
and Pagliano [49] argue, there can be a significant mistake if simply comparing the value of 
degree hours for two buildings without understanding the building’s occupancy schedule. It was 
observed that building D had twice less occupied hours than building B which indicated that 
during the occupied hours the overheating problem was more severe for building D. It was 
apparent that the results for Method A and Method B needed to be investigated simultaneously. 
Building D had slightly lower CDH values than building B for the summer of 2015 where the 
operative temperature was almost all the occupied hours above 26 °C (98 %). On the other 
hand, for building B the operative temperature was above 26 °C only for around half of the 
occupied time (50 %).  
As seen for buildings A–E, the problem with overheating (CDH values; Figures 43) appeared 
during the summer months 2015 and the problem with overcooling (HDH values; Figure 42) 
during colder months (spring 2015, autumn 2015, and winter 2015/2016). When thermal 
comfort was aggravated (exceeded recommended limit values for Method A and Method B) this 
was due to the problem with overheating in the summer and problem with overcooling in the 
winter months. None of the buildings had a problem with overheating and overcooling at the 
same month.  
It should be noted that the measured datasets for buildings C and E only included a time period 
during the winter months. Buildings C and E did not have the maximum room temperatures 
exceeding the upper temperature limit of Category II (26 °C), therefore had zero percentage of 
occupied hours when the operative temperature exceeded 26 °C and zero CDH for the observed 
time period. In order to evaluate whether buildings C and E had a problem with overheating it 
would be needed to obtain the data for the summer months.  
IAQ indicators 
Figure 44 showed that the CO2 concentrations were during the occupied time for the 
investigated time period below the recommended CO2 limit of Category II for building E. It was 
observed that for 2 % of the occupied time the measured CO2 concentrations were below 400 
ppm which was estimated as the outdoor CO2 concentration for the evaluation according to 
Category II. This implied that outdoor concentration of 400 ppm might be slightly 
overestimating actual outdoor CO2 concentrations and if being precise, Category II limit of 870 
ppm should be used instead 900 ppm. Still, there were no occupied hours when CO2 
concentrations would exceed the limit of 870 ppm.  
 
 
 
==PAGE:124==
5 Part B: Validation of the developed key figures on data from existing buildings 
110 
 
5.3.1.3 Joint assessment of energy and IEQ performance indicators 
Energy consumption against thermal comfort 
Figure 45 showed that buildings A and B were experiencing overheating in the summer of 2015 
(5 % recommended limit exceeded) despite the certain increase in electricity consumption (Qe) 
for the summer months of 2015 as than compared to the rest of the observed months. For 
building A, the recommended limit value of 5 % was exceeded for September 2015 yet this 
month had the highest electricity consumption as than compared to the other observed months. 
For building C, monthly electricity consumption values were some of the highest when 
compared to the rest of the investigated buildings (mean value of 10.7 kWh/m2). Still, there was 
a certain problem with the operative temperature below 20 °C, where the recommended 5 % 
limit value was exceeded for December 2015 and January 2016 which implied an insufficient 
design capacity of the heating system. Building E did not have a significant problem with the 
operative temperature outside the range (only 0.1 % of occupied time) while having low 
monthly total electricity consumption (mean value of 5.6 kWh/m2). Building A had a similar 
monthly electricity consumption as buildings B and D for the summer months of 2015 but a 
lower percentage of occupied hours when the operative temperature was outside the specified 
range for Category II (20–26 °C).  
Building B had a mean monthly consumption of 8.7 kWh/m2 where more than half of occupied 
hours exceeded the operative temperature of 26 °C in July and August 2015. For building D, 
mean electricity consumption was 7.2 kWh/m2 where the operative temperature was above 26 
°C for almost all the occupied hours (98 %) for the same two months. Building D had the highest 
consumption in August 2015 yet the worst thermal comfort as than compared to the rest of the 
observed months. 
Analysis of Figure 46 showing electricity consumption against DH values led to similar 
conclusions as for the previous plot (Figure 45). Monthly CDH values for building D were 
slightly lower than for building B. However, building B had double the number of occupied 
hours as building D. As observed, the increase in electricity consumption for buildings B and D 
in July and August 2015 did not improve thermal comfort. If this increase in electricity 
consumption was for the HVAC mechanical cooling operation, it was assumed that such cooling 
was not efficient.  
A quick observation of the 3D plot presented in Figure 47 showed that building B had higher 
monthly total electricity consumption for July and August 2015 than building D where both 
buildings had aggravated thermal comfort. As seen, the monthly percentage of occupied hours 
when the operative temperature exceeded 26 °C was lower where the amount of monthly 
degree hours was slightly higher for building B than for building D for July and August 2015. 
Simultaneous analysis of results for Method A and Method B in Figure 47 cleared the difficulties 
due to different number of occupied hours for the investigated buildings. Despite the similarity 
in the monthly DH values for buildings B and D in the summer of 2015, the results for Method A 
showed how frequent were these temperatures above the recommended upper limit of 
Category II (26 °C) in respect to the number of the occupied hours. This was presented as a 
percentage representing the number of hours when the operative temperature was above 26 °C 
==PAGE:125==
5 Part B: Validation of the developed key figures on data from existing buildings 
111 
 
normalized according to the buildings occupied hours (different number of occupied hours for 
buildings B and D). Looking at the coloured text in Figure 47, it was observed that the 
recommended limit values for Method A and Method B were exceeded for building C in 
December (III. quadrant) whereas for buildings A and E these recommended limit values were 
not exceeded. As seen, building E had the lowest monthly electricity consumption values (red 
lines) with good thermal comfort (I. quadrant).  
Disregarding limited building information, different time periods, the number of monthly 
occupied hours and having only total electricity consumption values, it was found that the 
buildings had similar monthly electricity consumption whereas buildings B and D had 
aggravated thermal comfort. These two buildings should be investigated further in order to find 
the source of the problem and improve the HVAC system operation (mechanical cooling 
schedule) leading to better indoor environment. Optimization measures should be done 
including energy efficient solutions. Natural ventilation during summer nights would most likely 
solve the problem with overheating without increasing the energy consumption for the 
investigated buildings (if possible due to the safety measures). 
Constant DH limit value 
Figure 46 showed monthly electricity consumption (Qe) plotted against monthly degree hours 
(DH) for the available datasets during the period May 2015–February 2016 for buildings A–E 
where the evaluation of results was done using the recommended DH limit of 13 DH as 
presented in Improvement method for Method A and B (page 26, Table 4). As already explained, 
using a constant DH limit allowed for an easier evaluation of the monthly DH for large number 
of buildings.  
In this section, the recommended DH limit values were recalculated for each individual building 
according to their monthly occupied hours while using the approach presented in Improvement 
method for Method A and B. This was done in order to investigate the error obtained while using 
a constant DH limit value of 13 DH. The DH limit values according to the building’s actual 
number of occupied hours are presented in Table 22 for buildings A–E.

actual DH limit due to the higher average monthly occupied hours value (260 h) for the DH limit 
defined in Improvement method for Method A and B. However, as seen from Figure 70, having a 
constant DH limit of 13 DH still allowed for a precise evaluation of thermal discomfort. It 
showed the need for having an upper limit for the evaluation of results for Method B. However, 
it can be further investigated whether it is accurate to have a common DH limit value when 
studying trends for a large number of buildings or if a different evaluation approach should be 
considered.

 “Weighted” ranking according to occupied hours - Energy performance and 
thermal comfort 
As seen, the number of occupied hours should be included for the comparison of energy 
efficiency and thermal comfort performance done for a large number of buildings. Buildings A–E 
had different occupancy schedules (number of occupied hours). When doing a joint assessment 
of energy consumption and thermal comfort, it was observed that building D had lower energy 
consumption than building B and also lower CDH values. As seen, building D had 29 % lower 
monthly average energy consumption than building B. One would assume that building D was 
more energy efficient and had less severe overheating problem in the summer 2015 but this 
was not accurate since building D had half the occupied hours of building B. However, as the 
results for Method A revealed, the temperatures were during the summer months almost all the 
occupied time above the upper temperature limit of Category II (26 °C) for the building D 
whereas for the building B the temperatures exceeded the upper limit only for around 50 % of 
the occupied summer hours.  
There is a need for an evaluation of the energy consumption according to a reference number of 
occupied hours when doing comparisons of a large number of buildings. It seemed that a 
suitable way for comparing the overall performance of a large number of buildings can be 
established by introducing normalization of energy consumption and thermal comfort to a 
reference number of monthly occupied hours. The hypothesis was that such “weighted” ranking 
could lead to a more accurate comparison of different buildings while harmonizing a buildings 
operation pattern over same length of occupied hours. For buildings A–E, it was decided to 
“normalize” the building’s monthly energy consumption values (Figure 36) and monthly DH 
(results from Figure 42 and Figure 43) according to the monthly occupied hours for building A 
(having 250 average monthly occupied hours).  
Firstly, energy consumption values (Figure 36) were divided with the corresponding building’s 
monthly occupied hours in order to obtain energy consumption (kWh/m2) per occupied hour 
for each observed month. Afterwards, these values were multiplied with the monthly occupied 
hours of the reference building (building A). This led to obtaining a “weighted” monthly 
electricity consumption. Equation 10 shows the calculation process for obtaining a “weighted” 
monthly indicator value according to a reference monthly occupied hours. This was developed 
in order to obtain “weighted” monthly electricity consumption where a building’s monthly 
electricity consumption was used as input for the monthly indicator value.  
 Weighted                          
                       
                                         
                               (Eq. 10) 
“Weighted” mean monthly electricity consumption was obtained by calculating the average 
value from the “weighted” monthly electricity consumption values for each building. Ranking of 
buildings energy efficiency was done according to these “weighted” mean monthly consumption 
values in order to establish a ranking from 1 to 5 which is presented in Table 23 as “RANKING 
Energy efficiency” (1 = energy efficient building, 5 = least energy efficient building among the 
investigated buildings). 
Secondly, the results for Method A (Table 18) were used for the thermal comfort ranking. 
Percentage values of occupied hours when the operative temperature was outside the range for 
==PAGE:128==
5 Part B: Validation of the developed key figures on data from existing buildings 
114 
 
the investigated buildings were ranked from 1 to 5 which is presented in Table 23; “RANKING 
Method A” (1 = building with lowest percentage value; 5 = building with highest percentage 
value). 
Thirdly, the mean “weighted” monthly DH values (Method B) were computed by using same 
approach as described for the ranking of the energy efficiency and using Equation 10. Monthly 
DH values were divided with the corresponding monthly number of occupied hours for 
buildings A–E. These values were then multiplied by the monthly number of occupied hours for 
building A. The “weighted” mean monthly DH values were obtained by calculating the mean 
value of the “weighted” monthly DH values for each building. Ranking for Method B was 
established (Table 23; “RANKING Method B”) where the “weighted” mean monthly DH values 
were sorted from 1 to 5 (1 = least frequent thermal discomfort, 5 = severe thermal discomfort).  
Results of the analysis are presented in Table 23 where “RANKING Energy efficiency” and 
“RANKING Thermal comfort” were used for benchmarking of buildings A–E according to their 
energy efficiency and thermal comfort performance.

As observed from Table 23, buildings A and E seemed to be operating in the most energy 
efficient way among the investigated buildings for the given time periods. These two buildings 
also had the best thermal comfort on average during occupied hours among the investigated 
buildings. As seen, buildings B and C were ranked as moderately energy efficient buildings and 
had average thermal comfort among compared buildings. Building D ranked as least energy 
efficient among the observed buildings with the worst thermal comfort.  
An evaluation process where a building’s occupied hours are taken into account seems 
necessary in order to establish a relevant comparison for a large number of buildings with 
different occupied hours. However, it should be noted that the buildings had a different 
measurement time period (incomplete yearly datasets) and building information was limited. 
==PAGE:129==
5 Part B: Validation of the developed key figures on data from existing buildings 
115 
 
For this reason, the established comparison for buildings A–E might not be precise. Still, it was 
shown that occupied hours play an important factor in a building’s performance evaluation 
process; energy and thermal comfort evaluation. The occupied hours parameter should be 
included in the benchmarking of a large number of buildings in order to harmonize the 
compared buildings with different occupied time and obtain reliable results.  
Energy consumption against IAQ 
Figure 48 showed that the monthly total electricity consumption values (z-axis) for the 
observed months were between 4.8–6.5 kWh/m2 while for all these months the building E had 
good IAQ (zero values on x- and y-axis). The building E was mechanically ventilated and the 
graph revealed that the ventilation exchange rates (leading to certain consumption) were 
sufficient in order to provide good IAQ. The developed 3D plot showed that for all the observed 
months there were no values where the measured CO2 concentrations would exceed the 
recommended CO2 concentration limit of Category II (900 ppm). To conclude, all the monthly 
values were found in the I. quadrant which indicated good IAQ for the building with its total 
electricity consumption.  
5.3.2 Practical usability of the developed key figures to study trends for a large 
number of buildings 
For Group B.1, the developed key figures in Part A were tested on BMS data obtained for 
existing commercial buildings A–E located in Moravia region in the Czech Republic. It was 
observed that the buildings were mainly experiencing an overheating problem during the 
summer months of 2015 (particularly buildings B and D) despite the high monthly electricity 
consumption. It was not discovered what part of total monthly electricity consumption went for 
space cooling but it was determined that the installed mechanical cooling system was not 
capable of maintaining good thermal comfort in the summer of 2015 (temperatures exceeding 
the upper limit of Category II: 26 °C) for most of the investigated buildings. For the examined 
buildings (except building C) the installed heating system was capable of maintaining good 
thermal comfort during the observed winter months. The recorded CO2 concentration datasets 
were available only for building E which had good IAQ. It was not possible to assess whether the 
installed ventilation systems in other buildings were capable of providing good IAQ. 
One of the thesis goals was to test the developed key figures on data from existing buildings for 
a large number of buildings. The conditions that need to be fulfilled for joint assessment 
evaluation of energy and IEQ performance indicators were already discussed in chapters 4.3.1 
and 4.3.4. Furthermore, the importance of including an occupied hours parameter in analyses 
when doing benchmarking was already a part of discussion in the previous section. In this 
section, possible integration of the key figures in building management and the level of 
information needed in order to study trends for a large number of buildings were addressed. 
During the background study it was investigated that building’s operation analyses are rarely 
done properly in practice due to limited measured data and building information [7] and 
available time for the extensive analyses [15].  
For buildings A–E, building information was fairly limited. Since every building operates in a 
complex way it would be important to understand the investigated building’s behaviour, 
==PAGE:130==
5 Part B: Validation of the developed key figures on data from existing buildings 
116 
 
occupancy density, equipment loading etc. [27]. It was discovered that the quality of raw data 
was important for further analyses and key figure computation in order to perform accurate 
analyses. As seen, when doing energy performance evaluation, there were energy readings 
where faulty values were suspected. However, according to the used outlier identification 
methodology [52], questionable values were not defined as outliers. Due to the limited available 
information, it could not be investigated further what lies behind the measured monthly energy 
readings or high-frequency BMS data. For the examined buildings, information about the type of 
installed BMS sensors, their position in the building and calibration process description would 
be needed in order to evaluate the reliability of the raw measured BMS datasets. Furthermore, 
the available IEQ data was available only for one location in the building therefore the 
representative IEQ data for a large number of most commonly occupied rooms could not be 
presented.  
Lack of building information can lead to a faulty interpretation of the key figure's plot. As 
discovered during analyses done for buildings A–E, it was difficult to compare different 
buildings and asses their energy efficiency in respect to their indoor environment when 
sufficient building description was not available. The division of total electricity consumption 
into subsystems (heating, cooling, and ventilation) and detailed building information 
characteristics would improve the evaluation process. Having datasets for a short period of time 
(for example two months) leads to limited data analyses. In general, the developed key figures 
should be used for evaluation over a longer period of time and therefore such long-term 
datasets should be used (recommended complete yearly datasets).  
To sum up, building manager doing a joint assessment of energy and indoor environment 
performance first needs to obtain sufficient information in order to correctly treat the raw BMS 
data and interpret the information that the plotted key figures are holding. Without this 
information, joint assessment analyses can be done for a large number of buildings as seen for 
buildings A–E. However, the findings can be fairly limited and the practical usability of such 
analyses can therefore be questionable. To accurately interpret the key figures plot and to study 
trends for a large number of buildings, it is highly recommended to obtain more detailed 
information as presented in Table 24. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:131==
5 Part B: Validation of the developed key figures on data from existing buildings 
117 
 
Table 24: List of recommended information needed for joint assessment analysis when analysing trends for a 
large number of buildings.

- Hourly recordings for longer period of time (twelve months or more) 
- Energy consumption data: Primarily heating, cooling, ventilation, if 
available also usage for domestic hot water, lighting, equipment 
- IEQ related data: Room temperature, CO2 concentrations 
- Outdoor temperature 
- Location and placement of sensors and their calibration technique 
Building 
characteristics 
- Building type and construction year (renovation year) 
- Floor area, percentage heated and air-conditioned

HVAC system 
- Characteristics of the installed system 
- Operation schedule 
Building’s occupancy 
- Occupied hours and holidays 
- Actual occupancy density and holidays 
Climate data 
- Location and climate zone 
- Reference year if comparison done for different years 
- Balance point temperature for DD method  
5.3.3 Group B.2: Single building with detailed building information 
5.3.3.1 Energy performance indicators 
General energy use 
The monthly heating consumption values presented in Figure 49 showed monthly heating 
consumption (Qh) from the installed TAD floor heating, radiators and energy that was used for 
the heating coils of the mechanical ventilation system. Detailed analyses of the results showed 
that around 75 % of the annual heating consumption was provided by the radiators where the 
rest by TAD and mechanical ventilation (for the year 2013).  
Monthly cooling consumption (Qc) illustrated in Figure 49 presented monthly cooling 
consumption of the mechanical ventilation system and TAD floor cooling. It was discovered that, 
the TAD system was not used for the cooling of the building during the investigated year 2013 
due to the problems with the ground water system. Therefore, Qc presented only cooling energy 
used for the mechanical ventilation system which was supplying air in the kitchen, the canteen 
and the meeting rooms. It was assessed that the current division of energy consumption 
according to the TAD floor area was underestimating actual mechanical ventilation 
consumption per unit area. The area should be recalculated according to the actual area that 
was ventilated by mechanical ventilation. Further analyses were done with the existing energy 
consumption values which served as an estimator since energy performance analysis of Viborg 
Town Hall was not the aim of this project. 
==PAGE:132==
5 Part B: Validation of the developed key figures on data from existing buildings 
118 
 
Observation of Figure 50 showed that the mechanical ventilation was mostly used for the 
cooling of air. Furthermore, Figure 49 and Figure 50 revealed that some of the heating was 
spent in summer and cooling in winter 2013. 
Energy consumption in relation to monthly degree-days 
The analyses of Figure 51 showed that higher monthly Qh values were obtained with the 
months getting colder (larger HDD). For the cooling season 2013, the analyses of Figure 52 
showed that more energy was spent on cooling with the months getting hotter (July and August) 
than in September where there were almost no CDD. Table 25 presents the regression analysis 
for the heating (Qh against HDD) and cooling season 2013 (Qc against CDD).

For the heating season 2013, the linear regression analysis showed that there was more energy 
spent for the months of October and November 2013 than the linear model predicted. For 
March 2013 less energy was spent than the linear model predicted. 
In both cases it was observed that the heating and cooling consumption were to some extent 
affected by the outdoor conditions (HDD and CDD). A good linear correlation was observed 
between the fitted model and corresponding energy consumption (R2 between 0.79-0.87) for 
both seasons. As described earlier, the cooling season only includes values for three months 
(July–September) where the analyses of the heating season included values for seven months. 
More observation monthly points result in a higher number of residual points. With fewer 
points for the cooling season, a better fit between the variables can be obtained. However, that 
does not necessarily show that the correlation between the cooling consumption and CDD is 
linear. More points (May, June with correct monthly energy values) would be desired in order to 
correctly interpret the correlation between the chosen variables and evaluate whether the fitted 
linear regression model presents a good fit. 
Energy signature 
Figure 53 revealed that highest heating consumption (Qh) values were obtained for the months 
having lowest monthly average outdoor temperatures in 2013 (January–March). It was 
observed that decrease in heating consumption from 13.5 kWh/m2 to around 8 kWh/m2 was 
obtained when the monthly average outdoor temperature arose from 0 °C to around 5 °C. In 
October 2013, monthly heating consumption of 3.5 kWh/m2 was needed when the average 
monthly outdoor temperature was around 10 °C. As shown by Hansen [63], TAD control was 
influenced by the average outdoor temperature for the previous day.  
==PAGE:133==

with the increased monthly average outdoor temperature. As seen, cooling consumption was 
increased from 2.6 kWh/m2 (September with monthly average outdoor T around 15 °C) to 
approximately 3.2 kWh/m2 for the months of July and August (monthly average outdoor 
temperature around 19 °C).

As seen, the monthly heating and cooling consumption were dependent on the outdoor

good linear correlation was obtained for the cooling season. It should be noted, that for the

three points due to the missing data for May and June 2013. 
Measured outdoor temperature by BMS sensors and from weather station 
Examination of Figure 55 showed that the calculated monthly average outdoor temperatures 
from BMS sensors showed lower values than were the obtained monthly average values from 
the nearest weather station (Viborg, Denmark) for the winter months 2013. For the spring, 
summer and autumn the values were pretty similar. In the summer (July and August 2013), the 
measured BMS temperatures were around 2 % higher than were the temperatures from the 
weather station. General observation of the results showed that BMS did not evidently 
overestimate the outdoor temperatures for the summer of 2013 due to the solar effect. It was 
discovered that the BMS sensors measuring outdoor temperature were positioned on the roof of 
Viborg Town Hall; however, they were not surrounded with the black surface or exposed to 
direct sun [63]. 
Furthermore, the deviation between the calculated monthly DD (HDD and CDD) values based on 
the measured BMS outdoor temperatures and monthly DD values obtained from the online free 
meteorological information source degreedays.net is presented in Appendix F. 
 
 
==PAGE:134==

5.3.3.2 IEQ performance indicators 
Thermal comfort indicators 
The evaluation of the thermal comfort indicators for the Method A (Table 19) and Method B 
(Figure 57) showed that Viborg Town Hall had a certain problem with the space overheating 
(temperatures exceeding 26 °C) during the year 2013.  
For the open plan offices on the 2nd and 4th floor, it was discovered that the annual percentage of 
occupied hours when the operative temperature was outside the specified range for Category II 
(20–26 °C) did not exceed the recommended value of 5 % (Annex G). The annual percentage 
value when the operative temperature was outside the specified range was found at 3.6 % for 
the open plan office on the 2nd floor and 3.0 % for the open plan office on the 4th floor. In both 
cases this represented a situation where was exceeded the upper temperature limit of Category 
II (26 °C). It was discovered that significant problem with overheating was for the mayor’s office 
(11.8 % of annual occupied hours). For the canteen, the recommended limit value of 5 % was 
slightly exceeded (5.6 % of the occupied time temperatures outside the range).  
Furthermore, the evaluation of monthly DH values for Method B (Figure 57) showed that the 
recommended 13 DH limit was exceeded for July and August 2013 for the two investigated open 
plan offices. Aggravated thermal comfort was observed also for the canteen and the mayor’s 
office (recommended limit of 13 DH exceeded in the summer 2013).  
IAQ indicators 
Figure 58 presented annual distribution of CO2 concentrations for 2013. As seen, poor IAQ was 
inspected for the northwest open plan office (L6) on the 3rd floor and for the mayor’s office (L8) 
on the 4th floor. It implied that natural ventilation of these rooms was not sufficient to satisfy the 
requirements of Category II. It was observed that for the open plan office on the 3rd floor (L6) 
the CO2 concentrations exceeded the recommended CO2 limit for Category II (850 ppm) for 27 
% of occupied hours.  
Annual CO2 concentrations were more than the 95 % of the occupied time below the upper CO2 
limit for Category II for the southwest open plan office on the 4th floor (L7) and the canteen on 
the 5th floor. The mechanically ventilated canteen (L9) had best IAQ in comparison with the 
other investigated spaces. 
5.3.3.3 Joint assessment of energy and IEQ performance indicators 
Energy consumption against thermal comfort 
Division of the energy consumption for space heating (Qh) and space cooling (Qc) allowed a 
joint analysis of energy consumption and thermal comfort for the heating and cooling season 
separately. Results for thermal comfort indicators showed that Viborg Town Hall was facing a 
problem with temperatures above 26 °C in the summer of 2013 and therefore figures for 
cooling season were of higher interest. 
==PAGE:135==
5 Part B: Validation of the developed key figures on data from existing buildings 
121 
 
Figure 59 and Figure 60 showed how much heating (Qh) was needed through the winter 
months of 2013 in order to maintain good thermal comfort. Figures showed how Qh increased 
from 3.5 kWh/m2 to 13.5 kWh/m2 from October to January 2013. As seen, there was no 
occurrence where the temperatures fell below the lower temperature limit for Category II (20 
°C) for the representative “mean” open plan office. 
Plots in Figure 61 and Figure 62 described the situation in the summer of 2013. Observation of 
Figure 61 showed that there was no mechanical cooling consumption spent for the 
representative “mean” open plan office where thermal comfort was aggravated for July (16.1 % 
of monthly occupied hours) and August (12.4 % of monthly occupied hours) 2013. For the open 
plan offices, mechanical cooling should be provided by the installed TAD and naturally by 
opening windows which were automatically controlled. Detailed energy consumption 
evaluation showed that cooling from TAD was not activated for the investigated period (year 
2013) due to an error in operation. The open plan offices were cooled solely by the natural 
ventilation system and energy consumption values are therefore zero for the representative 
“mean” open plan office in Figure 61. As seen, natural ventilation provided sufficient space 
cooling for most of the months of 2013 where the operative temperature was kept below the 
upper temperature limit of Category II (26 °C). For the representative “mean” open plan office 
the recommended limits for Method A (Figure 61) and Method B (Figure 62) were exceeded 
only for the months of July and August 2013.  
For the canteen, thermal comfort was still aggravated despite some active mechanical cooling in 
the summer of 2013 (consumption for July and August around 3.2 kWh/m2). The operative 
temperature exceeded the upper temperature limit of Category II (26 °C) for around 20 % of the 
monthly occupied time in July and August 2013. Furthermore, monthly DH exceeded the 
recommended 13 DH limit for these two summer months (20–25 DH).  
Analyses of the 3D plot in Figure 63 showed that for the representative “mean” open plan office 
no energy was spent on mechanical cooling and good thermal comfort was provided for all the 
months except for July and August 2013. For these two months the representative “mean” open 
plan office was categorized in the III. quadrant which implied aggravated thermal comfort and 
the need for detailed inspection. For the canteen, the plot showed that even though there was an 
increase in cooling energy consumption for the months of July and August (Qc around 3.2 
kWh/m2) in comparison with the spring and autumn months (Qc around 2 kWh/m2) this 
increase did not help maintain the temperatures below the desired upper temperature limit of 
26 °C. It was observed that both recommended limit values for Method A (5 %) and Method B 
(13 DH) were exceeded.  
Figure 64 and Figure 65 showed the building’s energy consumption and thermal comfort 
performance with changing outdoor temperature for the heating and cooling season of 2013. 
Figure 64 showed how the heating consumption decreased with increased monthly average 
outdoor temperature while providing a good thermal comfort for the representative “mean” 
open plan office through all the observed period. For none of occupied hours, the temperatures 
dropped below the minimum desired temperature of 20 °C. As observed, the installed heating 
system (TAD, radiators, mechanical ventilation system) was capable of providing good thermal 
comfort even for the coldest months.  
==PAGE:136==
5 Part B: Validation of the developed key figures on data from existing buildings 
122 
 
Observation of Figure 65 showed an increase in monthly percentage of occupied hours when 
the operative temperature exceeded the recommended upper limit of Category II (26 °C) for the 
representative “mean” open plan office and the canteen with the rising monthly average 
outdoor temperatures for July and August 2013. Despite of mechanical cooling of the canteen, 
higher values for the monthly percentage of occupied hours when the operative temperature 
exceeded 26 °C were observed for the canteen than compared to the “mean” open plan office. As 
stated in the building’s description, the ventilation system was activated by a temperature limit 
of 24 °C in order to cool the indoor air. The BMS sensors measuring indoor temperature were 
placed at the ceiling where HOBO loggers were placed at the height of a sitting person. A more 
detailed analysis of the indoor temperature datasets would be needed (including analysis of 
data obtained by the HOBO loggers) in order to find a reason for such deviations in recordings. 
Hansen [63] did a comparison between the room temperatures measured by BMS sensors and 
HOBO loggers. He discovered that for the canteen, the BMS sensors overestimated the room 
temperatures. 
Key figures presented in Figure 66 and Figure 67 were plotted to show the deviation in energy 
consumption and thermal comfort performance through the different years. Figure 66 revealed 
that the highest heating consumption (12–13 kWh/m2) was reached for the coldest months 
having monthly average outdoor temperatures of around 0 °C in January, February, March 2013 
and January 2016. This heating provided a good thermal comfort since there were no hours 
when the operative temperature dropped below 20 °C for the representative “mean” open plan 
office. Heating consumption was similarly affected by the outdoor conditions (increased Qh 
with lower Tout) where the operative temperature was all the occupied time maintained above 
the lower temperature limit of Category II (20 °C) for the observed period in 2013 and 2016. 
The observation of Figure 67 showed that even though the winter period of 2013 and 2016 was 
investigated (January–April), there were certain hours when the operative temperature 
exceeded the upper temperature limit of Category II (26 °C). This was identified for March 2013 
(monthly average Tout -0.5 °C) and March and April 2016 (monthly average Tout 4–6 °C). Still, 
the monthly percentage of occupied hours when the operative temperature exceeded 26 °C was 
lower than the 5 % (recommended limit of Annex G). Figure 67 showed that there was no 
mechanical cooling from TAD used in 2013 and 2016 and the representative “mean” open plan 
office was solely naturally ventilated during the observed periods in 2013 and 2016.  
Based on the 4-month period observation of Viborg Town Hall’s operation behaviour for two 
different years it was seen that the building operated in a similar way in the first quarter of the 
year 2013 as in the first quarter of the year 2016. However, energy performance and IEQ data 
should be investigated for the whole year (2014 and 2015) in order to do an accurate evaluation 
and establish a relevant conclusion about the building overall operation through the different 
years. As discovered, there was a problem with temperatures above 26 °C in the summer of 
2013 and TAD did not provide floor cooling for the open plan offices. The summer period of 
2014 or 2015 should be investigated if such data was available in order to see whether the 
inspected problem in 2013 was solved in the following years. It was difficult to do an 
assessment of the building’s operation performance over time based on analyses done for such 
short measured period (4-month winter period for 2013 and 2016).  
Measured monthly average outdoor temperature for Viborg Town Hall was presented in order 
to show how the energy and indoor climate performance depend on the outdoor conditions for 
==PAGE:137==
5 Part B: Validation of the developed key figures on data from existing buildings 
123 
 
different years. The energy consumption for 2016 was not normalized to a reference year 2013. 
In case of using the DD method, the CDD values would be equal to zero for the inspected winter 
period in 2013 and 2016. 
Energy consumption against IAQ 
Higher monthly average CO2 concentrations were observed from Figure 68 for the naturally 
ventilated “mean” open plan office than for the mechanically ventilated canteen for most of the 
months. Monthly average CO2 concentrations were lowest for the summer of 2013 (natural 
cooling activated) and highest for the winter 2013 (less often provided natural ventilation) for 
the representative “mean” open plan office. Figure 68 showed that monthly ventilation 
consumption (Qv) varied between 2.5–3.2 kWh/m2 where the average CO2 levels were kept 
below 500 ppm for the year 2013 for the canteen. As described, the mechanical ventilation 
system was activated when the CO2 concentrations exceeded 500 ppm in order to provide good 
IAQ.  
Figure 69 showed that having no mechanical ventilation cooling (zero consumption values) 
resulted in higher CO2 concentrations for the naturally ventilated “mean” open plan office. 
Measured CO2 concentrations were below the recommended CO2 limit of Category II (850 ppm) 
only for the summer months of June, July and August 2013. A great deviation in CO2 
concentrations was seen for the summer and winter. The window opening schedule was 
efficient in the summer of 2013 since providing good IAQ of the office spaces. However, the 
recommended CO2 limit of 850 ppm was exceeded for around 25 % of the occupied time for the 
winter months for the representative “mean” open plan office. Furthermore, the recommended 
CO2 exceedance limit value of 1300 ppm was exceeded for the winter months of 2013 and the 
monthly values were categorized in the III. quadrant (green coloured text) which implied poor 
IAQ. For the canteen, it was observed that the monthly ventilation consumption varied between 
2.5–3.2 kWh/m2 where CO2 concentrations did not exceed the recommended CO2 limits and 
were found in the I. quadrant. This showed that mechanical ventilation system provided good 
IAQ in the canteen.  
5.3.4 Practical usability of the developed key figures for continuous commissioning 
of a single building 
The joint assessment analyses of Viborg Town Hall were done in order to assess whether the 
developed key figures for the joint assessment of energy and IEQ performance indicators can be 
used as a tool in detailed building’s operation diagnostics. It was found that having more 
detailed building information and energy consumption division into subsystems can help during 
the analyses leading to more detailed findings. 
For Viborg Town Hall, it was observed that the installed heating system was capable of 
providing good thermal comfort for the whole year of 2013. It can be further investigated 
whether it is possible to optimize the schedule of the installed heating system by obtaining 
certain energy savings while still providing good thermal comfort. The evaluation of results for 
the representative “mean” open plan office showed that the natural ventilation application was 
not able to provide sufficient cooling of the office space in the summer of 2013 and for around 
15 % of the occupied time temperatures exceeded the desired maximum temperature of 26 °C 
==PAGE:138==
5 Part B: Validation of the developed key figures on data from existing buildings 
124 
 
(July and August 2013). Detailed inspection should be done in order to solve the problem with 
overheating and improve the indoor environment (for example: mechanical cooling by TAD, 
better shading of the existing glazing area, improved window opening control etc.). Despite the 
mechanical cooling of the canteen (monthly Qc consumption in July 3.2 kWh/m2), this room 
type was also facing the overheating problem in the summer of 2013 (20 % of the occupied 
time). As explained, BMS sensors placed in the canteen were most likely overestimating the 
actual room temperatures. It was discovered that the natural ventilation schedule was sufficient 
in providing good IAQ for the office spaces in the summer of 2013. However, improvements 
should be done to the window opening schedule for the other months of the year 2013 in order 
to provide better IAQ for the occupants all year around. Good IAQ was observed for the 
mechanically ventilated canteen for the whole year of 2013.  
Joint assessment of energy and IEQ performance done for a single building 
Several concerns about room-to-building level comparisons were exposed in chapter 4.3.4. This 
issue was also observed when doing a more detailed joint assessment analysis of energy and 
IEQ performance for a single building (Group B.2). As seen from the study of Viborg Town Hall, 
the building’s energy usage was presented as energy per unit area for different subsystems 
where datasets showing space heating, cooling and ventilation were analysed for the whole 
building.  
As discussed in chapter 4.3.4, representative mean IEQ performance indicators should be 
obtained from the measured IEQ data for a larger number of the most commonly occupied 
rooms. For Viborg Town Hall, the most commonly occupied room type was the open plan office. 
Installed BMS was positioned for the two open plan offices on the 2nd and the 4th floor. It was 
estimated that this area (“mean” – open plan office) covered 22 % of total heated floor area. It 
was assumed that such representative IEQ performance indicator values represent a typical IEQ 
situation in the building while covering a larger portion of the used space than when only 
presenting IEQ data of a single room. As discovered, by using averaging approach the 
information about rooms having best or worst IEQ is lost. Further investigation should be done 
in order to assess what the appropriate and accurate criterion is (mean, percentile ranking etc.) 
due to the room-to-building comparison issue when doing joint assessment analyses. 
Furthermore, for the evaluation of the monthly degree hours (Method B), the recommended 13 
DH limit value was used as developed in the Improvement method for Method A and B. As 
already discussed, having a constant DH limit simplifies the evaluation process. When using 
approach described in the Improvement method for Method A and B (page 26, Table 4) and 
actual occupied hours for Viborg Town Hall, the recommended DH limit value is 10.5 DH. 
Evaluation of results with a DH limit of 10.5 led to the same findings for the “mean” open plan 
office as an evaluation of results according to the constant DH limit of 13 DH (Figure 62). The 
only difference in the evaluations was for the canteen in April 2013. This monthly DH value 
exceeded the recommended limit of 10.5 but not 13 DH. As shown for Group B.1 in chapter 
“Constant DH limit value”, the obtained error due to the evaluation process where having a 
constant DH limit was relatively insignificant.  
The developed methodology for a joint assessment of energy performance and IEQ is mainly 
intended for buildings with an installed HVAC system. This was also the case of Viborg Town 
==PAGE:139==
5 Part B: Validation of the developed key figures on data from existing buildings 
125 
 
Hall, where cooling of the offices should be provided by TAD and natural ventilation application. 
As discovered, TAD did not provide cooling to the office spaces due to an error with the ground 
water system. Offices were solely naturally cooled and ventilated. It was important to 
distinguish whether the building’s energy was provided for the office rooms. As explained 
during the building’s description, the mechanical ventilation system was mostly used for the 
cooling of the canteen. It would be inaccurate to show the correlation between the mechanical 
cooling consumption and thermal comfort of the representative “mean” open plan office. It is 
important to distinguish rooms having mechanical, natural or hybrid ventilation when doing 
joint assessment analyses.  
Available BMS data and the logging interval 
Only room temperature data was available as BMS measured IEQ parameter for most of the 
investigated existing buildings (Group B.1 and B.2). For Viborg Town Hall, it was desired to 
analyse measured BMS CO2 concentrations, however as later discovered these readings were 
not available. Individually measured CO2 datasets recorded with HOBO loggers (coupled with 
Vaisala CO2 measurement devices) were used. This was possible due to many research projects 
done on the particular building for the year 2013. As discovered during the background study, 
in building management there is commonly no time, money or interest for such additional 
measurements if not explicitly requested or there is a reported suspicion of aggravated IEQ. 
Furthermore, the additional measured IEQ data (CO2 from HOBO loggers) can be measured for a 
different time period as are the measured energy BMS datasets. The objective of the developed 
key figures is to be applicable with the building’s installed BMS and their measured high-
frequency data. In such way, developed graphical plots can be automatically produced without 
any additional need for new IEQ measurements.  
During the thesis project, it was assessed whether the proposed key figures for joint assessment 
can be used for building’s internal benchmarking and continuous commissioning. The aim was 
to do a comparison of a building’s operation through different years; the operation performance 
for 2013 compared to the operation in 2014 or 2015. For Viborg Town Hall, it was discovered 
that it is possible to download hourly measured datasets for last 170 days in the history. Older 
datasets were transformed into eight hour averaged values. Due to the amount of data 
increasing with the building’s life cycle and limitations in data storage, such actions seem 
necessary. Using BMS data mostly for energy management (evaluation of energy consumption) 
[3] shows no necessity for storing hourly recorded data. Regarding the energy performance 
assessment, such recordings provide enough information in order to assess whether the 
building uses more or less energy over time. However, less frequent recordings of the IEQ 
related data over a longer time period make IEQ analyses difficult. Hourly recordings are 
needed in order to establish an accurate evaluation of IEQ according to the used methods 
(Method A, Method B and CO2 exceedance). With less frequent recordings, the needed detail of 
information is lost. Nevertheless, if a joint assessment analysis is implemented as part of 
continuous commissioning; hourly logged data should be available. This leads to a technical 
challenge that needs to be tackled since such large data sizes require sufficient space and high-
performance computing machines. This is also pinpointed by Fan et al. [42] where the authors 
emphasize that this issue needs to be solved before the building industry can benefit from large 
BMS datasets.  
==PAGE:140==
6 Conclusion and future work 
126 
 
6 Conclusion and future work 
6.1 Conclusion 
This project showed a new way of displaying two different data types (energy and IEQ related) 
on a single plot; key figure. The developed key figures differ from raw BMS data in that they are 
not directly available from the measured BMS parameters (energy (kWh), temperature (°C), CO2 
(ppm) etc.). Instead, they are obtained by combining performance indicators related to energy 
and IEQ performance (for example; energy per unit area in relation to degree hours). The 
presentation of these performance indicators on a single plot allows for a simultaneous 
assessment of the energy and IEQ performance for a single building or a pool of buildings. It is 
important to evaluate energy consumption in respect to IEQ and vice versa since only in this 
way can we create sustainable buildings that will operate in energy efficient way and at the 
same time provide a good indoor environment for the occupants. 
The developed key figures can give an insight into the building’s energy performance and IEQ 
behaviour over a representative period of time (heating, cooling season or yearly overview) 
while presenting monthly values calculated from the hourly recorded datasets. As shown, the 
developed key figures can be used to study trends for a large number of buildings equipped 
with a HVAC system if sufficient building information and datasets are available. The key figures 
can be presented on a simple level of building data management which allows identifying the 
outstanding energy consumption or tremendously aggravated indoor environment directly 
from a plot. It seems a useful tool for a continuous commissioning process since it can quickly 
alert the building manager when something is wrong with the building or its HVAC installations. 
This way, the building manager can decide when a detailed inspection is needed in order to find 
the source of the building’s poor operation. 
To emphasize, the use of advanced building technology should be not only to create more 
energy efficient buildings but also buildings that are healthier for its occupants. The aim of this 
thesis project was to discover whether it is possible to analyse the BMS data in a way that allow 
simultaneous evaluation of a building’s energy performance and indoor environment. The 
developed key figures proved to have potential for such evaluation, however, there is still a vast 
potential to improve the developed methodology and key figures for joint assessment of energy 
consumption and IEQ. It is believed that with further development and integration of the key 
figures into practice, the key figures could become a useful support tool for continuous 
commissioning and internal benchmarking. Furthermore, incorporating a joint assessment 
analysis approach in the national certification schemes could not only improve the building’s 
energy use but also indoor environment in the buildings. Over and above, as shown during the 
thesis project, there is a need for the development of software where the developed 
methodology for joint assessment of energy and IEQ performance indicators would be 
integrated as part of the building’s smart system.  
 
==PAGE:141==
6 Conclusion and future work 
127 
 
6.2 Future work and recommendations 
6.2.1 Key figures implementation for naturally ventilated buildings 
The developed key figures for joint assessment of energy consumption and IEQ were primarily 
intended for the analyses of buildings with an installed HVAC system. For new commercial 
buildings, installation of a HVAC system is a common design approach where energy sub-meters 
allow individual readings for heating, cooling and ventilation.  
The three methods described in Annex F, EN 15251 [38] are based on PMV thermal model. None 
of the investigated buildings in this project was solely naturally ventilated and therefore, the 
evaluation of thermal comfort was done using PMV thermal model described in EN 15251. 
However, there are several concerns when using a PMV model as discussed in Part A. One 
should consider using the adaptive approach introduced in standard ASHRAE 55 [51] when 
using it for naturally ventilated buildings. This approach is also presented in EN 15251 
standard, Annex A.2 “Acceptable indoor temperatures for design of buildings without 
mechanical cooling system” [38]. It is recommended when doing a joint assessment analysis of 
solely naturally cooled buildings where opening and closing of windows is possible by 
occupants. 
In general, the adaptive thermal approach allows a wider range for the acceptable operative 
temperatures due to the assumption that people can adapt to the indoor environment (opening 
windows, clothing, acclimatization etc.). The acceptable operative temperature boundary 
conditions are not defined as rigid limit values (as temperature limits based on PMV thermal 
model) but instead, are a function of the exponentially weighted running mean of the outdoor 
temperature. For the existing buildings, outdoor temperature data from the BMS or from the 
nearest weather station should be used. From the described adaptive thermal method in Annex 
A.2, EN 15251 [38], it can be seen that the acceptable temperature range depends on the 
outdoor running mean temperature where the upper limits are between 10 °C and 33 °C and 
between 15 °C and 30 °C for the lower limits.  
Integration of the adaptive model approach for the developed key figures can be done where the 
temperature boundary limits for the thermal comfort indicator evaluation (according to Method 
A and Method B) can be defined depending on the daily mean outdoor temperature for the 
previous day. The daily mean outdoor air temperature can be obtained by using Equation 11 
[38]. Regarding the chosen category of EN 15251, the upper and lower temperature limit of 
indoor operative temperature can be obtained using the equation in Table 27. 
                                                                                                                                     (Eq.11) 
where: 
    = Outdoor running mean temperature for today 
      = Daily mean external temperature for the previous day 
      = Running mean temperature for previous day 
  = A constant which specifies the speed at which the running mean responds to the outdoor 
temperature, recommend to be 0.8.

128

According to Annex A.2 EN 15251 [38], when outdoor running mean temperatures are below 10 
°C for the upper limit and below 15 °C for the lower limits (heating season), the design operative 
temperature limits for mechanically cooled office buildings (PMV model) should be applied. 
For the developed key figures, instead of having a constant upper limit for Category II (26 °C), 
this upper limit would need to be recalculated from day to day depending on the outdoor 
temperatures. The hypothesis is that an overheating problem would be identified as less 
frequent (lower results for Method A) and less severe (Method B) when applying the adaptive 
model for the evaluation of the thermal discomfort in summer. If assuming that outdoor running 
mean temperature is 20 °C this leads to an upper limit for Category II of 28.4 °C instead being at 
26 °C (as for Category II when using the PMV model). Consequently, the need for cooling would 
be decreased when having higher upper temperature limits as boundary values for thermal 
discomfort. Furthermore, ASHRAE 55 [58] defines as an acceptable thermal environment an 
environment in which at least 80 % of the occupants finds thermally acceptable.  
6.2.2 Recommended limit for Method B (Annex F, EN 15251) 
When doing an evaluation of thermal comfort according to Method B, having upper limit for the 
degree hours criterion seemed beneficial in order to establish whether thermal discomfort 
situation was severe or not. The DH limit value for the thesis project was calculated for certain 
number of monthly occupied hours. As shown, a more accurate evaluation would be obtained by 
recalculating the recommended limit for every building individually. When doing a comparison 
for a large number of buildings, such evaluation seems time consuming due to a different 
amount of occupied hours for each building. Furthermore, for the thesis project, acceptable 
violation of the boundary temperature limits for Category II was based on the limits of Category 
II of EN 15251 [38]. Further study can be done by conducting an extensive experimental 
research in order to evaluate whether such allowed violation criterion of the temperatures is 
accurate. Moreover, it would be interesting to observe whether is possible and suitable to 
establish a common DH limit value for the same building type; commercial buildings (having 
similar occupancy schedule).  
 
 
==PAGE:143==
6 Conclusion and future work 
129 
 
6.2.3 Further development of key figures and integration of the developed 
key figures as part of automated building control 
The developed key figures introduce a valuable tool used in the established joint assessment 
analysis approach where building’s energy and IEQ performance can be evaluated 
simultaneously. There can be done further development of the current key figures and its 
integration as part of the smart systems. Further studies can be done in order to improve the 
graphical visualization of the developed key figures. By using different background colours, 
violated performance requirements and recommendations can be indicated. Moreover, 
interactive 3D animation and possible rotation of the 3D figures could further improve the 
readability of developed 3D plots. There seem numerous possibilities of visualizing time series 
data and further exploration can be done in order to find new graphical features that would 
allow easy display of information showing joint assessment of energy and IEQ performance 
indicators. As discussed during the background study and was shown in the thesis project, the 
development of self-produced scripts is time-consuming and can therefore be inefficient when 
doing an overview of the building’s energy and IEQ performance.  
This issue needs to be primarily addressed if the aim is better usage of high-frequency BMS data 
in building management and decision making where both a building’s energy consumption and 
IEQ are addressed. More efficient building control can be obtained if the entire software and big 
data analyses are combined in one environment where data analyses are done automatically 
instead of manually. As a solution, an intelligent performance optimization package including 
graphical visualization of data by usage of the developed key figures can be implemented as part 
of the building smart system. In such way, the key figures inform the occupants of their energy 
usage and IEQ performance.  
It is believed that a simple graphical display of results on the developed key figures would help 
educate the building users to be proactive in their energy consumption and indoor 
environment. As described in the background study, it is estimated that 22 % of energy saving 
could be obtained by 2028 with more efficient BMS data usage [42]. Beside energy savings, it is 
believed that with more automated analyses of the BMS measured IEQ parameters indoor 
environment could be further improved.  
6.2.4 Using dynamic simulation models and key figures for continuous 
commissioning 
For the development of the new key figures, using dynamic energy simulations proved 
beneficial since it allowed for an easy and accurate interpretation of the correlation between 
different energy and IEQ performance indicators. Defining all necessary detailed climate 
characteristics and building information led to a later easier interpretation of the relationship 
between energy and IEQ parameters since all building information was known (weather, 
occupancy schedule, building characteristics and HVAC system schedule).  
As discussed during the literature review, simulation models can be used as a support during 
the commissioning process [7, 8, 16, 19-21]. The deviations between the predicted “ideal” 
design simulation operation and the actual building performance can indicate whether there is a 
need for detailed operation diagnostics [19]. This section includes a proposal and 
==PAGE:144==
6 Conclusion and future work 
130 
 
demonstration example on how using both the developed key figures and simulation models 
can lead to better commissioning and building operation. The measured BMS values obtained 
during the first years of building operation can be compared to the predicted designed value 
from dynamic simulation model in order to see whether the building achieved the designed 
building operation performance regarding energy efficiency and IEQ. 
Evaluation of energy consumption and IEQ can be done using the developed methodology for 
joint assessment of energy and IEQ performance indicators where measured BMS data is 
compared to the predicted simulation building design performance. The “single number” 
indicator presented in Figure 31 (page 66) can be further developed where instead of 
introducing a monthly recommended ratio (% per kWh/m2) based on current energy and IEQ 
performance requirements, predicted simulated values can be applied.  
A new key figure was developed and is presented in Figure 71 in order to analyse whether it can 
be used to show how the building and its systems perform in reality. It presents a 2D key figure 
which allows evaluation of a building during its life cycle where the building’s measured results 
can be compared to the building’s simulation results. This is expressed as a percentage of the 
achieved designed performance and reveals a building’s actual overall performance in respect 
to the predicted performance.  
The percentage of acceptable “aggravated” IEQ (x-axis) presents a monthly percentage obtained 
as the division between the actual measured and predicted (simulated) value of the IEQ 
performance indicator for the same time period (Equation 12).  
                                           
                                  
                                                      (Eq.12) 
The percentage of predicted “good” energy performance (y-axis) presents a percentage value 
where the measured energy consumption is compared to the predicted (simulated) energy 
consumption value obtained for the same month (Equation 13). 
                                                  
                           
                                        (Eq.13) 
In Figure 71, the building’s acceptable “aggravated” IEQ performance indicator and the 
predicted “good” energy performance are defined by the dashed lines showing a situation 
where the actual operation was as intended (100 % match). The dashed lines show whether the 
building’s actual monthly performance met the intended performance obtained for the same 
simulated month. The vertical dashed line presents a border limit for the IEQ performance 
indicator evaluation. If the monthly value is found left of the vertical dashed line then the actual 
IEQ for the observed month was better than the predicted one. If the monthly value is found 
right from the vertical dashed line then the actual IEQ was worse than the predicted acceptable 
“aggravated” IEQ. If the measured IEQ performance indicator value coincides with the simulated 
one, then the value will be found at the vertical dashed line indicating a 100 % match.  
The horizontal dashed line presents a border limit for the energy consumption evaluation. If the 
monthly value is found below the horizontal dashed line then the actual energy consumption for 
the observed month was lower than the predicted one. If the monthly value is found above the 
vertical dashed line then the actual energy consumption was higher than the predicted one. If 
==PAGE:145==
6 Conclusion and future work 
131 
 
the measured IEQ performance indicator value coincides with the simulated, then the value will 
be found at the vertical dashed line showing 100 % match.  
This way, the building’s operation can be evaluated. As a demonstration example, building A 
was assumed with an installed HVAC system and an advanced BMS measuring all the needed 
energy and IEQ related data. The evaluation of two months was done as an example presented 
in Figure 71. It should be noted that building A is only introduced as an example demonstrating 
the possible usability of the figure. To test the usability of the proposed method, a case study 
should be done for the existing building where simulation and measured values are observed.  
 
Figure 71: Developed evaluation method where percentage of acceptable “aggravated” IEQ is on x-axis and on 
y-axis percentage of predicted “good” energy performance operation (%) is presented. The dashed lines 
indicate a situation where building’s actual operation (energy consumption and IEQ) coincides with the 
simulated. The green and red stars show evaluations done for the demonstrated building A where the 
building’s operation was evaluated for two months. 
When doing the evaluation for the building example presented in Figure 71, it was seen that the 
building used less energy than predicted (percentage of predicted “good” energy performance 
operation lower than 100 %) in June (green star). Furthermore, this month the building also 
satisfied the designed (predicted) IEQ performance (percentage of acceptable “aggravated” IEQ 
lower than 100 %). It was assessed, that the building met the designed requirements since the 
monthly percentage values were lower than 100 %. Furthermore, building A more energy than 
intended spent in July (red star showing 150 % simulated energy consumption). Furthermore, 
the building had aggravated IEQ (150 % of acceptable value). It indicates poor “overall” building 
performance for this month. 
With further development of building smart systems, this can be integrated as a part of the 
installed BMS where the computation and graphical presentation of the data would be done 
automatically and the key figure presented on a screen would be the end product for the user 
(building manager). It can be integrated as part of advanced technology and warns the manager 
when the actual building’s energy and IEQ performance exceeds the recommended design 
(simulated) limits. A building manager can then decide whether a more detailed inspection of 
the building’s operation (systems, settings) and audit of HVAC system should be done in order 
==PAGE:146==
6 Conclusion and future work 
132 
 
to discover the source of aggravated building’s operation (related to energy consumption and 
IEQ). Solving the malfunction in this way would help calibrate the building’s operation to the 
intended “ideal” mode where both energy savings and better indoor environment would be 
obtained. Furthermore, certain acceptable violation limits can be developed (similar to the 
Annex G recommendation) in order to establish a tolerable, yet still acceptable, violation of the 
designed “ideal” building’s performance.  
Simulation models are also used when studying the influence of different control strategies of a 
HVAC system. As proposed by Claridge and Liu [16], a calibrated simulation model needs to be 
developed in order to model a current building’s performance situation. Then, by making 
changes to the system settings, different scenarios can be simulated. By graphically presenting 
these different simulated performance scenarios on the developed 2D key figures (showing 
energy consumption and IEQ), it can be assessed whether the implemented control change 
leads to an actual improvement of the overall building’s performance; energy efficiency and 
improved IEQ.  
There seem numerous possibilities on how building management and the building sector can 
benefit by using both the dynamic simulations and presenting the obtained results on the 
developed key figures. For example, it can be shown how different building thermal mass 
(comparing simulated scenarios for heavy and light weight construction), different building 
design solutions (building envelope, orientation, percentage of glazing area etc.), different 
occupancy schedule (number of occupied hours, number of occupants etc.) affect energy 
consumption and indoor environment. Over and above, by using the developed 3D plot it can be 
presented how significant the influence of different climate is on a chosen building design 
(energy consumption and IEQ). 
6.2.5 Research study and integration of the developed key figures for the 
joint assessment of energy and IEQ performance in benchmarking techniques 
The developed key figures showed a potential to analyse trends for a large number of buildings 
and to integrate them in current benchmarking techniques (comparing trends) where ranking 
can be done for a pool of buildings. A research study where for an extensive number of buildings 
a joint assessment of energy and IEQ performance indicators is done can show a practical 
usability of the developed indicators on a much broader scale.  
The concept of such research study is similar to the IEE Project TABULA (2009–2012) [67]. A 
classification scheme can be done where not only buildings are evaluated depending on their 
energy consumption (as in IEE Project TABULA) but also in respect to their indoor environment 
(Figure 71 or similar). It can be investigated, which parameters influence the relationship 
between energy consumption and provided indoor environment. For example influence of 
building type, age or type of utilized heating/cooling/ventilation system on the general ability of 
the building to provide comfortable indoor environment can be studied. Furthermore, such 
study can show whether for newer buildings with an advanced HVAC system this really leads to 
better thermal comfort and indoor air quality while decreasing energy consumption than in 
comparison with the older buildings having poor thermal insulation and a poorly utilized HVAC 
system. For different building types (residential, commercial buildings) a representative 
performance of the buildings in a specific country can be established.
